2201 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Dudley v. Corbett


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Onondaga County


46 Misc. 2d 205; 259 N.Y.S.2d 572; 1965 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1938



May 7, 1965 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the writ is now dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court issued a show cause order to determine why relator inmate, who filed a writ of habeas corpus, was under detention by respondent sheriff. The inmate was committed pursuant to a bench warrant after he was charged by an indictment for murder in the first degree.


OVERVIEW: The inmate alleged that custody of his body was illegally obtained because Va. Code Ann. § 53-303, under which his custody was obtained, was unconstitutional. He claimed that he had asylum in the State of Virginia and that his delivery to the custody of law-enforcement officials of New York State based on a certificate, but without the conduct of extradition proceedings or his waiver of them, was a violation of his rights under U.S. Const. art. IV, § 2(2), and 18 U.S.C.S. § 3182. The court disagreed and found that custody was lawfully obtained. All of the statutory requirements under Va. Code Ann. § 53-303 were met, and the statute was not declared to be unconstitutional. Thus, there was jurisdiction over the person of the inmate for the purpose of proceeding with a trial. The inmate's removal from the State of Virginia without extradition papers and without his waiver of extradition did not violate any of his constitutional rights or any rights under 18 U.S.C.S. § 3182.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the request for a writ of habeas corpus.


HEADNOTES

Extradition -- conformance to law of foreign State -- relator's removal from Virginia to New York without extradition papers or waiver of extradition did not violate his constitutional rights; writ of habeas corpus dismissed -- Virginia statute under which custody of relator was obtained is entitled to full faith and credit -- extradition from one State to another need not conform to Federal statute providing therefor; Virginia's delivery of custody of relator under its statute did not violate Federal statute -- relator cannot question court's jurisdiction over his person regardless of how his presence in Onondaga County was obtained.

1. Relator's removal from the State of Virginia without extradition papers and without his waiver of extradition did not violate any of his constitutional rights and his writ of habeas corpus is dismissed.

2. The Virginia statute under which custody of relator was obtained after full compliance with the procedures of that statute by the District Attorney of Onondaga County is entitled to full faith and credit under the United States Constitution (art. IV, § 1).

3. Section 3182 of the Federal Criminal [***2] Code (U. S. Code, tit. 18, § 3182) provides for extradition. Extradition from one State to another need not be in accordance with the provisions of section 3182 which are not exclusive. A State may enact legislation to supplement the Federal statute by permitting extradition in less exacting terms than those imposed by said statute. Accordingly, the delivery of relator by the State of Virginia to the custody of respondent in the State of New York pursuant to the Virginia statute did not violate section 3182.

4. Regardless of how the personal presence of relator was obtained in Onondaga County, the jurisdiction over his person for the purpose of proceeding with this trial cannot now be questioned.

 

2202 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Empire State Bldg. Corp. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


46 Misc. 2d 461; 259 N.Y.S.2d 939; 1965 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1943



May 7, 1965 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Plaintiff's motion for summary judgment is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff company moved for summary judgment against defendants, a city and others, in its suit seeking to declare New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code §§ I46-4.0, I46-6.0 illegal and unconstitutional insofar as they authorized the imposition of a real property transfer tax on the company as the grantee of the federal government.


OVERVIEW: The company had purchased property located in the city from the federal government, and the city sought to tax the property under the statutes in question. The city argued that the exemption of the federal government from taxation did not relieve the company, as the grantee of the federal government, from liability for the tax. The court found that: (1) under the Enabling Act, 1934 N.Y. Laws ch. 873, a transaction by or with the federal government was exempt from taxation; (2) the net effect of the Enabling Act's language was to render the section somewhat ambiguous, requiring construction against the taxing authority; and (3) the city code provision assessing the transfer tax on the grantee in a conveyance from the federal government was in contravention of the state's tax enabling legislation.


OUTCOME: The court granted the company's motion for summary judgment and declared that the city's code provision was illegal.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- transfer tax -- section I46-6.0 of Administrative Code of City of New York which requires grantee from United States to pay transfer tax violates State Enabling Act which exempts "transaction by or with" United States.

An act enabling the City of New York to impose transfer taxes, which exempts a "transaction by or with" the United States from taxation is violated by section I46-6.0 of the Administrative Code which exempts the United States of America from a tax but provides that the grantee from the United States of America shall be liable for the tax and the filing of a return. Plaintiff grantee from the United States is entitled to judgment declaring section I46-6.0 illegal and unconstitutional.

 

2203 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Bedingfield v. Dairymaid Farms, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Suffolk County


46 Misc. 2d 146; 259 N.Y.S.2d 292; 1965 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2025



April 21, 1965 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff filed a motion for reconsideration of an order of the court, which had held that plaintiffs' note of issue was premature.


OVERVIEW: At the time that plaintiffs filed their note of issue, no statement of readiness had been filed. The court held that he note of issue was premature. Contending that the note of issue contained a statement claiming a preference as permitted by N.Y. App. Div. 2nd Dep't Special R. I(6), pt. 5, plaintiffs filed a motion for reconsideration. The court granted the motion for reconsideration and adhered to its original order. Finding that ordinarily, a note of issue might not be filed without a statement of readiness, the court held that the exceptions provided for by N.Y. App. Div. 2nd Dep't Special R. VI, pt. 7 and N.Y. App. Div. 2nd Dep't Special R. VIII, pt. 7 did not apply to the case before the court. The court held that the advantage provided by N.Y. App. Div. 2nd Dep't Special R. VIII, pt. 7 would only have been available if there had been compliance with N.Y. App. Div. 2nd Dep't Special R. VIII, pt. 6, which the court found that there had not been.


OUTCOME: The court granted plaintiffs' motion for reconsideration and on reconsideration adhered to its original decision that plaintiffs' note of issue was premature.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Trial -- statement of readiness -- filing of note of issue without statement of readiness is premature; claim of preference in note of issue without "application" for preference does not permit elimination of statement of readiness.

The filing of a note of issue without a statement of readiness is premature despite the fact that plaintiff claimed a preference in the note of issue. Rule VIII of part 7 of the Rules of the Appellate Division, Second Department, permits an action to be placed on the calendar without a statement of readiness only if an "application" for a preference is simultaneously made. The conclusion of prematurity is not in conflict with CPLR 3402 which allows the filing of a note of issue 40 days after service of the summons is complete. CPLR 3401 authorizes the adoption of rules regulating the filing of notes of issue, and is consistent with the inherent power of the court to control its business. Calendar control is exercised through rules which condition the filing of notes of issue on the performance by the parties of specified acts.

 

2204 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


L. F. Letts & Son, Inc. v. Cortland


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Cortland County


47 Misc. 2d 240; 262 N.Y.S.2d 294; 1965 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2033



April 20, 1965 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, property owners and a lessee, brought a proceeding under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 against respondents, a city and others, seeking a judgment that Cortland, N.Y. Mun. Code 66-17 was unconstitutional or otherwise inapplicable to their property. They also sought an order directing that the city issue permits for the construction of a new filling station to replace the existing one.


OVERVIEW: The property owners had improved their premises with a gasoline service station that had been in continuous operation for many years. The filling station was later leased to the lessee. Petitioners filed an application for a reconstruction permit to construct a new station to replace the existing one, but the city denied the application, alleging that it was precluded by § 66-17, which had been enacted after the erection of the station. Petitioners brought an Article 78 proceeding for a declaration that the ordinance was unconstitutional or did not apply to their property and for an order to issue them the permits. It sought a permanent injunction against enforcement of § 66-17 against them. The court granted the injunction and directed the city to issue the permits. The ordinance required proposed amendments to be submitted to the Board of Appeals for prior report and recommendation, and that requirement was a condition precedent to action by the city's Common Council. The Council failed to conform with the requirements of its own legislation, so § 66-17 was void.


OUTCOME: The court granted the permanent injunction and directed the city to issue permits to petitioners.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- zoning -- section of Municipal Code of City of Cortland, enacted by Common Council and intended to replace existing regulations of zoning ordinance governing filling stations is invalid for failure of Council to comply with requirement that proposed amendments be submitted to Board of Appeals for prior report and recommendation -- owner and lessee of filling station entitled to permits for its removal and replacement as matter of right, as prior to attempted enactment of section.

1. A section of the Municipal Code of the City of Cortland, enacted by the Common Council of said city and intended, in conjunction with an amended section, to replace existing regulations of the zoning ordinance governing automobile filling stations is invalid because of the failure of the Council to comply with another section which required that proposed amendments be submitted by the Council to the Board of Appeals for the latter to make a prior report and recommendation thereon. This requirement is not a mere procedural technicality but is a condition precedent to action by the Council which, under the provisions of the section, may only override an adverse recommendation [***2] of the board by a two-thirds vote.

2. Prior to the attempted enactment of the section herein determined to be invalid, the owner and lessee of a filling station would have been entitled, as a matter of right, to permits for its removal and replacement with a new station. With the invalidation of the section, under which they would have been required to obtain special permission from the Council for the proposed removal and reconstruction, they are still entitled to these permits as a matter of right.

 

2205 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Kearney


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Erie County


45 Misc. 2d 1041; 258 N.Y.S.2d 769; 1965 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2038



April 20, 1965 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought an order vacating a 1954 sentence against him as a third felony offender and directing a resentence. He alleged that a 1932 predicate judgment of conviction from Canada was invalid because he had not been represented by counsel and did not intelligently waive his right to counsel. The issue was whether the same remedy was available to attack a predicate judgment of conviction from a foreign country as one from another state.


OVERVIEW: In 1964, the N.Y. Penal Law § 1943 was amended to provide for collateral attack of a predicate judgment of conviction from other states. This provided a remedy for defendants sentenced as multiple offenders where a prior conviction was shown to have been unconstitutionally obtained in another state. Defendant argued that this amendment should also apply to convictions from other countries and sought resentencing from a 1954 sentence which was based in part on a 1932 Canadian conviction. The court granted defendant the relief sought. A restrictive interpretation would have caused a denial of equal protection to certain defendants. The remedy of resentence found to exist with respect to prior sister state convictions also existed where the predicate judgment was alleged to have been obtained in the courts of a foreign country in violation of the rights of the person accused under the applicable constitutional provisions. Defendant was awarded a hearing to make his case that the Canadian conviction was obtained in violation of his constitutional rights, specifically the right to counsel.


OUTCOME: The court held that the remedy of resentence found to exist with respect to prior sister state convictions also existed relative to convictions obtained in the courts of a foreign country, allegedly in violation of defendant's constitutional rights. Defendant was ordered produced for hearing upon the allegations of his petition as to lack of counsel.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- multiple offender punishment -- with respect to prior convictions in courts of foreign country, used as basis for multiple offender punishment, if defendant alleges that conviction had been obtained in violation of his constitutional rights, remedy of resentence under section 1943 of Penal Law, as amended in 1964, is available -- hearing on petition is ordered.

1. The remedy of resentence under section 1943 of the Penal Law (as amd. by L. 1964, ch. 446), dealing with increased punishment for multiple felony offenders, existing with respect to prior sister State convictions, exists also with respect to predicate judgments alleged to have been obtained in the courts of a foreign country "in violation of the rights of the person accused under the applicable provisions of the constitution of the United States" (Penal Law, § 1943).

2. An issue of fact exists as to whether defendant intelligently and deliberately waived his right to counsel upon arraignment and trial by a Police Magistrate in Ontario, Canada. Defendant should be produced before the court for a hearing upon the petition in support of his motion for resentence.

 

2206 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Seaman v. Fedourich


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Broome County


45 Misc. 2d 940; 258 N.Y.S.2d 152; 1965 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2060



April 13, 1965 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff citizens initiated a case against defendants, members of a city council, the mayor, and a judge, seeking to have the present representation and voting procedure of the city council declared a violation of the equal protections provisions of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 11. Defendants filed a motion to dismiss the complaint. Plaintiffs requested that the motion to dismiss be treated as a motion for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs sought to have the representation and voting procedure of the city council declared a violation of the equal protection clauses of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 11. Plaintiffs also sought to enjoin defendants from acting in their official capacities until a legal and constitutional council was created. Defendants sought the dismissal of the complaint on the ground that the court did not have jurisdiction over the subject matter of the case. The court determined that it had jurisdiction over the subject matter because N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 1 gave it original jurisdiction in cases concerning State constitution violations and in civil right cases concerning Federal constitution violations based on voting procedures. The court held that plaintiffs were deprived of their right to equal representation because the scheme of apportionment was unequal due to the fact that 7 councilmen represented less than 27 percent of the city population. The court granted summary judgment in favor of plaintiffs because there was no question of fact concerning violations of their rights to equal representation under both the State and Federal constitutions.


OUTCOME: The court held that plaintiffs were entitled to summary judgment and declared that the apportionment of city council members violated the equal protection provisions of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 11.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- apportionment of Common Council of city -- court has jurisdiction of action challenging present apportionment of Common Council of City of Binghamton; plaintiffs are aggrieved by denial of civil right to equal representation -- "one person, one vote" principle applicable to Common Council -- present apportionment is unconstitutional.

1. Plaintiffs, citizens and qualified voters of the City of Binghamton, have, according to the allegations of the complaint, been denied by the present apportionment their civil right to equal representation on the Common Council of the City of Binghamton guaranteed by the Federal and State Constitutions and each is specifically affected thereby. Accordingly, the court has jurisdiction of this action in equity whether it be regarded as a plenary action to test whether the New York Constitution is violated or a civil right action to test whether the Federal Constitution is violated by the present representation and voting procedure of the Common Council of Binghamton.

2. The "one person, one vote" principle is applicable to the apportionment of elected members of legislative bodies of governmental units below the level of [***2] State Legislatures.

3. Where under the present apportionment 7 Councilmen represent less than 27% of the population and can pass legislation not favored by 6 representing more than 70% of the population, the apportionment deprives each plaintiff of his right to equal representation in violation of the Fourteenth Amendment of the Federal Constitution and section 11 of article I of the Constitution of New York. Plaintiffs are granted summary judgment to that effect.

 

2207 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Walsh v. New York State Liquor Auth.


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, SPECIAL TERM, SUFFOLK COUNTY


45 Misc. 2d 827; 257 N.Y.S.2d 971; 1965 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2067



April 9, 1965 - Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff applicants filed a motion for reargument of a prior decision that had dismissed their complaint against defendant liquor authority for failure to state a cause of action. In that complaint the applicants sought a declaratory judgment and injunction based upon an attack on the legality of a bulletin and rule of the liquor authority that stated a general policy to grant all applications for liquor licenses.


OVERVIEW: The applicants' complaint had previously been dismissed because factual allegations in the complaint had not actually occurred. However, in support of their motion for reargument the applicants supplied a copy of a telegram issued by the liquor authority which then established that the liquor authority was acting upon its general policy to grant all liquor licenses. The court held that the telegram now established the happening of applicants' factual allegations in their complaint. Consequently, the court determined there was a valid basis for reconsideration of the earlier dismissal, and granted the applicants' motion to the extent of vacating the earlier dismissal for failure to state a cause of action. However, the court refused to grant the requested relief in the applicants' motion, a preliminary injunction, because only reconsideration of the motion to dismiss was before it, and not the merits of the underlying action. Furthermore, the issues needed more development in order for the court to rule on an injunction.


OUTCOME: The court granted the applicants' motion to reargue the prior dismissal of their complaint that attacked the legality of the liquor authority's policy to grant all liquor license applications. Therefore, the court vacated its prior decision that dismissed the complaint. However, the court denied the applicants' motion to the extent that it sought a ruling on the merits of the case, and the requested relief of an injunction.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Intoxicating liquors - location of retail package liquor stores - motion for reargument may be accepted as one for renewal - hypothetical pleading may become factual after events have occurred - action is now maintainable against State Liquor Authority to declare its bulletin and rule 17 governing applications and locations of retail package liquor stores unconstitutional and invalid - but no preliminary injunction or temporary restraining order will be issued.

1. A motion for reargument, made on unacceptable grounds, may be accepted as one rightly made for renewal and reconsideration on the ground that the material facts have changed.

2. A pleading which is mainly hypothetical in its allegations and mainly future imperfect in tense may, after the pleaded events have actually occurred, be read as factual and as in the present tense.

3. Despite the review provisions of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law ( § 121), an action may be maintained for a declaratory judgment against the State Liquor Authority in a proper case, such as this action to declare unconstitutional and invalid the State Liquor Authority's Bulletin No. 390 and its amended rule 17 (9 NYCRR 42.1 et seq.)  [***2] governing applications for licenses and locations of retail package liquor stores.

4. Accordingly, plaintiffs' application for renewal and reconsideration of the court's decision granting defendant's motion to dismiss the complaint is granted, and said decision is in part vacated, and the motion for dismissal is denied. Of course, howeve, in this posture of the case, summary judgment should not be granted ( CPLR 3211, subd. [c]).

5. Even if plaintiff has met the minimum requirements for a preliminary injunction or a temporary restraining order ( CPLR 6301), the court in its discretion will decline to grant such drastic and immediate remedy in this case.

 

2208 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


New York C. R. Co. v. Lefkowitz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


46 Misc. 2d 68; 259 N.Y.S.2d 76; 1965 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2069; 59 L.R.R.M. 2095



April 8, 1965 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff railroads, all engaged in the business of transportation in interstate commerce in the state, brought an action for a judgment declaring unconstitutional N.Y. R.R. Law §§ 54-a, 54-b, 54-c, the "Railroad Full Crew Laws." The railroads also sought to enjoin defendant public officers from enforcing the laws at issue. Intervenors were defendant labor organizations representing railroad employees.


OVERVIEW: The railroads sought a judgment declaring unconstitutional N.Y. R.R. Law §§ 54-a, 54-b, 54-c, the "Railroad Full Crew Laws." The railroads also sought to enjoin the public officers from enforcing those laws. Labor organizations representing the railroad employees intervened. The court held that legislation designed to provide public safety carried with it a strong presumption of validity when challenged in the courts as well as a rebuttable presumption of the existence of the necessary factual support for its provisions. The court found that the railroads failed to show that the laws were unconstitutional beyond a reasonable doubt. They failed to establish that the laws deprived them of their property without due process of law when enacted or that the dangers had lessened so that the minimum crew requirements became clearly unreasonable and arbitrary. The court ruled that the statutes did not contravene the Equal Protection Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment or of N.Y. Const. art. I, § 11 because the legislature had the power and discretion to classify in the adoption of police laws. The court directed that judgment be entered dismissing the railroads' complaint.


OUTCOME: The court held that the legislation challenged by the railroads was constitutional. Accordingly, the court ruled that the public officers charged with enforcing the laws at issue and the labor organizations representing railroad employees were entitled to judgment dismissing the railroads' complaint.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- Railroad Full Crew Laws -- Full Crew Laws (Railroad Law, §§ 54-a, 54-b, 54-c) are valid and constitutional exercise of police power for safety -- technological progress has changed "firemen" of steam locomotives into "helpers" of diesel engines, and has increased speed and length of freight trains, and has not substantially reduced safety functions of firemen, brakemen, conductors and engineers -- railroads have failed to prove that cost of compliance is unduly burdensome -- Full Crew Laws do not deprive railroads of property or equal protection or unduly burden interstate commerce -- Railway Labor Act (U.S. Code, tit. 45, § 151 et seq.) and Public Law 88-108 of 1963 (77 U.S. Stat. 132) and Arbitration Board's award made thereunder, have not pre-empted field of railroad safety regulation and rendered our Full Crew Laws repugnant to Supremacy clause of United States Constitution (art. VI, cl. 2).

1. The so-called Full Crew Laws (Railroad Law, §§ 54-a, 54-b, 54-c) were enacted in 1913, 1936 and 1937 under the police power of the State to promote safety in railroad operations. They still validly and constitutionally prohibit (cf. U.S. Const., art. I, § [***2] 8, cl. 3; art. VI, cl. 2; 14th Amdt.; N. Y. Const., art. I, §§ 6, 11) the operation in this State of any freight train of more than 25 cars unless manned by a crew of not less than one engineer, one fireman (this job is now often called helper), one conductor, and three brakemen; and of any train other than a freight train of 5 cars or more, unless manned by a crew of not less than one engineer, one fireman, one conductor, and two brakemen, and additionally a baggageman if it is a baggage train or passenger train carrying baggage; and of any freight train of 25 cars or less unless manned by a crew of not less than one engineer, one fireman, one conductor, and two brakemen; and of any light engine without cars unless it is manned by one engineer, one fireman, and one conductor or brakeman (Railroad Law, § 54-a, enacted in 1913); and of any fuel-electric engine unless it is manned by a crew of not less than one engineer and one fireman or helper (Railroad Law, § 54-b, enacted in 1936); and of any locomotive or motor or self-propelled unit to switch or transfer cars from one railroad or railroad yard to another, unless manned by a crew of not less than one engineer, one fireman or helper,  [***3] one conductor or foreman, and two trainmen or two helpers (Railroad Law, § 54-c, enacted in 1937). This action by a number of railroads which are engaged in interstate commerce in New York State, seeking a judgment to declare said laws invalid and unconstitutional and to enjoin the New York State Attorney-General and the New York State Public Service Commission and a District Attorney from enforcing said laws, is dismissed after trial, and judgment is granted to said defendants.

2. Technological progress since the enactment of the Full Crew Laws has lightened the work of the train crews and has eliminated many of the dangers which existed at that time; but that same progress has rendered engines and trains more speedy and more complicated than ever. The 10 railroad plaintiffs in this case have not succeeded in proving that the dangers of operation which the Full Crew Laws sought to remedy have now lessened so much that those laws have become unreasonable, arbitrary, burdensome, and a confiscation or deprivation of the railroads' properties without due process of law.

3. Modern diesel-electric and electric locomotives have altered "firemen" into "helpers" or "firemen-helpers";  [***4] but a modern freight train is often over a mile long, and the evidence establishes that the number of firemen-helpers and brakemen which was required by the Full Crew Laws is still required in order to perform substantial safety functions. If the reasonableness of a statute is fairly debatable, the courts are precluded from overturning it.

4. Greater flexibility in regulating train crew requirements could be attained if the subject matter were delegated to the Public Service Commission or some other regulatory agency; but that is for the Legislature to decide. The Legislature chose to enact uniform requirements for all the railroads; and the railroads have not proved that that choice has been rendered arbitrary or capricious by modern circumstances. Indeed, the railroads have failed to prove that their present-day costs of complying with those safety statutes have increased disproportionately over what those costs were when those statutes were enacted.

5. Sections 54-a and 54-c of the Railroad Law apply to "any railroad of more than fifty miles in length within this state", while section 54-b applies to "any railroad within this state"; but it cannot be said that the legislative [***5] police power has unreasonably singled out the railroads for safety regulation or has so unreasonably classified the railroads as to deny their constitutional right to the equal protection of the laws.

6. Our Full Crew Laws do not constitute a direct interference with or an undue burden upon interstate or foreign commerce; rather, they are designed to aid and protect those engaged in such commerce.

7. Congress has not, by and under Public Law 88-108 of 1963 (77 U.S. Stat. 132), pre-empted the field of railroad safety regulation to such an extent as to render the full crew laws of the many States which have such laws repugnant to the "Supremacy" clause of the United States Constitution (art. VI, cl. 2). Perhaps, as to other working conditions, the Railway Labor Act (U. S. Code, tit. 45, § 151 et seq.) has taken the field and has provided for regulation by collective bargaining agreements. But the field has not been pre-empted as to State laws relating to minimum requirements of health or safety, even when applied to employees whose collective bargaining agreements are imbued with the force of Federal law by virtue of the Railway Labor Act. Congress has the power to preclude [***6] State regulation; but it has not done so.

8. United States Public Law 88-108 of 1963 (77 U.S. Stat. 132) was a joint resolution of Congress, approved by the President, and enacted in an emergency "To provide for the settlement of the labor dispute between certain carriers by railroad and certain of their employees." It prohibited a then pending nationwide strike or lockout over pay and working conditions, and established an Arbitration Board under the Railway Labor Act, and empowered the board to make a binding award in the then pending disputes as to "Use of Firemen (Helpers) on Other Than Steam Power" and as to "Consist of Road and Yard Crews" and as to "Minimum Safe Crew Consist", such award to continue in force for not more than two years, and all other issues to be decided by collective bargaining. The board made such an award. Pursuant to that award, the railroads are now operating locomotives without firemen in those States which have no full crew laws; and, pursuant to that award, special boards of adjustment have been appointed in some of those States and also in some of the States which do have full crew laws; and some of those boards have approved changes in work rules [***7] so as to permit smaller crews. The Congressional joint resolution and committee reports and the award and reports of the Arbitration Board indicate that there was no intent to supersede State full crew laws, but that, on the contrary, it was intended that the disputes as to crew requirements be settled by collective bargaining agreements. It is quite feasible for the railroads to comply both with New York State's Full Crew Laws and with the permissive provisions of the Federal Arbitration Board's award. In this situation, it cannot be said that Congress, under the "Supremacy" clause (U. S. Const., art. VI, cl. 2), has invalidated our Full Crew Laws.

 

2209 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Joseph E. Seagram & Sons, Inc. v. Hostetter


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


45 Misc. 2d 956; 258 N.Y.S.2d 442; 1965 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2079



April 8, 1965 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Plaintiffs' application for a preliminary injunction is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff liquor sellers filed a motion for a preliminary injunction to enjoin defendant state liquor authority from enforcing the New York Alcoholic Beverage Control Law (Law). The liquor authority filed a cross motion for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: After amendments to the Law required them to file a schedule of sale prices and the net bottle and case price paid, the liquor sellers applied for a preliminary injunction against the state liquor authority from enforcing the Law. The liquor authority filed a motion for an order dismissing the complaint or for summary judgment. The court denied the liquor sellers' application for a preliminary injunction and directed judgment in favor of the liquor authority, finding that the Law, the purpose of which was to regulate the intrastate sale and distribution of alcoholic beverages, was constitutional because it did not interfere with interstate commerce. In addition, the Law was reasonably adapted to determine whether unjustifiable prices were being charged to a state consumer and whether price competition was prevailing in the industry. It was also not unconstitutional for the legislature to delegate power to the liquor authority to carry out the Law.


OUTCOME: The court denied the liquor sellers' application for a preliminary injunction enjoining the liquor authority from enforcing the law and instead directed judgment for the liquor authority.


HEADNOTES

Intoxicating liquors -- price competition -- sections 7 and 9 of chapter 531 of Laws of 1964 amending Alcoholic Beverage Control Law to provide for price competition in sales of liquor are constitutional -- economic hardships resulting from enactment do not affect constitutionality -- requirement that price schedules be filed is not unreasonable -- any vagueness in terms can be cured by rules of State Liquor Authority -- statute not invalid as inconsistent with purpose to promote temperance -- statute does not contravene interstate commerce clause -- classification in statute is reasonable -- requirement of schedules irrespective of place of sale does not interfere with commerce -- requiring bottle and case price is given effect.

1. Sections 7 and 9 of chapter 531 of the Laws of 1964 amended the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law to provide for the filing, by brand owners, distillers or manufacturers of alcoholic beverages, monthly, schedules of prices of cases and bottles and discounts to retailers and an affirmation by the brand owner that the price to wholesalers in the schedule is no higher than the lowest [***2] price at which such item was sold to any wholesaler anywhere in the United States. The purpose was to foster price competition in the manufacture, sale and distribution of liquor and to result in lower prices to New York consumers. In this action for judgment declaring such sections unconstitutional, defendants are granted summary judgment declaring such sections valid and constitutional.

2. The strongest presumption of validity attaches to chapter 531 of the Laws of 1964 because it is an enactment under the police power, and the fact that it causes or might cause an economic hardship upon those whom it affects is no argument against its constitutionality. That economic hardship, possible reduced profit margins, difficulty in increasing prices and in competing in other States result or that expenditures for new equipment must be made does not bear on the constitutionality of the statute.

3. The provisions of chapter 531 which require the filing of price schedules and affirmations are neither arbitrary nor unreasonable. That plaintiffs might find it difficult to obtain the required information or are limited by the act in the price differentials allowed does not make the act arbitrary [***3] or unreasonable.

4. The claimed vagueness of terms such as "related person" and "inducements of any kind whatever" contained in the amendments to the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law can be cured under the power given to defendants to make rules. This delegation of power relates to the execution of the law and is valid.

5. The statute is not invalid on the ground that it is inconsistent with the declared policy of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law to promote temperance or that it will not serve to cure the monopolistic and anticompetitive practices at which it is directed.

6. The regulation and control of the sale and distribution of alcoholic beverages is a matter of local concern and the mere fact that such regulations have or may have some repercussions upon the activities of a business which operates Nationwide does not invalidate the State action, particularly where the subject of the action is within the police power of the State. Since the purpose of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law is to regulate and control the intrastate sale and distribution of alcoholic beverages, it does not contravene the commerce clause of the Federal Constitution or the Sherman Antitrust Act or [***4] the Robinson-Patman Antidiscrimination Act.

7. The allegation in the complaint that section 9 violates the Constitution by discriminatingly imposing maximum price limitations upon sales made by persons dealing in liquor sold under "private labels" and sales made by vintners and wholesalers of wine is rejected. A different classification for brand owners, private brands and vintners was found by the Legislature to exist. The section is valid.

8. The statute, by requiring schedules for sales irrespective of the place of sale or delivery does not thereby interfere with interstate commerce, because the purpose of the statute is to regulate "within the state" ( Alcoholic Beverage Control Law, § 101-b, subd. 1) and because sales outside the State can be made with the permission of defendants (§ 101-b, subd. 3, par. [a]). The requirement that a licensee obtain prior permission is not such a burden on interstate commerce as to make it invalid.

9. Requiring "net bottle and case price paid by the seller" has value in determining whether the fundamental principles of price competition prevail in the industry and in determining whether unjustifiable prices are being charged to consumers [***5] in the State and must be given effect.

 

2210 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Goldstein v. Rockefeller


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


45 Misc. 2d 778; 257 N.Y.S.2d 994; 1965 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2093



April 6, 1965 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a special proceeding under N.Y. Const. art. III, § 5 and 1911 N.Y. Laws 773, plaintiffs, who were local citizens and voters, sued certain state and county defendants and various town councils, seeking a declaration that the apportionment of Monroe County's Board of Supervisors and N.Y. County Law § 150 were unconstitutional. They also sought an order of reapportionment and filed a motion for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The suit was based on a claim that the challenged apportionment did not comply with the so-called "one person, one vote" principle and violated equal protection. The court was also asked to declare N.Y. County Law § 150 unconstitutional. Plaintiffs and the Board questioned the constitutional validity of the split referendum provisions of the Municipal Home Rule Law. The court found that N.Y. Const. art. II, § 5 and the 1911 N.Y. Laws 773 did not apply. But it concluded that the apportionment scheme of the Board was unconstitutional in that it violated both the U.S. Const. amend. XIV and the N.Y. Const. art. I, §§ 1, 11. The "one person, one vote" principle applied. N.Y. County Law § 150 was held unconstitutional insofar as it applied to the challenged apportionment but not in its entirety as some counties might be applying different methods. The court had no inclination or intention to devise and impose a plan for constitutional apportionment, a legislative function, but it entered a judgment of unconstitutionality and provided for a further judgment and an order which could be promptly appealed if desired.


OUTCOME: The court ordered that a judgment be entered declaring the challenged apportionment unconstitutional but declined to make suggestions as to constitutional apportionment. It granted permission to amend to allege a class action and denied a motion to drop State parties. The "one person, one vote" principle was held to apply to County Board of Supervisors' elections. The present apportionment violated equal protection.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- apportionment of supervisors in county -- "one person, one vote" principle requires adjudication of unconstitutionality of apportionment of elected Supervisors, each having one vote on Monroe County Board of Supervisors, since population of towns and wards varies from 2,339 to 55,337 -- court adjudges that normal two-year terms of Supervisors to be elected in November, 1965, shall expire on December 31, 1966, unless some constitutional method of apportionment becomes effective before then -- court will entertain application for further relief after April 1, 1966 in light of then existing legislation and efforts to correct situation.

1. The Board of Supervisors of Monroe County consists of 43 Supervisors -- one for each of the 19 towns and one for each of the 24 wards of the City of Rochester. Each Supervisor has one vote. The population of the towns varies from 2,339 to 55,337. The population of the wards varies from 6,965 to 24,595. The situation violates the New York State Constitution (art. I, §§ 1, 11) and the equal protection provision of the United States Constitution (14th Amdt.) and the principle of "one person, one vote" promulgated by the [***2] United States Supreme Court ( Baker v. Carr, 369 U.S. 186, and Reynolds v. Sims, 377 U.S. 533, followed). Section 150 of the County Law, insofar as its application requires the present unconstitutional apportionment, is likewise unconstitutional. The New York Supreme Court, Monroe County, grants judgment so declaring.

2. The court further adjudges that the present Board of Supervisors of Monroe County shall continue to function; and that the Supervisors to be elected in November of 1965 shall be elected as at present, unless a constitutionally acceptable method of apportionment becomes effective before then; however, their normal two-year terms shall expire on December 31, 1966, unless the court shall otherwise meantime direct. The court retains jurisdiction of this case and will entertain an application for further relief after April 1, 1966 in the light of then existing legislative and decisional law and the defendants' interim efforts to comply with the constitutional mandate.

 

2211 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Fenster v. Criminal Court of New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


46 Misc. 2d 179; 259 N.Y.S.2d 67; 1965 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2109



March 31, 1965 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Petitioner has failed to show that he has a clear right to the relief sought and the cross motion is granted and the petition is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant brought a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding seeking an order prohibiting plaintiff Criminal Court of the City of New York from trying him on a vagrancy charge pending before it on the ground that N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 887(1) was on its face unconstitutional. The criminal court cross-moved to dismiss the petition on objections in point of law pursuant to the provisions of N.Y. C.P.L.R. 7804(f).


OVERVIEW: Defendant alleged that within two months of said arrest, he was twice arrested and charged with the same offense. He further alleged that both charges were subsequently dismissed, that he had been subjected to three arrests and prosecutions under the vagrancy law within three successive months and had been put to considerable expense each time in raising bail and for counsel fees, and had been subjected to unnecessary imprisonment including light confinement while awaiting arraignment. Defendant contended that § 887(1) offended U.S. Const. amends. XIII, XIV. The court concluded that defendant failed to show that he had a clear right to the relief sought. The court determined that a man's mere property status, without more, could not be used by a state to test, qualify, or limit his rights as a citizen of the United States. The court explained that the statute was aimed at a lazy person, who hung around the streets and public places without employment when he could obtain something to do. The court concluded that the statute did not limit a person's rights merely on the basis of his property status or on the basis of mere unemployment.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed defendant's petition and granted the criminal court's cross-motion.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- vagrancy -- prosecution of "person who, not having visible means to maintain himself, lives without employment" (Code Crim. Pro., § 887, subd. 1) does not offend United States Constitution (13th and 14th Amdts.) -- prohibition is proper remedy against court acting under unconstitutional statute -- prohibition proceeding dismissed.

1. The Code of Criminal Procedure (§ 887, subd. 1) provides that "The following persons are vagrants: 1. A person who, not having visible means to maintain himself, lives without employment". These words are not too vague to be applied criminally. The Court of Appeals has stated that "Long usage has fixed their significance." ( People v. Sohn, 269 N. Y. 330, 334, followed.) This subdivision does not offend the United States Constitution (13th and 14th Amdts.).

2. Prohibition is a proper remedy to restrain a criminal court from trying a person for a violation of a clearly unconstitutional statute. However, petitioner has not shown a clear right to this remedy, hence this proceeding is [***2] dismissed.

 

2212 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Bryant v. Wilkins


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Wyoming County


45 Misc. 2d 923; 258 N.Y.S.2d 455; 1965 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2122



March 29, 1965 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motion for partial summary judgment is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmates moved for partial summary judgment directing respondents, Commissioner of Corrections and prison warden, to issue rules and regulations respecting the rights of the inmates to practice the religion of Islam.


OVERVIEW: The inmates brought an action against respondents in federal court claiming they had been subjected to religious persecution and discrimination while incarcerated. The federal court held that the initial balancing of rights had to be determined by the state court. The inmates brought a motion for partial summary judgment and the court granted the motion. The court found that in refusing to recognize the Black Muslim group as a "religion," the Commissioner assumed powers beyond the scope of his legislative authority and denied free exercise of religion and equal protection of the law guaranteed by the Constitution. The court determined that the Commissioner's rules were drafted on the unconstitutional assumption that he could determine that the Muslims were a "sham" and not entitled to any religious exercises. The court found that the Commissioner could not fix arbitrary standards for clergymen who sought to visit inmates. The court ordered the Commissioner to revise the regulations and stated that he could put restrictions on the time, place, and conditions members of all sects could hold congregational meetings, but could not deny inmates their free exercise of religion.


OUTCOME: The court granted the inmates' motion for partial summary judgment and ordered the Commissioner to prepare and promulgate revised regulations respecting the inmates' right to practice their religion.


HEADNOTES

Prisons and prisoners -- religious services -- in refusing to recognize Black Muslims as religion, Commissioner of Correction has violated prisoners' right to free exercise of religion -- Commissioner adopted regulations on assumption that Black Muslimism was sham and its members not entitled to practice it -- Commissioner unconstitutionally fixed standards for clergymen, restricting giving of spiritual advice to "qualified" clergymen of "approved" faith and infringed on petitioners' rights -- Commissioner is directed to promulgate regulations consistent with Constitution.

1. In refusing to recognize the religion of Islam as practiced by a sect known as Black Muslims and headed by Elijah Muhammed, the Commissioner of Correction has assumed powers beyond the scope of his legislative authority, has contradicted an affirmed finding by a Federal court that it is a religion and has denied to prisoners, who profess that faith, the free exercise of religion and equal protection of the law guaranteed by the United States Constitution.

2. In adopting regulations and a statement of policy, the Commissioner assumed unconstitutionally [***2] that such religion is a sham and its members not entitled to exercise any religious exercises. Such regulations prevent members from obtaining spiritual advice and ministration from ministers of their own faith and from conducting the religious exercises described in their petitions.

3. Freedom to select the clergy and to determine the qualifications of ministers and chaplains is an essential part of the free exercise of religion entitled to Federal constitutional protection against State interference. There is no authority in the Commissioner to fix arbitrary standards for clergymen who seek to visit inmates in State prisons and provide religious guidance to them. The Legislature has never defined "clergymen" to require State certification of them. Regulations of the Commissioner restricting the giving of spiritual advice to a "qualified" clergyman of an "approved" faith permit exclusion of all Black Muslim inmates from the exercise of every religious service and infringe on petitioners' constitutional rights.

4. The Commissioner is directed to promulgate regulations consistent with the Federal and State Constitutions which will maintain the balance between prison security and [***3] the free exercise of religion, and indicate the time, place and conditions under which members of all religious sects will be permitted to hold services in the prisons in accordance with the intent of section 610 of the Correction Law.

 

2213 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Walsh v. New York State Liquor Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Suffolk County


45 Misc. 2d 601; 257 N.Y.S.2d 971; 1965 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2149



March 22, 1965 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion by defendant to dismiss the complaint is granted, the application for injuction denied, and the stay heretofore granted is vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff liquor licensees brought a declaratory judgment action against defendant New York State Liquor Authority to declare the Authority's Bulletin No. 390 and Rule 17 unconstitutional. The liquor licensees sought to enjoin the issuance of any new licenses. The Liquor Authority filed a motion to dismiss the complaint.


OVERVIEW: The liquor licensees alleged that the Liquor Authority intended to issue an indiscriminate number of new licenses that would cause the liquor licensees a loss of revenue from their existing retail liquor stores. The Liquor Authority suggested that the liquor licensees could not presume that the Liquor Authority would act illegally or improperly. The court held that the liquor licensees had not been deprived of property without due process of law and that the liquor licensees were not entitled to an injunction. The issuance of new liquor licenses did not constitute an illegal taking of property. The liquor licensees did not have a constitutional right to limited competition. The Liquor Authority's policy statement was not prima facie illegal. There was no basis for an injunction based upon an unfounded presumption that the Liquor Authority would exceed its authority.


OUTCOME: The court denied the liquor licensees' motion for an injunction and vacated its previous stay of the issuing of new liquor licenses. The court granted the Liquor Authority's motion to dismiss the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Intoxicating liquors -- policy affecting issuance of licenses for retail package stores -- complaint in action to declare Bulletin No. 390 of State Liquor Authority, dealing with issuance of new package liquor store licenses, unconstitutional is dismissed where plaintiffs have not been deprived of property without due process of law.

In an action by plaintiffs, owners of existing licensed retail package liquor stores, to declare the State Liquor Authority's Bulletin No. 390 and rule 17, stating a policy with respect to the issuance of new licenses, unconstitutional, their complaint is dismissed. Plaintiffs have not been deprived of any property rights without due process of law. The granting of a license to plaintiffs does not create an eternal contract not to increase the number of licenses. Any issuance of a new license, fairly made or otherwise, will inconvenience plaintiffs. The general policy statements in the bulletin and rule have not been, in this action, applied to a specific case.

 

2214 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Petition of Orans


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


45 Misc. 2d 616; 257 N.Y.S.2d 839; 1965 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2172



March 15, 1965 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner citizens brought a proceeding against respondent state officials for judicial review of the Reapportionment Compliance Act (Act), 1964 N.Y. Laws, ch. 976. The officials made a motion to dismiss pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3211(a).


OVERVIEW: The United States Supreme Court held that New York's "apportionment scheme" violated the Equal Protection clause of the Fourteenth Amendment. In response, the Legislature passed the Act in a special session. The plan set forth in the Act increased the size of the state Assembly from 150 to 165 members. Prior to commencement of the state action, a federal district court found that the Act did not violate the federal constitution. The court held that the Act violated the New York Constitution. The court found that the "scheme" found unconstitutional consisted only of the formulae found in N.Y. Const. art. III, §§ 4, 5 as applied in the particular statute before the Supreme Court. The court held that N.Y. Const. art. III, § 2 had not been invalidated, and provided for no more than 150 members of the Assembly. The court held that the number of members could not be increased without an amendment or a new constitution, thus the Act was invalid. The court found that the provisions of the Act with regard to the state Senate must also fall, because N.Y. Const. art. III, § 5 required apportionment of both houses "by the same law."


OUTCOME: The court held that the Reapportionment Compliance Act was invalid under the New York Constitution.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- apportionment of Legislature -- provisions in Reapportionment Compliance Act (L. 1964, ch. 976) for 165-member State Assembly was invalid as disregarding constitutional mandate for 150-member Assembly (N. Y. Const., art. III, § 2) which survived United States Supreme Court's invalidation ( WMCA v. Lomenzo, 377 U.S. 633) of State's scheme of legislative apportionment (art. III, §§ 4, 5) -- reapportionment of Senate was inseparable from that of Assembly and was also void despite separability provision in reapportionment statute -- statute was not rendered invalid merely for having been enacted at special, rather than regular, session of Legislature -- New York Supreme Court had jurisdiction to review reapportionment statute (N. Y. Const., art. III, § 5; L. 1911, ch. 773) -- situation created by United States courts' decisions was not such emergency as to justify reapportionment statute on basis of constitutional provision (art. III, § 25) for emergency governmental operation after enemy attack or natural disaster -- antigerrymandering provisions of State Constitution (art. III, §§ 4, 5) survived United States Supreme Court's ruling; evidence properly considered  [***2] by New York Supreme Court established prima facie cases of gerrymandering requiring hearing which however need not be held until determination of appeals concerning constitutionality of legislation.

1. The provision in the Reapportionment Compliance Act (L. 1964, ch. 976) for a State Assembly of 165 members (and alternative reapportionment plans [L. 1964, chs. 977, 979, 981] for, respectively, 180, 186 and 174 Assemblymen, held by the U. S. Dist. Ct. [S. D. N. Y.] to be unconstitutional in view of U. S. Const., 14th Amdt.) disregarded the constitutional mandate for an Assembly of 150 members (N. Y. Const., art. III, § 2) and was invalid.

2. New York State's "scheme" of legislative apportionment (N. Y. Const., art. III) which the United States Supreme Court's decision ( WMCA v. Lomenzo, 377 U.S. 633) invalidated as violating the equal protection clause (U. S. Const., 14th Amdt., § 1) was composed only of the formulae found in sections 4 and 5 of article III of the State Constitution and the statutes found in article 8 of the State Law -- and no more. There was nothing inherent in the invalidation of the "scheme" which would require that the provision (art. III, § [***3] 2), dictating the size of the Assembly, be likewise considered invalid. On the contrary, the United States Supreme Court held ( Reynolds v. Sims, 377 U.S. 533) that State constitutional provisions should be deemed violative of the Federal Constitution only when validly asserted constitutional rights could not otherwise be protected and effectuated; that courts should attempt to accommodate the relief ordered to the apportionment provisions of State constitutions insofar as possible; that determining the size of its legislative bodies is a matter within the discretion of each individual State, and that its opinion should not be read as indicating that there are any Federal constitutional maximums or minimums on the size of State legislative bodies.

3. The constitutional provision for 150 Assemblymen (proposed by the Constitutional Convention of 1894) cannot be held, upon any principles of constitutional history including a review of the 1894 Convention Record, to be so inextricably interwoven with the apportionment scheme, also proposed by that convention and presently held invalid by the Federal courts, that such requirement of a 150-member Assembly must also necessarily fall.

 [***4] 4. The legislative increase in Assembly seats cannot be based upon the so-called "historic constitutional policy" of giving each county at least one Assembly seat (N. Y. Const., art. III, § 5) inasmuch as such policy was not observed in the increase from 150 to 165 Assemblymen in the Reapportionment Compliance Act itself. Moreover, the disparate loudness of the voice of the smaller counties appears to be exactly what the United States Supreme Court held to be condemned by the Federal Constitution.

5. The conclusion that the Legislature was without power to increase the size of the Assembly does not mean that the number can never be changed. Previous uniform practice has been to embody such change in a new Constitution or a constitutional amendment to be adopted by vote of the people. The specification of the number of Assemblymen was placed in the State Constitution by the people and it should not be changed except by appropriate constitutional amendment voted upon by the people. Only this method is available on this aspect of the case and, unless and until it is utilized, the size of the Assembly as set out (art. III, § 2) must stand.

6. The constitutional provision that [***5] reapportionment and redistricting of Senate and Assembly should be "by the same law" (art. III, § 5) forbade a severance of the Houses of the Legislature for such purposes. The two are inseparable and if one falls for invalidity the other must fall with it. Consequently, although the "separability" provision in the Reapportionment Compliance Act (§ 401) does not purport to save the statute as to one House when the act as to the other is held void, even if it undertook to do so, the Constitution takes precedence and governs. Accordingly, the 1964 apportionment statutes are void as to Senate reapportionment and redistricting as well as to that of the Assembly.

7. The Reapportionment Compliance Act was not rendered unconstitutional by virtue of its having been enacted at an Extraordinary or Special Session of the Legislature, rather than at a "regular session" thereof. Constitutional provisions (art. III, §§ 4, 5) for apportionment at a "regular session" have been interpreted as merely defining the time when the Legislature is first empowered to alter districts for legislative representation, rather than as qualifying the character of the session at which the apportionment can [***6] be enacted, and a careful study of subsequent relevant amendments and proposed amendments to the Constitution requires the same interpretation today. Moreover, the statement of the Federal District Court that its order, issued in compliance with the United States Supreme Court's decision, had given the Legislature sufficient time to reapportion "during the regular session" cannot be relied on to support any inference that such reapportionment must as matter of law be enacted at a regular session, especially since the District Court pointed out that it was not passing upon claims under the State Constitution.

8. Jurisdiction of the New York Supreme Court over the person of the respondents and the subject matter of the proceeding is well established by virtue of the State Constitution (art. III, § 5) making apportionment subject to review by the Supreme Court at the suit of any citizen; by the applicable State statutes (L. 1911, ch. 773) enacted pursuant thereto and in furtherance thereof, and by the refusal of the United States courts to pass upon the validity of the Reapportionment Compliance Act under the State Constitution.

9. The situation created by or resulting from the decisions [***7] of the United States courts, requiring the enactment of reapportionment legislation, was not such emergency as to have compelled abandonment of all provisions of article III of the State Constitution. The emergency envisaged by constitutional provision (art. III, § 25) empowering the Legislature to provide for emergency governmental operation and succession to public office in cases of enemy attack or natural disaster, must be more dire and critical than the basis here presented as justification for the Legislature's action.

10. The antigerrymandering provisions of the State Constitution forbidding division of counties and towns into legislative districts, with stated exceptions, and requiring continuity, convenience and compact form for districts (art. III, §§ 4, 5) survived the condemnation of the United States Supreme Court's ruling in support of the "one-man, one-vote" principle. The New York Supreme Court was authorized under both Federal and State Constitutions to receive and consider proffered evidence of alleged gerrymandering. Several isolated but significant factors support allegations that open partisan gerrymandering was a fundamental purpose and accomplished fact [***8] of the apportionment statutes enacted in December, 1964. Figures and analyses presented established prima facie cases of gerrymandering. However, they cannot, in view of the respondents' denial and defenses, warrant any disposition as a matter of law. A hearing must be held to resolve the factual issues raised. However, inasmuch as the statute has been held invalid, such hearing need not be held until the United States Supreme Court and the New York State Court of Appeals determine appeals already pending or to be taken concerning the constitutionality of the reapportionment legislation.

 

2215 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Norris


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Kings County


46 Misc. 2d 44; 258 N.Y.S.2d 967; 1965 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2186



March 10, 1965 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion to suppress is in all respects denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to suppress evidence asserting that the evidence was obtained as a result of an illegal search and seizure without a search warrant and without probable cause.


OVERVIEW: Defendant filed a motion to suppress evidence. At the hearing on the motion to suppress the arresting officer testified that at approximately 3:00 a.m. he observed defendant and another man on a street corner. As he approached, the other man ran away and that was what attracted his attention to defendant. The officer testified that as he approached, defendant put his hand under the glove he was wearing on his left hand, or up his sleeve. The officer testified that defendant's hand movements made him apprehensive. A search revealed a number of marijuana cigarettes. The court denied the motion finding that the officer had probable cause to stop and frisk defendant because the time of the incident, coupled with the other man's sudden departure and defendant's furtive movements warranted the officer in reasonably suspecting that defendant was committing, had committed, or was about to commit a crime. The officer was authorized to search defendant's person for dangerous weapons.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion to dismiss evidence.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- unlawful search and seizure -- circumstances warranted police officer in suspecting that defendant was committing crime; search for dangerous weapon was proper -- search having revealed marijuana cigarettes, substance constituting crime, search, seizure and arrest were proper.

1. A police officer was warranted in reasonably suspecting that defendant "is committing, has committed or is about to commit" (Code Crim. Pro., § 180-a, subd. 1) one of the crimes named in said statute by its occurrence at 3:00 a.m., the hurried departure of defendant's companion upon the police officer's approach, and defendant's sudden and unexplained movement toward his glove or sleeve. The officer was therefore authorized to make a search of defendant's person "for a dangerous weapon".

2. The search or frisk, leading to the arrest, having taken place while defendant was "abroad in a public place" (§ 180-a, subd. 1) and having revealed "the possession" by him of marijuana cigarettes, the substance constituting the crime, the police officer was warranted in taking it and arresting him. The motion to suppress is denied.

 

2216 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Chittenden v. Chittenden


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Monroe County


46 Misc. 2d 347; 259 N.Y.S.2d 738; 1965 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2188



March 10, 1965 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff ex-wife brought an action against defendants ex-husband and second spouse for a judgment declaring invalid the judgment of a Mexican court divorcing the ex-spouses and declaring invalid the subsequent marriage of the ex-husband. The ex-husband and second spouse moved to dismiss the complaint on the ground that the court had not acquired jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: An ex-wife brought an action against her ex-husband and his second spouse. The ex-wife sought to have declared invalid the judgment of a Mexican court divorcing the ex-spouses, and she also sought to have declared invalid the ex-husband's second marriage. The ex-husband and his second spouse moved to dismiss the complaint on the ground that the court had not acquired jurisdiction. The court found that personal service on the ex-husband was properly made pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 232, 235. The court also found that service by publication on the second spouse was proper under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 314. The court concluded that an action to alter or affect matrimonial status was a proceeding in rem where substituted service by publication or in any other authorized form was sufficient notice to answer the requirement of due process. The court held that service on both the ex-husband and the second spouse was proper as to that part of the complaint seeking judgment declaring the Mexican divorce invalid. The court found no requirement that the child of the ex-husband and the second spouse be made a party to the proceeding.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion to dismiss brought by the ex-husband and his second wife. The court held that service on both the ex-husband and the second wife was effective as to that part of the complaint that asked for judgment declaring the Mexican divorce invalid.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Husband and wife -- foreign divorce -- New York Supreme Court finds that wife neither signed application to Mexican court for divorce nor appeared there -- her action to declare Mexican divorce void is in rem, hence her husband and his new, nonresident wife were both properly served in Pennsylvania -- affidavit of service (under Civ. Prac. Act, § 235) may be filed late -- child of husband's new marriage is not necessary party -- court does not decide whether service in Pennsylvania will support that part of complaint which seeks declaration that new marriage is invalid.

1. A Mexican decree of divorce recited that both parties appeared personally before that court. The decree was based on an application for a divorce by mutual consent, purportedly signed by husband and wife. The New York Supreme Court finds that the wife did not sign the application and that she did not appear in the Mexican court personally or in any other manner.

2. An action by the wife to declare the Mexican decree void is in rem, hence both the husband and his new wife, who is not a domiciliary of New York, were properly served personally in Pennsylvania (Civ. Prac. Act, § 232, subd. 1; § 235; CPLR [***2] 314; 105, subd. [m]).

3. The affidavit of service on the husband in Pennsylvania was not filed here, as required (by Civ. Prac. Act, § 235); however, this defect is not jurisdictional and the court will permit a late filing now.

4. The child of the new marriage is not a necessary party to this action.

5. The court does not decide whether the services of summons in Pennsylvania will support that part of this action by which plaintiff seeks a judgment declaring that the marriage of the defendants is void and that plaintiff's marriage to her said husband is still valid.

 

2217 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Newman v. Charles S. Nathan, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


46 Misc. 2d 407; 259 N.Y.S.2d 637; 1965 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2189



March 10, 1965 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Third-party defendant, a Kentucky corporation, filed a motion to dismiss the third-party action filed against it by third-party plaintiff buyer for lack of personal jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: The corporation manufactured a part which the buyer purchased and included in its manufacture of chairs. An individual filed an action against the buyer claiming that the chair caused his injuries. The buyer filed a third-party complaint against the corporation. The corporation claimed that the buyer could not maintain an action against it in a New York court because the corporation did not have contacts in the State of New York. The court denied the corporation's motion to dismiss, holding that the nondomiciliary corporation was amenable to the personal jurisdiction of the New York courts under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 302(a)(2). The court found that the corporation could have reasonably anticipated that its product would be sold in New York as a substantial part of another product. The court noted that it was uncontroverted that the corporation knew that the part it manufactured was being made a part of a chair that was sold throughout the country, including in New York. The court concluded that the requirements of § 302(a)(2) were constitutional under the Due Process Clause of the United States Constitution. The court found that New York was the most convenient forum for the trial.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the corporation's motion to dismiss the third-party complaint against it for lack of personal jurisdiction.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Process -- service on nonresidents -- breach of warranty is tortious act -- nondomiciliary manufacturer whose product is incorporated into article reasonably anticipated to be sold in New York is amenable to personal jurisdiction under "long arm" statute ( CPLR 302); its motion to dismiss third-party complaint is denied -- New York is most convenient forum for trial.

1. A breach of warranty is a tortious act within the meaning of the "long arm" statute ( CPLR 302, subd. [a], par. 2).

2. A nondomiciliary manufacturer, regardless of intervening transactions outside New York, is amenable to the personal jurisdiction of the New York courts under CPLR 302 (subd. [a], par. 2) if there be a reasonably anticipated sale in New York of the specific product which caused the injury, by itself or as a substantial part of another product. Here the complaint sufficiently alleges the commission of a tortious act in this State to conform to CPLR 302 which statute is constitutional under the due process clause of the United States Constitution. The motion of the nondomiciliary to dismiss the third-party complaint is denied.

3. Since the witnesses to the occurrence which is the subject [***2] of this action are New York residents, this State is the most convenient forum for trial.

 

2218 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Augello


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Erie County


45 Misc. 2d 402; 257 N.Y.S.2d 125; 1965 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2194



March 9, 1965 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought an order in the trial court (New York), to take interrogatories of a witness out of the state. Defendant desired to introduce the interrogatory at a hearing which would determine the voluntariness of defendant's alleged confession in connection with his prosecution and conviction for first-degree felony murder. The court granted defendant's motion for a "Huntley" hearing.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought the testimony of his former wife, who was allegedly present at the time of defendant's apprehension and interrogation. The People contended that the examination of witnesses on commission out of state was controlled by N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 636, which provided that when an issue of fact was joined upon an indictment, defendant could have any material witness residing out of the state examined in his behalf. The court rejected the People's contention that the type of proof to be presented on a "Huntley" type hearing was not a joinder of an issue of fact "on an indictment" within the meaning of § 636. The court held that defendant was entitled to the commission he sought. The court granted defendant's motion because an issue of fact was joined upon the original indictment in the hearing.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- confessions -- post-conviction hearing on voluntariness of his confession having been granted, defendant is granted commission to take testimony of nonresident witness (Code Crim. Pro., § 636).

Defendant, after his conviction of murder, was granted a hearing as to the voluntariness of his confession ( People v. Huntley, 15 N Y 2d 72, followed). For the purpose of the hearing, he is entitled to a commission to examine out of the State (Code Crim. Pro., § 636) his former wife who was allegedly present when he was arrested and interrogated, and who now lives in California.

 

2219 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Blanchfield


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special and Trial Term, New York County


45 Misc. 2d 536; 257 N.Y.S.2d 438; 1965 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2220



March 2, 1965 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff state filed a motion, pursuant to N.Y. Jud. Law § 749-aa, for a special jury to try the murder first degree case against defendant.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested and charged with murder in the first degree. Subsequently, the state filed a motion for a special jury to try the case. Defendant opposed the motion on the ground that the affidavit merely set forth the usual form averments on such an application. Defendant further opposed the motion, citing the Recommendation and Survey Relating to the Abolition of Special Juries contained in the Eighteenth Annual Report of the New York Judicial Council (1952). He argued that the survey showing imbalances as to occupations, educational qualifications, and sex between the special and general jury panels should now, in view of the more liberal and progressive reasoning evidenced in recent decisions, lead to the conclusion that the operation of the special jury system would deprive him of due process. The court granted the state's motion for a special jury, by holding that although, if again presented with this issue, the Supreme Court might hold that a special jury would deny due process, the court could not so hold because it was bound by the previous determinations of the Supreme Court in the absence of proof which would support a contrary conclusion.


OUTCOME: The court granted the state's motion for a special jury to try the murder first degree case against defendant.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- murder in first degree -- special jury -- motion for special jury in murder first degree case granted; case is one of "importance" (Judiciary Law, § 749-aa) -- operation of special jury system does not deprive defendant of due process.

1. In a murder first degree case, the motion of the District Attorney for a special jury under section 749-aa of the Judiciary Law is granted. A capital case is one of "importance" within the terms of the statute. The possibility of imposing capital punishment still exists under the new procedure for determining the sentence for murder in the first degree.
