In re Estate of Unseld


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, Nassau County


44 Misc. 2d 649; 254 N.Y.S.2d 744; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1165



December 18, 1964 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The claim of the Department of Mental Hygiene is accordingly dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court addressed defendant Department of Mental Hygiene's claim against the testatrix's estate in plaintiff executor proceeding under N.Y. Surr. Ct. Act. § 211-b.


OVERVIEW: Upon her death in 1963, the testatrix left a daughter that was a patient in a state hospital. In 1952, an agent of the Department indicated to the testatrix that it would accept less than the monthly cost of the daughter's care but that the Department reserved the right to file a claim against the testatrix's estate. After the testatrix's death, the executor brought a proceeding under N.Y. Surr. Ct. Act § 211-b, and the Department filed a claim against the testatrix's estate. The executor argued against the claim, arguing that the amendment to N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law § 24(1) effected by 1956 N.Y. Laws ch. 809 removed parents from those who could be liable for the differential between the actual cost of hospitalization and the amount set by the Commissioner of the Department. The court dismissed the Department's claim, finding that the 1956 amendment removed all past, present, and future liability of parents for the differential between the actual cost of hospitalization of a child in a state institution and the lesser payment set by the Commissioner. The court ruled that communication by the Department's agent did not amount to an agreement by the testatrix to pay any differential.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the Department's claim.


HEADNOTES

Insane and other incompetent persons -- liability for care -- mother of patient in State mental hospital did not make agreement to pay differential for care of daughter between actual cost and lesser sum set by Commissioner by letter of Commissioner -- moreover, by amendment of subdivision 1 of section 24 of Mental Hygiene Law in 1956, parents no longer liable for such differential; claim of Department of Mental Hygiene against mother's estate is dismissed.

1. The State Department of Mental Hygiene wrote testatrix, the mother of a patient in a State mental hospital, on March 6, 1952 that although the actual cost of hospitalization for her daughter was $ 85 per month, they would accept $ 12 per month. Such letter did not constitute an agreement on the part of testatrix to pay any differential.

2. The amendment of subdivision 1 of section 24 of the Mental Hygiene Law, effected in 1956, removed the authority of the Commissioner of Mental Hygiene to assess a differential claim against anyone except the patient or his estate, and removed any liability, past, present and future, of parents for the differential [***2] between the actual cost of maintenance and hospitalization of a child in a State institution and the lesser payment set by the Commissioner. The claim of the State Department of Mental Hygiene is dismissed.

 

2235 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Port Jefferson v. Board of Supervisors


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Suffolk County


44 Misc. 2d 1083; 256 N.Y.S.2d 34; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1172



December 18, 1964 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The complaint is therefore dismissed.

The companion proceeding herein, whereby the Treasurer of the Incorporated Village of Port Jefferson seeks from the County Treasurer a refund of all sums representing taxes levied and collected against real property within the village since its incorporation to pay annual expenses of the County Police Department, is, for the same reasons, also dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, village, mayor, and taxpayers, sought a judicial declaration that the incorporation of the village automatically effected its dissociation from the Suffolk County Police District (district). Defendant Board of Supervisors of the County of Suffolk opposed a dissociation.


OVERVIEW: The village was incorporated three years after the district was formed. The territory that comprised the village, formerly a part of a town, had elected to be a part of the district. Plaintiffs sought to dissociate from the village. The court dismissed the complaint. The court found that the act of the village's incorporation did not automatically dissociate the village from the district which its inhabitants, before incorporation, as residents of the town, had irrevocably elected to join. The court rejected plaintiffs' contention that the enactment of the county charter operated to transfer a function of local government from the village without the majority consent of its electors in violation of N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 2. The court noted that the village was not even in existence at the time the charter was enacted. The court determined that the district could not be disembodied except by approval of a majority of the electors of the entire district.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the complaint seeking a dissociation of the village from the police district.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- Suffolk County Charter and Police District -- voters of newly incorporated village, within town whose voters had voted to establish Suffolk County Police District, cannot now vote to dissociate village from said police district (Suffolk County Charter, §§ 103, 1206, 1207, 1210, subd. a) -- levy of taxes on realty within such village, to support Suffolk County Police District, constitutional (N. Y. Const., art. I, §§ 1, 6, 11; art. III, § 17; art. IX, former § 2; U. S. Const., 14th Amdt.) -- court of original jurisdiction declines to adjudge that, because supervisors of towns of very unequal population each have same vote on County Board of Supervisors, Suffolk County Charter is unconstitutional.

1. The Suffolk County Charter (L. 1958, ch. 278) became operative [***2] on January 1, 1960, following a referendum thereon and the election of a County Executive thereunder. With the referendum, a proposition was also submitted and approved under the Charter, establishing one unified Suffolk County Police District for the five contiguous towns which voted in favor of this proposition. The Suffolk County Charter provided that all general or special laws should be modified or superseded in their application to Suffolk County by the adoption of the Charter (§ 103), and that such vote for one unified police district "shall be irrevocable" (§ 1207). Accordingly, the general provisions of the Village Law ( §§ 34, 35, 188-a) which appear to free any newly created village from liability for future debts of any special district of which its territory was a part, and which authorize an incorporated village to establish its own police department, do not apply to the Suffolk County Police District. Three years after the Suffolk County Police District was created, the Village of Port Jefferson was incorporated, within the Town of Brookhaven. The electors of that town had voted in favor of the police district. The electors of such newly created village had no [***3] power to vote to dissociate the village from the County Police District and to establish a separate police department for the village.

2. The Suffolk County Charter (L. 1958, ch. 278, as amd.) was adopted in accordance with article IX of the New York State Constitution. The provisions of said Charter (§§ 1206, 1207, 1210, subd. a), under which the Suffolk County Police District is established and is supported by taxes levied on the real property within said police district, are valid and constitutional. It cannot be said that those provisions, as applied to the subsequently created Village of Port Jefferson, violate constitutional provisions against "local bills" for "Incorporating villages" (N. Y. Const., art. III, § 17), or constitute a deprivation of home rule, or a "transfer" of a function of an existing village government without its electors' consent (N. Y. Const., art. IX, former § 2), or a disenfranchisement of the voters of this village, or a denial to them of the equal protection of the laws, or an imposition on them of a tax burden which deprives them of liberty or property (N. Y. Const., art. I, §§ 1, 6, 11; U. S. Const., 14th Amdt.).

3. In Suffolk County, which has [***4] no cities or wards, the towns have been the basic units of government since 1703 (L. 1703, ch. 131). Each town supervisor is chosen by the entire electorate of his town to sit on the County Board of Supervisors, and there he has only one vote, regardless of the great differences in population among the various towns. This system has not been repealed or modified by the State Constitution (first adopted in 1777) or by any subsequent Legislature of the State. There is now pending in the United States District Court an action to declare the Suffolk County Charter unconstitutional on the ground of this disproportionate representation on the County Board of Supervisors by the supervisors of towns which have small populations. In this situation, this State court of original jurisdiction will not adjudge that, because of such disproportionate representation, the Suffolk County Charter (particularly §§ 201, 203, 1206, 1207, 1210, subd. a) and the County Law ( §§ 150, 153, subd. 4) violate the due process and equal protection clauses of both the State and the Federal Constitutions. The court will rest on the presumption of constitutionality of legislation. Therefore, this action and this [***5] proceeding, seeking a refund of, and an injunction against, taxes levied on real property in the Village of Port Jefferson for the support of the Suffolk County Police District, are dismissed.

 

2236 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Goldenthal v. Terry


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Queens County


44 Misc. 2d 851; 255 N.Y.S.2d 151; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1212



December 11, 1964 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant brought a motion seeking a dismissal of plaintiff's action. The motion was brought pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3211(a)(8) on the ground that the court lacked jurisdiction over defendant.


OVERVIEW: Substituted service was made on defendant by posting a copy of the summons and complaint at his last known address pursuant to an order of the court issued in reliance upon N.Y. C.P.L.R. 308(4). Defendant claimed that the service was invalid since he was not living at the address specified in the order at the time service was made. Defendant also contended that § 308(4) was unconstitutional as an improper delegation of power to the judiciary. The court denied the motion, reasoning that it was not disputed that defendant was residing at the address prior to the time that service was made, that he answered correspondence delivered to that address, and that he listed that address as his residence with the Motor Vehicle Bureau. The court explained that an existing domicile continued until a new one was acquired and that the burden of proof rested upon the party who alleged a change. The court determined that a legislative enactment carried with it an exceedingly strong presumption of constitutionality. The court stated that although the presumption was rebuttable, defendant failed to show beyond a reasonable doubt that § 308(4) was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Process -- substituted service -- substituted service in compliance with order of court was valid even though defendant was not living at address specified at time service was made -- CPLR 308 (subd. 4) permitting court to devise method of service of process is not unconstitutional delegation of legislative power to court.

1. Substituted service in compliance with an order of the court was not invalid and gave the court jurisdiction even though defendant was not living at the address specified in the order when service was made. Defendant had been residing there and he offers no evidence that he acquired a new residence. An existing domicile continues until a new one is acquired and the burden of proof rests upon the party who alleges a change. Defendant fails to show that the service was not reasonably calculated to give him notice and an opportunity to be heard.

2. The power of the court to determine a method of service of process in special cases pursuant to CPLR 308 (subd. 4) is judicial and the subdivision is not unconstitutional as an unlawful delegation of legislative power. The court is peculiarly suited to devise a method since it can ascertain the facts and make [***2] a determination based thereon.

 

2237 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Johnston


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Police Court of New York, Village of Maybrook


44 Misc. 2d 651; 255 N.Y.S.2d 59; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1210



December 11, 1964 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants filed a motion to dismiss the respective informations against them filed by the State. All four defendants were charged with having violated a local law by willfully storing abandoned, junked, discarded, and unlicensed motor vehicles in violation of the local law and after notice. Two of the defendants also were charged with violation of the local law by resisting and obstructing village agents in removing such vehicles.


OVERVIEW: The court analyzed the local law and found that the law at issue established no condition prerequisite to the village clerks' sending the notice, no provision of any kind was made for entry upon land, or the removal and destruction of the motor vehicle, either as to authority, responsibility, time, place, manner of doing, and record of property taken; or for any deposition, complaint, information, hearing, warrant, writ, or determination prior to the entry upon land and removal and destruction of the motor vehicle. The court noted that two of the informations referred to those defendants as having willfully stored abandoned motor vehicles. The court held that willfully storing on one's property negated abandonment, therefore, those two informations contained a defense to the charges upon their face. The court held that the portion of the local law assessing a penalty against violators was unenforceable, because it was so confusing in language. The court also held that defendants were within their rights in resisting the unlawful action of the village in attempting to remove the property without due process of law.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the informations.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- automobile graveyards -- village's local law relating to abandoned, junked and discarded motor vehicles is constitutionally invalid.

A local law relating to abandoned, junked, discarded and unlicensed motor vehicles is constitutionally invalid. Its language is confused and unenforcible. There is no authority, except by implication, for an entry upon private property against the desire of the occupant. The act of the village's agent in removing property for destruction under the implication of the local law, is in violation of defendants' rights to be secure against unreasonable seizures, and a deprivation of property without due process of law. Defendants were within their rights in resisting the unlawful action of the village. The informations against them charging violations of the local law are dismissed.

 

2238 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Ceravit Corp. v. Black Diamond S.S. Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Special Term, New York County


44 Misc. 2d 484; 254 N.Y.S.2d 253; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1259



November 25, 1964 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the motion to dismiss the action is granted, and judgment shall be entered accordingly.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court considered the motion of defendant carrier that sought to dismiss the complaint of plaintiff shipper that sought to recover for damage caused when a shipment of detergents froze.


OVERVIEW: The shipper contracted with the carrier for the carrier to transport a shipment of detergents. The shipment froze, and the detergents were damaged. The shipper filed an action against the carrier that sought to recover for the damages. The carrier filed a motion to dismiss the complaint based on the court's lack of jurisdiction over the matter because both companies were foreign corporations with insufficient contacts with New York to provide the court with jurisdiction over the dispute. The court granted the carrier's motion to dismiss the complaint. The court explained that N.Y. Bus. Corp. § 1314(b)(5) enabled an action against the carrier if the carrier was engaged in business in New York. The court determined that the carrier was not a New York corporation and the shipment contract with the shipper had no substantial contacts with New York, so the carrier was entitled to dismiss the complaint.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion of the carrier that sought to dismiss the shipper's action to recover for damage caused to a shipment of detergent.


HEADNOTES

Corporations -- foreign corporations -- jurisdiction over interstate carriers -- section 1314 (subd. [b], par. [5]) of Business Corporation Law unconstitutional when applied to cause of action in which defendant foreign corporation is interstate carrier, contract was not made in this State and goods accepted for transport were upon route having no contact with New York State.

Section 1314 (subd. [b], par. [5]) of the Business Corporation Law, authorizing an action by a foreign corporation against another foreign corporation doing business in this State, is unconstitutional when applied to a cause of action in which defendant is an interstate carrier, the contract was not made in this State, and the goods accepted for transport were upon a route having no contact with this State. Accordingly, an action by a Swiss corporation to recover from an interstate carrier authorized to do business in this State, for damages to goods transported from Philadelphia, Pennsylvania, to Switzerland, on a ship which had no contact with New York harbor in the subject transaction,  [***2] pursuant to a contract made in Philadelphia, is dismissed for lack of jurisdiction.

 

2239 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Estrada


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Kings County


44 Misc. 2d 452; 253 N.Y.S.2d 876; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1319



November 9, 1964 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In the trial court (New York), pursuant to N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 813-c, defendant filed a motion to suppress certain evidence he alleged was unconstitutionally obtained that resulted in his indictment for aiding and abetting a felony under N.Y. Penal Law § 1751.


OVERVIEW: The police had a search warrant for a garage only. They saw a man buying a silver foil package from a "seller" in the garage, who sent defendant to obtain the package. A police officer followed the buyer after he left, arrested and searched him, and found the foil package containing narcotics. Defendant and the seller were arrested. Defendant refused to plead guilty and was indicted for aiding and abetting a felony under N.Y. Penal Law § 1751. Defendant filed a motion to suppress, alleging that the contraband unreasonably seized from the buyer could not have been used against him. The court granted the motion and held that the narcotics in question were unconstitutionally seized from the buyer. The court held that the primary taint doctrine applied only to "derivative evidence," but the illegal search of the buyer led to defendant's arrest. The court held that if any "derivative" evidence had been found on defendant, it would not have been admissible as the "fruits of the poisonous tree." The court found that if a search was directed against others than the person searched, then the product of an unreasonable search was not admissible against such others.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion to suppress.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- unlawful search and seizure -- narcotics seized by unlawful search of buyer of package cannot lawfully be used as evidence against defendant who is separately charged with felony of aiding sale of said narcotics; such defendant has standing to move to suppress such evidence (Code Crim. Pro., § 813-c); defendant's motion is granted -- constitutional principles require exclusion, not only of unlawfully seized evidence, but also of fruits of unlawful search.

1. Police observed a garage and saw that a person therein sold a package to a buyer and that the seller sent the present defendant to the rear of the garage to get the package for him. One policeman followed the buyer and arrested him and found narcotics in the package. Meantime, other policemen arrested both the seller and the present defendant. The buyer was charged with the misdemeanor of possession of narcotics ( Penal Law, § 1751-a); but on his pretrial motion, it was held that the search warrant, which the police had obtained, to search the garage, was not intended to include the buyer who was not described in the search warrant, and that what the police observed was not the commission of a crime in their [***2] presence (under Code Crim. Pro., § 177, subd. 1, prior to its amdt. on July 1, 1963); and that, therefore, the search was not a reasonable incident of a lawful arrest; accordingly the narcotics were suppressed as evidence, but, as contraband, were not returned (Code Crim. Pro., § 813-c). As for the seller, the charge against him was reduced to a misdemeanor to which he was permitted to plead guilty. As for the present defendant, he refused to plead guilty, and eventually he was indicted for aiding and abetting the selling, as a felony ( Penal Law, § 1751). The People concede that they propose to use the seized evidence against this defendant. Therefore, his motion to suppress, as against himself, the evidence which had been unlawfully seized from the buyer, is granted (Code Crim. Pro., § 813-c).

2. Regardless of whether or not the court's findings in the case against the buyer are binding in the case against the present defendant, who was not "the moving party" in that other case (cf. Code Crim. Pro., § 813-c), this court independently finds that the package of narcotics was unconstitutionally seized from the buyer.

3. The present defendant has standing to move to suppress [***3] such evidence, since said section 813-c of our Code of Criminal Procedure provides that: "A person claiming to be aggrieved by an unlawful search and seizure and having reasonable grounds to believe that the property * * * may be used as evidence against him in a criminal proceeding, may move for the * * * suppression of its use as evidence."

4. The constitutional prohibition against unreasonable searches and seizures requires the suppression, not only of unlawfully seized evidence, but also of the product or fruits of the unlawful search. This exclusionary principle of law is intended to deter lawless law enforcement. One of the targets of the police officers' unlawful search of the buyer was this present defendant. If this defendant and the buyer had been charged together or had been tried together, this seized evidence would be admissible against neither of them. If both of them had been arrested together after consummation of the sale, both arrests would have been unlawful and the search would have been unreasonable as to both of them. The fact that this defendant has been separately arrested and separately charged should not change the constitutional principle. Accordingly [***4] this defendant's motion to suppress the evidence which was unconstitutionally seized from another person -- the buyer -- is granted.

 

2240 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Van Berkel v. Power


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


44 Misc. 2d 506; 254 N.Y.S.2d 74; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1342



October 30, 1964 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner naturalized citizen filed a motion for an order and judgment declaring unconstitutional, null, and void N.Y. Elec. Law § 150 (embodying part of N. Y. Const. art. II, § 1), which required a person to have been a naturalized citizen for at least 90 days prior to the day of an election in order to vote therein. Respondent Attorney General sought an order dismissing the petition for legal insufficiency.


OVERVIEW: N.Y. Elec. Law § 150 required a person to have been a naturalized citizen for at least 90 days prior to the day of an election in order to vote therein. The naturalized citizen registered to vote but was not allowed to vote in the next general election because the 90-day waiting period had not expired. The naturalized citizen brought a motion for an order declaring § 150 unconstitutional, null, and void. The court held that the State could set forth voter qualifications applying to all citizens but could not establish degrees of citizenship. By imposing the additional 90-day requirement upon a naturalized citizen, the State created a separate standard for citizens who had been naturalized. This was in direct conflict with the Constitution of the United States. Accordingly, N.Y. Const. art. II, § 1, and N.Y. Elec. Law § 150, insofar as they established an additional 90-day period requirement for naturalized citizens, were declared unconstitutional and null and void.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the Attorney General's petition and found in favor of the naturalized citizen. The court ordered the Attorney General to allow the naturalized citizen to vote in the upcoming election.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Elections -- qualifications of voters -- section 1 of article II of New York State Constitution and section 150 of Election Law are declared unconstitutional insofar as they impose additional 90-day period requirement upon naturalized citizens before they may qualify to vote.

1. In a proceeding seeking an order of nullity of a provision of section 150 of the Election Law (embodying part of N. Y. Const., art. II, § 1), a motion by the Attorney-General for dismissal, on the ground that the prerequisite procedural steps (Election Law, § 171) were not taken, is denied, as it appears that a "challenge affidavit" was executed.

2. Petitioner, who was naturalized as a citizen of this country on August 11, 1964, was prevented from registering to vote in the November, 1964 election, since on the day of election he would have been a naturalized citizen for less than 90 days. The State may prescribe reasonable limitations upon the right to vote, but such standards must be consonant with our basic constitutional precepts and may not discriminate between citizens by birth and citizens by naturalization (U. S. Const., 14th Amdt.). Accordingly, section 1 of article II of the New York [***2] State Constitution and section 150 of the New York Election Law, insofar as they establish an additional 90-day period requirement for naturalized citizens before they may qualify to vote, are declared unconstitutional and null and void, and respondents are directed to register petitioner to vote in the forthcoming election if he otherwise complies with the requirements for registration and voting.

People v. Kaplan


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


44 Misc. 2d 110; 252 N.Y.S.2d 927; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1401



October 2, 1964 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the motion to dismiss, made at the close of the People's case, upon which decision was reserved, is granted, and the complaint is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, who was charged with violating N.Y. Penal Law § 484-h by selling magazines containing photographs of nude and partially nude females to a youth under the age of 18 years of age filed a motion to dismiss the charges against her. The court considered the motion.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with violating N.Y. Penal Law § 484-h by selling two magazines containing photographs of nude and partially nude females to a youth under the age of 18 years. At the close of the State's case, defendant filed a motion to dismiss on constitutional grounds. The court held that the statute was unconstitutional in its application to defendant's case. It was the intention of the Legislature to treat separately narrative and pictorial presentations. The publications were sorely lacking in taste, but were not so violative of contemporary community standards as to constitute "hard-core pornography." The court also determined that the statute was so broad in its potential sweep that it was too vague to meet the constitutional requirements of the federal and State constitutions. The statute lacked specificity because it prohibited the pictures in the magazines at issue as well as classic works of art. Therefore, defendant was entitled to dismissal of the charges against her.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion to dismiss the charges against her.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- sale of publications containing photographs of nudes -- section 484-h of Penal Law rendering criminal sale of publication of pictures of nudes posed to arouse passion is not specific and is unconstitutional -- statute differentiates between narrative presentations and pictorial presentations; People v. The Bookcase (14 N Y 2d 409) is not controlling.

1. Section 484-h of the Penal Law which renders criminal the sale to a person under the age of 18 years of a publication containing "pictures of nude or partially denuded figures, posed or presented in a manner to provoke or arouse lust or passion or to exploit sex, lust or perversion for commercial gain" is unconstitutional since it not only applies to the publication sold by defendant portraying bare-breasted and bare-buttocked females of doubtful pulchritude in various poses but also to paintings by the great masters such as Rembrandt and Titian. The sweep of the statute is too broad and it lacks specificity.

2. The Legislature in section 484-h treated separately [***2] narrative presentations which require a finding that the description be of "illicit sex or sexual morality" or that it be "obscene, lewd, lascivious, filthy, indecent or disgusting" and pictorial presentations and so the ruling in People v. The Bookcase (14 N Y 2d 409, 418) holding the statute unconstitutional as to narrative presentations is not controlling.

 

2242 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lenti


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Nassau County


44 Misc. 2d 118; 253 N.Y.S.2d 9; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1425



September 24, 1964 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion to inspect the Grand Jury minutes is denied. The motion to dismiss the indictment is also denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were charged with hazing in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 1030. Defendants filed a motion for an order to inspect the grand jury minutes upon which the indictment was founded and for an order dismissing the indictment arguing that the statute was vague, the acts did not occur in or while attending a public school or institution of learning, the victims consented, and the victims were accomplices as a matter of law.


OVERVIEW: Defendants participated in the initiation of the victims as pledges into their fraternity. The indictment stated that defendants wrongfully and knowingly assaulted the victims by striking them about the body and face. In addition to the charges for hazing in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 1030, defendants were charged with multiple counts of assault. Defendants' main objection was that § 1030 was unconstitutionally vague. The court denied both the motions filed by defendants. The court concluded that: (1) a criminal statute had to be sufficiently explicit so that all those subject to its penalties knew what acts it was their duty to avoid; (2) § 1030 was constitutional as applied because the term hazing had a common meaning that was reasonably ascertainable; (3) acts of hazing which occurred off-campus were prohibited by § 1030; (4) due to the extreme violence of the assaults, it could not be held that the victims had consented to the assaults; and (5) after a review of the grand jury minutes, there was sufficient evidence to sustain the indictment.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendants' motions to inspect the grand jury minutes and to dismiss the indictment against them.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- hazing -- statute making "hazing" misdemeanor (Penal Law, § 1030) is constitutional; word is defined in dictionaries and is commonly understood -- hazing may occur in private home, by persons "in or while attending" school (Penal Law, § 1030 -- indictment for third degree assault (Penal Law, § 244) also proper; victims did not consent to physical harm -- evidence before Grand Jury sufficiently showed criminal intent and corroboration (cf. Code Crim. Pro., § 399).

1. The Penal Law ( § 1030; L. 1894, ch. 265) makes it a misdemeanor to commit "what is commonly called hazing, in or while attending * * * public schools". The statute is not too vague to be valid and constitutional. The term "hazing" is defined in dictionaries and is commonly understood. In this case, four students of a school in a union free school district were properly indicted for hazing five students who were pledges of a fraternity.

2. It is immaterial that the hazing occurred in a private home, not on school grounds. The words "in or while attending" pertain to the status of [***2] the defendants and not to the locus of the hazing.

3. The defendants were also properly indicted for third degree assault (Penal Law, § 244). One boy spent several weeks in a hospital as a result of the beating. It cannot be said that the victims consented to physical harm.

4. The defendants also contend that the evidence before the Grand Jury did not show criminal intent, and that the victims were accomplices whose testimony requires corroboration (cf. Code Crim. Pro., § 399). The court has read the Grand Jury minutes and is satisfied therefrom that the evidence sustains the indictment. Defendants' motion to inspect the Grand Jury minutes and to dismiss the indictment is denied.

People v. Broderick


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Kings County


43 Misc. 2d 1014; 252 N.Y.S.2d 838; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1426



September 24, 1964 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The application is therefore denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant made an application for an order to resentence defendant as a second felony offender rather than a third felony offender upon a judgment, which convicted defendant on his own plea of guilty of attempted possession of burglar's tools as a felony and sentenced defendant as a third felony offender to a term of imprisonment of 5 to 7 years. Defendant asserted that a conviction in Nebraska was obtained in violation of his right to counsel.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed that his conviction of forgery in a Nebraska district court nine years earlier was obtained in violation of his constitutional rights because he was not advised of his right to counsel and did not understandingly and intelligently waive such right. The court denied defendant's application. The court held as follows: (1) defendant's bare averment that he did not consciously waive his right to counsel was controverted by a contemporaneous record of the Nebraska court, which indicated clearly that the rights of defendant were made known to him and that he waived the same; (2) defendant did not sustain the burden of establishing by a fair preponderance of the evidence the averment of his petition, that he was not advised of the right to counsel and did not consciously waive the right of counsel in the Nebraska; and (3) when defendant entered his plea of guilty in the Nebraska proceeding, he was fully aware of the consequences thereof and he was afforded every right to which he was entitled under the United States Constitution.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's application for an order to resentence him as a second felony offender rather than a third felony offender.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- multiple offender sentence -- Nebraska court's record stating that defendant waived his right of counsel is not refuted by defendant's incredible testimony at present hearing held after conviction and sentence in New York as multiple felony offender (cf. Penal Law, § 1943, as amd. by L. 1964, ch. 446); his application for resentence is denied.

Defendant, now 48 years old, has had nine convictions, three of which were for felonies. He now applies to be resentenced as only a second felony offender, on the asserted ground that, 13 years ago at age 35 when he pleaded guilty in Nebraska of a felony, he did not understandingly and knowingly waive his constitutional right to counsel. A full hearing has now been had by this New York court on this issue (see Penal Law, § 1943, as amd. by L. 1964, ch. 446). The Nebraska court's record states that this defendant waived his right to counsel. His present testimony is not sufficiently credible to refute the judgment and document of a court of a sister State. Defendant has had an eighth grade education. He has no language barrier. He knows about assigned counsel; on at least two [***2] of his prior convictions in New York he had the benefit of assigned counsel. Yet, after the present hearing was completed he wrote a letter to this court, denying that he knew that he was being represented by counsel on this hearing -- although he was most effectively represented by experienced Legal Aid Society counsel assigned to him by this court. His application for resentence is denied.

 

2244 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Fire Dep't of Rochester v. Rochester


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


45 Misc. 2d 1097; 258 N.Y.S.2d 691; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1443



September 18, 1964 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff fire department brought an action against defendant city for a declaratory judgment and an injunction against the enforcement of Rochester, N.Y., Local Laws 5, 6 (1963).


OVERVIEW: The fire department had been incorporated by 1864 N.Y. Laws ch. 140 and was authorized by the terms of that act to receive funds derived from a tax upon foreign insurance companies doing business in the city and to administer such funds for the benefit of injured firefighters and others. The challenged ordinances purported to give the treasurer of the city power to collect and administer the taxes imposed by N.Y. Ins. Law §§ 553, 554 upon the foreign insurance companies. The court held that the city did not possess the power to amend or supersede a special incorporating and authorizing act of the state legislature, where that act was not a special act involving the property, affairs, and government of the city. Further, the taxes collected under §§ 553, 554 were state funds and subject to control by the state legislature only. Although matters concerning the organization, operation, and administration of fire departments were local affairs within the meaning of the New York Home Rule Law, the imposition of the tax upon foreign insurance companies was state-wide; hence, the funds derived from the tax were state, and not city, property.


OUTCOME: The court found the challenged ordinances to be void and unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- tax on foreign insurers -- plaintiff, incorporated by chapter 140 of Laws of 1864 and authorized to receive funds derived from tax on foreign insurers is entitled to judgment declaring Local Laws, 1963, Nos. 5 and 6 of City of Rochester, which provide that such funds be received by defendant city, void and unconstitutional; such funds are not city property but State money and plaintiff's authority was created by act of Legislature and cannot be voided by Local Law.

Plaintiff, incorporated by chapter 140 of the Laws of 1864 by the Legislature and authorized to receive funds derived from a tax levied upon foreign insurance corporations (now Insurance Law, §§ 553, 554), even though plaintiff has, since 1879, ceased to engage in fire fighting, is entitled to judgment declaring Local Laws, 1963, Nos. 5 and 6 of the City of Rochester, which provide that such tax moneys be received and managed by defendant, are void and unconstitutional. Chapter 140 is a special act of the Legislature predating the adoption of the City Home Rule Law and does not involve the property, affairs and government of defendant city, and thus subject to change or revocation under [***2] section 11 of the City Home Rule Law. The payment to plaintiff of funds collected pursuant to sections 553 and 554 of the Insurance Law, to the extent that they are consistent with public safety and police power, are matters of primary State concern. The imposition of the tax is State-wide, and the funds derived therefrom must be regarded as State, not city, property.

 

2245 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. La Belle


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Rensselaer County


44 Misc. 2d 324; 253 N.Y.S.2d 897; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1449



September 14, 1964 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] September 24, 1964.



DISPOSITION: Motion denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, charged with first degree murder, filed a motion for a pretrial determination of the voluntariness of a written statement made by him. The court initially denied the motion, and defendant sought reconsideration and a stay of the trial pending an appeal from the dismissal of a writ of habeas corpus.


OVERVIEW: The court initially ruled that defendant was not entitled to a pretrial determination of the voluntariness of the statement because the prosecution had not yet offered to introduce the statement into evidence. Defendant then applied for a writ of habeas corpus, challenging the legality of his detention. On reconsideration of defendant's request for a pretrial determination of voluntariness, the court again opined that a hearing to determine the voluntariness of a statement was premature until such statement was offered. To prevent further delay, however, the court vacated its previous order and scheduled a hearing on the issue of voluntariness.


OUTCOME: The court vacated its previous order that had denied a pretrial determination of the voluntariness of defendant's statement, scheduled a hearing on the issue of voluntariness, and denied defendant's motion for a stay as moot.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- confession -- voluntariness and admissibility of confession must be determined by trial court without jury's knowledge -- proper time for such determination is if and when District Attorney offers to introduce confession in evidence -- but, under circumstances of this case, court will hold pretrial hearing.

1. The voluntariness, if disputed, of a defendant's confession or statement must be determined by the court alone, and the jury must have no inkling as to it. If the court determines that the statement was voluntary, it will be submitted to the jury to determine its contents, weight and credibility; and thus, evidence as to the circumstances surrounding the making of the statement may then be submitted to the jury. ( Jackson v. Denno, 378 U.S. 368, followed.)

2. Ordinarily, there is no reason why the issue of the voluntariness of a confession should be determined pretrial; the proper time for the court to determine that issue is if and when the District Attorney offers to introduce the confession in evidence.

3. However, in this case, the defendants have obtained numerous adjournments of the trial, and have [***2] made many intermediate motions; and the movant defendant has recently applied to a Justice of another court for a writ of habeas corpus on the asserted ground that his detention is illegal while the voluntariness of his confession remains undetermined. That writ has been dismissed, and this defendant asserts he will appeal from that dismissal, and he now seeks to stay the trial of this case. Stay is denied. The court will hold a pretrial hearing to determine the voluntariness of this defendant's statement.

 

2246 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Perlmutter v. Standard Roofing & Tinsmith Supply Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Sullivan County


43 Misc. 2d 885; 252 N.Y.S.2d 583; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1472



September 1, 1964 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the motion to dismiss is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant wholesale distributor, a foreign corporation, filed a motion pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3211(a) to dismiss plaintiff retailer's complaint upon the grounds of lack of jurisdiction of the person of the wholesale distributor. The retailer's underlying action was for breach of contract or breach of warranty.


OVERVIEW: The retailer ordered tile from the middleman. The middleman placed the orders with the wholesale distributor, who then billed the retailer. The retailer received complaints that the tile was defective. The retailer filed its action against the wholesale distributors, who filed a motion to dismiss for lack of personal jurisdiction. The court granted the motion to dismiss. The court held that the wholesale distributor was an out-of-state corporation with no offices in the state, and not authorized to do business in the state. The telephone directory did not contain the wholesale distributor's listing. The wholesale distributor did not maintain any salesmen soliciting business within the state, it had no assets or bank accounts within the state, nor did it own any real estate or personalty within the state. Additionally, it was never part of the original transaction between the retailer and the middleman, and its only function was to bill the retailers in connection with the sale. The court held that to grant personal jurisdiction on the basis of such unsubstantial contact would have offended the traditional notions of justice and fair play.


OUTCOME: The court granted the wholesale distributor's motion to dismiss the retailer's action for breach of contract or breach of warranty because the court did not have personal jurisdiction over the wholesale distributor.


HEADNOTES

Process -- service on nonresidents -- in action for breach of contract, New Jersey corporation served personally with process in New Jersey did not have sufficient contacts with New York State so as to give jurisdiction to courts of New York.

In an action to recover damages for breach of contract, the court failed to acquire jurisdiction of the first defendant by personal service of process upon it in New Jersey. The facts indicate that plaintiffs made a contract with the second defendant who is now out of business and there are insufficient facts to establish any privity of contract between defendants. The sole contact of the first defendant with this State was its billing for the materials to plaintiffs and the limited negotiations regarding the complaints surrounding the alleged defective materials. To grant personal jurisdiction over a foreign corporation on the basis of such unsubstantial contact would offend the traditional notions of justice and fair play. The complaint is dismissed.

Wholesale Laundry Board of Trade, Inc. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


43 Misc. 2d 816; 252 N.Y.S.2d 502; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1482; 50 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P51,160



August 25, 1964 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff trade board filed a cause of action against defendant city seeking a judicial declaration that Local Law No. 45 of the year 1964 (Law), which fixed a minimum wage of $ 1.50 per hour was unconstitutional. Thereafter, the trade board filed a motion for summary judgment or, in the alternative, for judgment declaring the Law unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: In opposition to the trade board's motion for summary judgment, the city contended that the prior constitutional provision prohibited local laws inconsistent with the laws of the state, whereas the amendment prohibited local laws inconsistent with any general law. The city also argued that the State Minimum Wage Act was not a "general law" pursuant to N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 3 (d)(1), because the Industrial Commissioner was authorized to establish a different minimum wage rate for different localities in respect to particular industries. The court granted the motion and held that the Law was a "general law" because the provisions for a minimum wage up to $ 1.25 per hour and the establishment by the Commissioner of a higher minimum wage for particular occupations, varying with localities, applied alike to all cities and counties. The court held that there was nothing in the new amendment to the New York Constitution, which authorized any departure from the appellate court's decision that the Law was unconstitutional. The court then held that the Law violated the constitutional prohibition against local laws inconsistent with "general laws" of the state on the same subject.


OUTCOME: The court granted the trade board's motion for summary judgment in the trade board's action, which sought a judicial declaration that the city Law was unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- minimum wage law -- local law of City of New York imposing minimum hourly wage of $ 1.50 is inconsistent with State Minimum Wage Act and is unconstitutional.

A local law of the City of New York imposing a minimum wage of $ 1.50 per hour is inconsistent with the State Minimum Wage Act (Labor Law, §§ 650-665) which provides a minimum wage of $ 1.15 per hour until October 15, 1964 and is unconstitutional. A change in article IX of the Constitution effective January 1, 1964 so that local laws could not be inconsistent with "any general law" as compared with a prior provision prohibiting inconsistency with the "laws of the State" does not render the local law constitutional on the theory that the State Minimum Wage Act is not a general law.

State Comm'n for Human Rights v. Farrell


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


43 Misc. 2d 958; 252 N.Y.S.2d 649; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1486; 57 L.R.R.M. 2005; 1 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 100; 50 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P51,162; 1 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P9697



August 24, 1964 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent union and respondent employer members sought review of the order of petitioner State Commission for Human Rights (New York) (commission), which determined that the union and the employer members denied or withheld the right to participate in the sheet metal apprentice program from qualified Negroes and other minority groups.


OVERVIEW: The commission sought enforcement of its order, which held that the union and the employer members denied qualified minority groups the right to be admitted to and participate in the sheet metal apprentice program solely because of race and color. The order further held that the employer members aided and abetted the union in denying qualified Negroes the right to enroll in the program. The court affirmed the commission's findings, holding that no provision existed safeguarding any applicant against discrimination because of race, color, creed, or national origin and that there was no provision entitling an applicant to seek review of a rejection of his application for training. The court held that the provision in the apprenticeship-training program for preference or credit because of filial relation was illegal and unconstitutional because it was contrary to modern day societal objectives concerning job qualifications. The court held that admission to a profession or industry based exclusively upon the applicant's qualifications to perform in the profession or industry as determined by objective criteria was to be encouraged.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the commission with the exception of finding No. 66, which was modified.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Civil rights -- discrimination in apprentice training -- in proceeding to enforce order affecting employment practices in sheet metal industry, findings of State Commission for Human Rights are supported by evidence and are conclusive -- enforcement is ordered on basis of plan for training apprentices containing objective standards that are nondiscriminatory -- findings against individual respondents based on prior conduct and past acts are not approved and are stricken.

1. In a proceeding to enforce an order affecting employment practices in the sheet metal industry, the findings of the State Commission for Human Rights to the effect that respondent union and the Joint Apprenticeship Committee denied to qualified Negroes and other minority groups the right to participate in an apprentice program because of race and color, that the union never had a Negro apprentice, that 80% of the trainees were related to the members of the union, and that admission to apprentice training was not based on objective standards are supported by substantial evidence and are conclusive.

2. Enforcement of the order is by a plan for training apprentices (Appendix "A"), containing standards approved [***2] by the court. The plan is enlightened, progressive and in accordance with the principles of nondiscrimination, equality of opportunity and on the basis of qualification alone under objective standards.

3. Individual respondents, representatives of employers and labor, acted both in their individual and in their representative capacities. The employer members received no compensation for their services to the Joint Apprenticeship Committee. The findings of the commission against the individuals as to their prior conduct and past acts are not supported by evidence and are stricken from the order.

 

2249 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Landers v. O'Connor


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Queens County


43 Misc. 2d 1040; 252 N.Y.S.2d 766; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1499



August 17, 1964 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Application is in all respects denied, stay vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In the trial court (New York), petitioner lawyers sought an order prohibiting the use, projection, and submission of alleged wire-tap evidence in a criminal matter pending before a grand jury in which their clients were witnesses before the grand jury.


OVERVIEW: The lawyers represented their clients, who were witnesses before the grand jury in a criminal matter. Certain evidence was to have been presented to the grand jury that was obtained from an illegal telephone interception. The lawyers filed a petition seeking to prohibit the use and submission of that evidence. The court denied the motion and held that intercepted telephone conversations were not illegal or inadmissible in evidence in the New York courts when they were secured pursuant to the provisions of N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 813-a and that the district attorney was not prohibited from presenting that legal evidence to the grand jury. The court found that it was not prepared to disregard the well-established precedent that § 605 of the Federal Communications Act was not so all-inclusive as to have prohibited the use of evidence in a state court or a duly constituted grand jury that was secured by an order allowing a wire tap.


OUTCOME: The court denied the lawyers' petition and vacated the stay.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- wire-tap evidence -- telephone conversations, intercepted by wire-tap order, are admissible in courts of New York.

Telephone conversations, intercepted by a wire-tap order pursuant to section 813-a of the Code of Criminal Procedure, are not illegal or inadmissible in the courts of this State and an application to prohibit a District Attorney from presenting such legal evidence to a Grand Jury is denied.

 

2250 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Board of Education v. Board of Education


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


43 Misc. 2d 803; 252 N.Y.S.2d 384; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1510



August 12, 1964 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motions of the plaintiffs for summary judgment in these actions are granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a board of education and others, moved for summary judgment in two actions for declaratory relief seeking a determination that Rochester, N.Y., Local Law No. 8 (May 28, 1963) was unconstitutional and further seeking a permanent injunction against defendant, the Board of Education of the City of Rochester, because of threatened acts pursuant to Local Law No. 8.


OVERVIEW: The legislature enacted 1915 N.Y. Laws ch. 359, which provided for the physical annexation by the City of certain areas. The enactment directed the Rochester Board to receive children residing in the unannexed portion of the school district without payment of tuition. Many years later, the City adopted Rochester, N.Y., Local Law No. 8 (May 28, 1963), which required payment of tuition by the children residing in the unannexed areas. Plaintiffs sought a declaration that Local Law No. 8 was invalid. Plaintiffs moved for summary judgment, which the court granted. The court held that the annexation statutes were a valid exercise of the legislature's powers. The court further held that Local Law No. 8 was unconstitutional. The court noted that public education was a state and not a city function. In addition, a board of education was a separate corporate body and the property, affairs, or government of a city did not include the board of education. Moreover, the New York Constitution expressly excluded from the power granted to cities to adopt local laws, any enactment that applied to or affected the administration of the public school systems in such cities.


OUTCOME: The court granted plaintiffs' motions for summary judgment.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- annexation of school districts to city -- by legislative enactments portions of towns and school districts were annexed to City of Rochester, legislation providing that Rochester school system receive children residing in certain districts without payment of tuition; such statutes are constitutional -- local law of City of Rochester to provide for education of such children on payment of tuition is not authorized by City Home Rule Law since city cannot make enactment affecting Board of Education of City of Rochester which is separate corporate body -- local law is unconstitutional and void.

1. Chapter 359 of the Laws of 1915, by which there was annexed to the City of Rochester various school districts, provided that the Board of Education of the City of Rochester receive children residing in a portion of a school district which was not annexed without payment of tuition. Chapter 547 of the Laws of 1918 provided for annexation of other lands and provided that free tuition rights be extended to certain other school districts. Such annexation statutes are a valid exercise of legislative [***2] power and are constitutional.

2. By local law to become effective September 1, 1964, the City of Rochester provided that students from such districts attend Rochester schools "upon the payment of tuition". Subdivision 1 of section 11 of the City Home Rule Law which authorizes the local legislative body of a city to amend local laws in relation to the property, affairs or government "of the city" does not warrant such enactment since the Board of Education is a separate corporate body and the property, affairs or government "of the city" does not include the Board of Education.

3. The action of the city in enacting the local law is unconstitutional and its attempt to amend the legislative enactments is void.

 

2251 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Martin v. State Liquor Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


43 Misc. 2d 682; 252 N.Y.S.2d 365; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1528



July 31, 1964 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Plaintiffs' application for a preliminary injunction is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff liquor store owners applied for a preliminary injunction in their suit against defendant New York State Liquor Authority for a declaratory judgment that 1964 N.Y. Laws 531, §§ 13, 14 were invalid. The Authority moved to dismiss the complaint.


OVERVIEW: 1964 N.Y. Laws 531, §§ 13, 14 repealed a prior law that denied liquor licenses where liquor stores were already located nearby. Denying the injunction and entering a judgment for the Authority, the court said that 1964 N.Y. Laws 531, §§ 13, 14 were constitutional. The Authority determined whether the public convenience and advantage was promoted by the issuance of licenses and the location of licensees under N.Y. Alco. Bev. Cont. Law § 2, 1934 N.Y. Laws 478. The Authority exercised police power in the regulation of liquor traffic. Liquor licenses were not contracts between New York and the licensee such as to give the latter vested rights and so as to be protected against subsequent legislation. No person had a vested interest in any law or legislative policy that entitled the person to have it remain unaltered for the person's benefit. The power to enact necessarily implied the power to repeal and one legislature could not have been limited or bound by the actions of a previous one.


OUTCOME: The court denied the preliminary injunction, denied the motion to dismiss, and directed a judgment for the Authority.


HEADNOTES

Intoxicating liquors -- location of retail package liquor stores -- delegation of legislative power to locate retail liquor package stores on basis of standard of "public convenience and advantage" as contained in sections 13 and 14 of chapter 531 of Laws of 1964, which repealed subdivisions 4 and 4-a of section 105 of Alcoholic Beverage Control Law is constitutional -- holders of licenses have no vested right to protection against subsequent legislation -- judgment directed for defendant declaring sections 13 and 14 constitutional despite defendant's motion to dismiss complaint.

1. The delegation of legislative power to license retail package liquor stores to the State Liquor Authority under sections 13 and 14 of chapter 531 of the Laws of 1964 which repealed subdivisions 4 and 4-a of section 105 of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law and reaffirmed the power of the State Liquor Authority to determine "whether public convenience and advantage will be promoted" by the issuance of retail liquor package store licenses without other guides as to where such stores shall be located contains a sufficient guiding [***2] standard as to make such delegation of power constitutional. The standard "public convenience and advantage" is specific and clear and is not so vague and indefinite as to be a mere general reference to public welfare. This criterion is related to the main purposes of the law stated to be "fostering and promoting temperance" in the consumption of alcoholic beverages and "respect for and obedience to law".

2. Plaintiffs, holders of retail package store licenses, have no vested property rights and are not protected from subsequent legislation.

3. Where the material allegations of the complaint are admitted and no questions of fact are presented in an action for declaratory judgment respecting the constitutionality of a statute, declaratory judgment may properly be directed. Accordingly, defendant's motion to dismiss the complaint is denied and judgment is directed in favor of defendant declaring sections 13 and 14 of chapter 531 of the Laws of 1964 constitutional and valid.

 

2252 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cassese


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Westchester County


43 Misc. 2d 869; 251 N.Y.S.2d 540; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1577



July 10, 1964 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The judgment of conviction is reversed on the law and the facts and the fine of $ 25 is remitted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the order of the Court of Special Sessions of the City of Yonkers (New York), which convicted him of operating a taxicab without a proper license in violation of Yonkers, N.Y., Ord. No. 16-1951 (1962)


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed that he operated a private limousine service from an office located on private property. He was convicted of violating Yonkers, N.Y., Ord. No. 16-1951 (1962). The court reversed and held that Ord. 16-1951 was unconstitutional to the extent that it mandated that a private rental vehicle be equipped with all of the equipment required on vehicles commonly recognized as "taxicabs." Ord. 16-1951 effectively destroyed the legitimate business of private car rental. Ord. 16-1951 was not intended to define a private rental car as a taxicab. Yonkers, N.Y., Gen. Ord. No. 1-1962, art. II, § 9 (1962) provided that operators of any vehicle or taxicab used for private or public rental were to register in the office of the commissioner. Therefore, there was a distinction between private rental vehicles and taxicabs. A literal enforcement of Yonkers, N.Y., Ord. No. 16-1951 (1962) would abolish defendant's private car rental business. Defendant's business did not contain any of the equipment required of a taxicab.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that convicted defendant of operating a taxicab without a proper license.


HEADNOTES

Licenses -- taxicabs -- defendant operator of private limousine service not guilty of violating ordinance of city requiring taxicabs to be licensed -- private car rental business is lawful -- to extent that ordinance mandates that private rental vehicles be equipped with taximeter, domelights, etc., it is unconstitutional.

1. Defendant, the operator of a private limousine service within the City of Yonkers, who parked his vehicle on private property when not in use, whose vehicle was not equipped with a meter or lights, who did not cruise the streets, and who responded only to a request for service received by telephone, was not guilty of violating an ordinance of the City of Yonkers requiring operators of taxicabs to be licensed, despite the broad definition of "taxicab" in the ordinance as to include private rental automobiles.

2. The private car rental business is a lawful business which cannot be made unlawful by the definition of "taxicab" in the ordinance.

3. Insofar as the ordinance mandates that a private rental vehicle be equipped with a taximeter, domelight, medallion [***2] and other equipment, it destroys the business of private car rental and is unconstitutional.

 

2253 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Ferdon v. Rogers


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


43 Misc. 2d 676; 252 N.Y.S.2d 1; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1606



July 3, 1964 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the petition is denied and the proceeding dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayers brought the N.Y.C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding to compel respondent members of the Board of Trustees of the Village of Scarsdale, N.Y., to conduct a referendum regarding Scarsdale, N.Y., Local Law No. 1 (1963) or, in the alternative, to declare that provision and 1963 N.Y. Laws ch. 897 unconstitutional. The taxpayers also asked the court to require the Board to continue using certain lands as parks.


OVERVIEW: The Board developed plans to convert a village-owned park into a parking lot. The New York legislature passed enabling legislation, 1963 N.Y. Law ch. 897, authorizing the change in the use of the land "by ordinance or local law." The Board then enacted Scarsdale, N.Y., Local Law No. 1 (1963), which discontinued the dedication of the land as a park. The taxpayers then requested a referendum on the issue and filed the petition after the request was denied. The court rejected the taxpayers' claim that N.Y. Village Home Rule Law § 17 subd. 6 (current version at N.Y. Mun. Home Rule Law § 24) required a referendum; discontinuance of the land as a park did not constitute an alienation of village property. The court also held that the local ordinance and the enabling legislation were not unconstitutional. The Board complied with N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 16, as well as New York Village Home Rule Law § 22 (current version at N.Y. Mun. Home Rule Law § 40). The mayor's statement that the village did not have the authority under local law to change the land's use was sufficient to establish the necessity for the enabling legislation, and that legislative determination was not reviewable.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petition and dismissed the proceeding.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- referedum -- local law discontinuing use of land as park land is not subject to referendum since land was not alienated -- enabling act of Legislature authorizing village to discontinue use of land as park land is not unconstitutional; Mayor's request for legislation recited fact of necessity but in any event whether facts established necessity is not subject to court review.

1. A local law discontinuing the use as park land of a lot owned by a village is not an "alienation * * * of village property" which would subject the local law to referendum on petition under subdivision 6 of section 17 of the Village Home Rule Law. The local law has not affected ownership, possession or control of the lot.

2. The enabling act of the Legislature (L. 1963, ch. 897), which authorized the village to discontinue use of the lot as park land, was not unconstitutional. The Mayor's request for passage was made on the ground that the village lacked power to enact such legislation. Such fact established necessity. Moreover, whether the facts established necessity is not subject to review by the [***2] courts because of section 22 of the Village Home Rule Law and section 57 of the Legislative Law.

 

2254 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Sweeney v. Cannon


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


43 Misc. 2d 435; 251 N.Y.S.2d 146; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1625



June 26, 1964 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly defendant's motion is denied, and summary judgment granted in favor of plaintiff.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff class representative attacked 1963 N.Y. Laws ch. 204, § 467-a, which required attorneys who had been admitted to practice before September 1, 1963 to file a certificate of registration and pay a fee. Defendant official filed a N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3211 motion to dismiss. Construing the motion as one for summary judgment, art. 3211(c), for which no cross motion was necessary, art. 3211(b), the class representative sought summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: Attorneys admitted before September 1963 had to register and pay a $ 15 fee. The class representative attacked the law on grounds of equal protection. The official sought dismissal on grounds that the complaint failed to state a cause of action and that no justiciable controversy existed that could be made the subject of a declaratory judgment. The court denied the official's motion to dismiss. The law was invalid under the equal protection clauses of U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1, and N.Y. Const. art. I. § 11. The law discriminated in favor of lawyers admitted after September 1, 1963. Classifications that bore some reasonable relation to the object sought to be accomplished were a permissible discrimination. To justify a cutoff date, the inherent nature of the activity had to call for, or at least permit, a cutoff. But if the purpose was to register qualified attorneys, there was no rhyme or reason for a cutoff date. The classification was plainly arbitrary and unreasonable. The absence of a penalty did not render the law innocuous. Attorneys had to obey valid laws whether they carried any sting or not and were subject to disciplinary proceedings for ignoring them.


OUTCOME: The court denied the official's motion to dismiss and granted summary judgment in favor of the class representative.


HEADNOTES

Attorneys -- registration -- statute (Judiciary Law, § 467-a) requiring attorneys admitted to practice on September 1, 1963 to file certificate of registration and pay $ 15 fee unconstitutionally denies equal protection of laws to them; no reasonable basis for discrimination in favor of attorneys admitted after September 1, 1963 -- justiciable issue is presented despite absence of penalty in statute.

1. Section 467-a of the Judiciary Law provides that every attorney licensed and admitted to practice on September 1, 1963 shall file a certificate of registration with the Clerk of the Court of Appeals, which certificate shall be accompanied by a fee of $ 15. Such section denies the equal protection of the law to lawyers admitted prior to September 1, 1963 and discriminates in favor of those admitted after September 1, 1963. Such classification bears no reasonable relation to the object sought to be accomplished. If the purpose is to register qualified attorneys, there is no reason for the cutoff date of September 1, 1963. The cutoff date prefers some members of a class at the [***2] expense of others of the same class.

2. A justiciable issue is presented. Despite the absence of a penalty in section 467-a, plaintiff's duty as an attorney is to obey valid laws and he is liable to disciplinary proceedings for ignoring them. The passage of the deadline for registration has not rendered the threat any less real or destroyed the genuineness of the controversy.

 

2255 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Mohawk Containers, Inc. v. Hancock


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Oneida County


43 Misc. 2d 716; 252 N.Y.S.2d 148; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1627



June 26, 1964 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the motion of the plaintiff is denied and the complaint is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporate landowner sought to extinguish, pursuant to N.Y. Real Prop. Acts. Law § 1951, a restrictive covenant held by defendant residential landowners against industrial use of land between the properties.


OVERVIEW: The corporate landowner owned two parcels of land. The land on which its factory stood had been reclassified as industrially zoned, but the land behind it had not been rezoned and remained residentially zoned. The residential landowners owned land behind the corporate landowner's property, adjacent to the residentially zoned land. The corporate landowner filed a complaint for a declaratory judgment, seeking extinguishment of restrictive covenants placed on it when its land had been reclassified as industrially zoned. The court dismissed the complaint. The court held that there were no changed circumstances that would have permitted the extinguishment of the restrictive covenants and that the interest of the residential landowners in preserving the value of their property was a benefit to the residential landowners so as to preserve the restrictive covenant. The court concluded that the interest of the residential landowners was not unconscionable or oppressive as to justify the extinguishment of the covenant.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the complaint by the corporate landowner against the residential landowners.


HEADNOTES

Deeds -- restrictive covenants -- in action to extinguish restriction limiting use of plaintiff's land to one-family residences, defendants, home owners, are granted judgment; restriction not rendered obsolete; character of area has not changed even though village changed zone of plaintiff's property to permit industrial use.

In an action under section 1951 of the Real Property Actions and Proceedings Law for a declaratory judgment to extinguish a restriction on the use of plaintiff's land that limits it to single-family residences, defendants, owners of one-family homes who sincerely believe that relinquishment of the restriction will cause them financial loss, are entitled to judgment adhering to the restriction which is valid and binding, even though some of their neighbors are willing to modify it. It is immaterial that the village changed the zone of plaintiff's property permitting industrial use thereof. There has been no change in the character of the neighborhood so as to render enforcement of the restriction ineffective.

 

2256 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Wood v. Freeman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


43 Misc. 2d 616; 251 N.Y.S.2d 996; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1647



June 19, 1964 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Since it is the general rule that a statute is not to be overthrown by anything short of a positive conviction of illegality nor held unconstitutional in the absence of cogent, clear and compelling reasons, petitioners' application for such relief is denied herein, as they have failed to show that they have been deprived of property without due process of law because of lack of provision in section 179-b that notice of hearing be given to them by the Board of Appeals.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landowners filed an action pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 seeking to reverse the decisions of respondents, the members of a village board of appeals, to permit a corporation to operate a golf course on land contiguous to that of the landowners and denying the landowners' application to revoke such use permit or to hold a rehearing on the golf course's use permit.


OVERVIEW: A corporation was granted a permit to operate a golf course as an increased use under a prior zoning variance. The landowners, who were abutters of the golf course land, filed an application to have the use permit revoked or to have a public hearing on the permit. The board of appeals denied the application and the landowners sought review. Without reaching the merits of the landowners' claim, the court dismissed the action on the grounds that the landowners, by virtue of the fact that they were not residents or property owners of the village in which the golf course was located, were not "aggrieved" persons under N.Y. Village Law § 179-b and therefore had no status to challenge the decision of the board of appeals of the village. In so holding, the court determined that § 179-b did not violate the landowners' right to due process because the permit did not deny the landowners the use and enjoyment of their property in any way requiring notice and opportunity for a hearing. The court reasoned that the landowners had a remedy against the golf course in case of any interference with their use and enjoyment of their properties arising from the operation of the golf course.


OUTCOME: The court denied the landowners' petition for relief.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- variance for construction of golf course -- grant of variance by Village Board of Appeals to construct golf course on property on village boundary cannot be challenged by contiguous homeowners who are not residents of village since they are not aggrieved persons under Village Law ( § 179-b) -- even if they were aggrieved persons, four-month Statute of Limitations bars proceeding -- section 179-b not unconstitutional; failure of petitioners to receive notice of hearing, not provided for by section, did not deprive them of use and enjoyment of property -- if operation of golf course interferes with enjoyment, they can assert claim against owner of golf course.

1.
