The court was charged with judicial knowledge and notice of the fact that defendant's written confession was not "legal evidence." The court found that the fact that sufficient testimony was adduced on the trial to warrant defendant's conviction did not validate an indictment, which would be good if such additional proof had been presented to the grand jury. Defendant had a constitutional right to have an indictment found against him based only upon legal evidence constitutionally obtained. The court of appeals ruled that the evidence used against him, and which was the sole basis for his indictment by the grand jury, was unconstitutionally obtained from him.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the indictment and withheld signing an order for a period of 15 days to afford the district attorney an opportunity to present the case to another grand jury.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- indictment -- indictment founded on illegal confession is dismissed even though on trial evidence was sufficient to warrant conviction apart from confession -- court has power to entertain motion to set aside indictment.

1. An indictment, founded upon a written confession, held, after trial and appeal, to be inadmissible, must be dismissed even though sufficient evidence was adduced on the trial, apart from the confession, to warrant defendant's conviction. The trial testimony does not validate said indictment.

2. A strict construction of section 251 of the Code of Criminal Procedure, which provides that the Grand Jury ought to find an indictment when all the evidence before them, unexplained or uncontradicted, warrants a conviction, would necessitate holding the indictment sufficient because nothing indicated that the confession was inadmissible. However, section 251 must be construed with section 249 of the Code of Criminal Procedure which provides that a Grand Jury can receive only "legal evidence". Accordingly, protection of the rights of moving defendant requires liberal construction. Moreover, irrespective of the statute, the court has inherent power to [***2] entertain a motion to set aside an indictment based on insufficient evidence.

 

2279 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Galloway v. Saletan


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


42 Misc. 2d 458; 248 N.Y.S.2d 299; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2033



February 26, 1964 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, petitioner sought a judgment to restrain respondent, the school board of trustees, from holding a special meeting of the voters of the school district to vote upon a resolution for the addition, expansion, and remodeling of a school.


OVERVIEW: The board submitted a resolution concerning an improvement to a school to the voters for approval. It was rejected by a small margin. Shortly thereafter, the board submitted a resolution for the resubmission of the same proposition to the voters at a special meeting within a month and a half after the prior losing vote. Petitioner argued that the adoption of the resolution by the board was an abuse of discretion, and that the proper method for resubmission was in accordance with N.Y. Educ. Law § 2008. The board contended that it had the authority to call the meeting pursuant to N.Y. Educ. Law § 2007 and that petitioner failed to exhaust the administrative remedies. The court dismissed the petition, finding that no statute was violated. There was no provision in § 2007 to the effect that if a proposal by the board was once submitted and disapproved that it could not again be submitted by the board absent a finding of fraud, nor was there any statutory restriction as to the interval of time which was required to elapse before a matter was resubmitted. The act of the board in determining to resubmit the proposal to the voters was discretionary under § 2007.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petition.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- elections -- act of board of school district in determining to resubmit defeated proposal to electorate is discretionary -- no statute violated thereby; petitioner has remedy by appeal to State Commissioner of Education; proceeding under article 78 of Civil Practice Law and Rules does not lie.

1. Section 2007 of the Education Law, which authorizes a school district to call a special meeting whenever necessary, contains no provision that if a proposal by the board of education thereof is once submitted and disapproved that it may not again be submitted by the board absent a finding of fraud, error or misunderstanding in connection with the original submission, nor is there any statutory restriction as to the interval of time which must elapse before a matter is resubmitted. The act of respondent board in determining to resubmit a defeated proposal within a month and a half is discretionary.

2. Since no statute was thereby violated and an administrative determination is involved which may be reviewed on appeal by the State Commissioner of Education, this proceeding under article 78 of the Civil Practice Law and Rules (7801, subd. 1) does [***2] not lie.

 

2280 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Klipp v. New York State Civil Service Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Suffolk County


42 Misc. 2d 35; 247 N.Y.S.2d 632; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2052



February 21, 1964 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] An article 78 proceeding in the nature of mandamus is the proper remedy for the petitioners and their petition is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a county clerk and others, brought a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding seeking an order directing respondent New York State Civil Service Commission to approve an amendment to the Rules and Appendices of the Suffolk County, N.Y., Civil Service Commission placing three deputy county clerks in the exempt class.


OVERVIEW: The county board of supervisors created three positions of deputy county clerk to be part of the exempt class of the civil service under N.Y. County Law § 526(3) and eliminated three deputy county clerk positions from the competitive class. The Commission rejected the proposal, so petitioners sought an order directing the Commission to make the change. The court granted the petition, holding that the board of supervisors had authority to exempt the positions and that the Commission was required to approve the action. The court noted that N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 41(1)(b) provided that the deputies of principal executive officers authorized by law to act generally for and in place of their principals were exempt positions and that N.Y. County Law § 526(3) stated that the county clerk could appoint deputies authorized to act generally for and in the place of their principal. The court held that by its use of the identical language the legislature intended that the positions authorized by § 526(3) were to be classified as exempt. The court held that the Commission was required by N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 20(2) to amend its rules and appendices to conform them to the statutory change.


OUTCOME: The court granted the petition.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- classification -- exempt positions -- by Legislature's use, in subdivision 3 of section 526 of County Law, of same phrase found in Civil Service Law ( § 41) defining positions in exempt class, it intended that positions of Deputy County Clerk when newly created by Board of Supervisors be in exempt class -- legislative power to classify positions in exempt class was here reasonably exercised by delegating legislative power to determine number of positions to Board of Supervisors -- neither State nor County Civil Service Commission had power to veto legislative action of Board of Supervisors -- that one petitioner occupies competitive position of Deputy County Clerk as provisional does not affect result.

1. Subdivision 3 of section 526 of the County Law (as added by L. 1962, ch. 626) empowered a County Clerk to appoint and remove such additional deputies "authorized to act generally for and in the place of their principal" as shall be authorized by resolution of the Board of Supervisors. Acting pursuant thereto, the Board of Supervisors [***2] of Suffolk County by resolution created three positions of Deputy County Clerk, all in the exempt class and abolished three positions with similar titles in the competitive class. Section 41 of the Civil Service Law listed, as positions in the exempt class, the deputies of principal executive officers "authorized by law to act generally for and in place of their principals". By the Legislature's use of the quoted language in subdivision 3 of section 526 of the County Law, it intended that the positions be exempt.

2. In this case, it was within the power of the Legislature to delegate to the Board of Supervisors the power to legislate as to the number of positions of Deputy County Clerk, the county might require. What was done here was a reasonable exercise of the legislative power to classify positions under the civil service and one which does not violate the Constitution.

3. Neither the State Civil Service Commission nor the Suffolk County Civil Service Commission had any discretionary power to veto the authorized legislative action of the Board of Supervisors.

4. That one petitioner has, under a provisional appointment, occupied a position with the title of Deputy County Clerk [***3] in the competitive class does not affect the result. Nor is it significant that the competitive position is to be abolished and that he, without taking a qualifying examination, may be appointed to the new position. The legislative classification prevails and has removed his position from the control of the Civil Service Commission.

 

2281 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Moore v. State


Claim Nos. 37974, 37975


Court of Claims of New York


42 Misc. 2d 314; 248 N.Y.S.2d 18; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2076



February 18, 1964 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff infants filed lawsuits against defendant state to recover for personal injuries sustained by them while playing in an abandoned building owned by the state.


OVERVIEW: The infants were injured while playing within the state's abandoned building. The court granted judgment for the state in the infants' personal injury lawsuit. The court held that the infants were trespassers as a matter of law. Although the state might have known that persons including children were entering onto the property, such knowledge did not constitute an invitation to such persons to so continue. The state had a common-law duty to infant trespassers to refrain from inflicting willful, wanton, or intentional injuries and not to create a trap or maintain an inherently dangerous article without the exercise of a high degree of care to prevent foreseeable injury to others. The court concluded that the evidence did not warrant the imposition of liability upon the state. The injuries sustained by the infants were caused by nonstructural defects, and there was no proof of any willful, wanton, or intentional acts on the state's part. There was evidence to show the state's attempts to keep the building closed. Furthermore, the state governed its municipalities and a municipality could not encroach directly or indirectly upon the sovereign powers of the state.


OUTCOME: The court granted judgment for the state in the infants' lawsuit to recover for personal injuries sustained by them while playing in an abandoned building owned by the state.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] State -- maintenance of premises -- infant claimants entering State-owned abandoned building were trespassers -- State not liable for injuries sustained by infants; injuries not caused by structural defects or by willful, wanton or intentional acts of State.

1. Each infant claimant, who entered an abandoned building within the City of New York owned by defendant, the front of the premises being boarded up, was a trespasser as a matter of law. While defendant may have known that children were entering the premises, such knowledge was not an invitation to such persons to continue.

2. The State is not liable for the injuries sustained by claimants. Their injuries were caused by nonstructural defects for which no liability may be imposed on defendant, and there was no proof of any willful, wanton or intentional acts on defendant's part. To the contrary the evidence shows defendant's attempts to keep the building closed.

 

2282 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Chase v. Glen Cove


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial and Special Term, Nassau County


41 Misc. 2d 889; 246 N.Y.S.2d 975; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2157



January 28, 1964 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owners filed a declaratory judgment action against defendants, the city and others, seeking a declaration on the validity of an amendment to a zoning ordinance, which created a high rise housing district. The owners had alleged that the amendment was not in accordance with a comprehensive or a well-considered plan, that it was confiscatory, and that it was enacted pursuant to defective procedures.


OVERVIEW: A city amended a building zone ordinance, rezoning an area to create a municipal high rise housing district. The owners of rezoned parcels and of parcels adjacent to the rezoned area filed a declaratory judgment action against defendants alleging that the amendment was not in accordance with a well-considered plan, that it was confiscatory, and that it was enacted under defective procedures. The court held that: (1) while inconsistency existed, in that the height and the population density of the proposed use did not conform to restrictions elsewhere in the city, inconsistency alone did not show that a plan was not well considered, and because the rezoning was for a general welfare purpose, the owners had, but failed, to convincingly demonstrate irrationality; (2) the amendment was confiscatory because the owners were deprived of any beneficial use of their land, as the district created allowed only a single use, for municipal housing; and (3) the property description in the notice of hearing setting forth the nature of the change provided insufficient notice because the description did not give an average reader reasonable warning that his property might be affected.


OUTCOME: The court entered a judgment declaring that the amendment to the zoning ordinance was invalidly adopted.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- zoning -- zoning amendment creating Municipal High Rise Housing District in which only that one use is allowed is confiscatory, since condemnation and just compensation are postponed indefinitely -- moreover, notice of hearing and zone amendment described zone erroneously -- but project is comprehensive and well considered and promotes general welfare, and errors can be corrected by Common Council.

1. An amendment to the City of Glen Cove Building Zone Ordinance, creating a Municipal High Rise Housing District, is adjudged invalid because confiscatory and because not properly enacted.

2. The ordinance allows only one use in the district it creates: municipal housing. The owners are thus deprived of any beneficial use of their property -- the classic example of an unconstitutional taking. In such a situation, the city must provide for condemnation and just compensation now, not at some indefinite future time.

3. The published notice of hearing before the Planning Board described the proposed zone so erroneously as to make it impossible to tell whether certain lots -- lots 563, 589, 580, 27 and 47 -- were or were not included. Some owners were thus [***2] unaware that their properties were to be rezoned and they did not attend the hearing. It is immaterial that the Planning Board recommended disapproval of the rezoning; the City Council overruled the board. And the ordinance, as finally adopted, contained practically the same erroneous description of the zone.

4. However, a public housing project is clearly in furtherance of the general welfare (N. Y. Const., art. XVIII; Public Housing Law, § 2). The proposed height (8 stories) and population density (40 apartments per acre) contrast with the restrictions elsewhere in the city (height 3 stories; population density 16 apartments per acre); but the General City Law ( § 20, subd. 24) provides that "the regulations in one or more districts may differ from those in other districts." The evidence shows, and the court's view confirms, that the rezoned property fronts on an arterial highway and that the land behind it rises sharply, cutting it off from residentially zoned properties above and behind it. It has not been shown that governmental or commercial facilities will be overburdened by the increased population density or that the increased height will overburden facilities or constitute [***3] an eyesore. The whole plan is comprehensive and well considered. It will be delayed because of the above-stated errors, which, however, can be corrected by the Common Council.

 

2283 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Froslid v. Hults


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Queens County


41 Misc. 2d 570; 246 N.Y.S.2d 899; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2158



January 27, 1964 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The relief sought with respect to the 1965 registration plates is denied, since that would require the court to look into the proverbial crystal ball and speculate as to what the provisions of the law pursuant to registration plates will be at that time.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner licensee filed an action under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 to review a decision respondent commissioner of the department of motor vehicles. The licensee sought, in the nature of a writ of mandamus, an order directing the commissioner to permit him and others similarly-situated to obliterate or to cover over a certain inscription on the metal license plates to be issued to him, upon the registration of his vehicles, by the commissioner.


OVERVIEW: A licensee owned motor vehicles and a trailer, which were required to be registered with and to bear metal registration plates issued by the commissioner. The plates issued had the inscription "World's Fair," referencing the forthcoming World's Fair, which was operated by a private nonprofit corporation. The licensee filed an action under art. 78 for an order to compel the commissioner to permit him to obliterate or to cover over the inscription, alleging that the issuance of such plates was unconstitutional and that it compelled him to use private property for advertising purposes on behalf of a private corporation, without compensation. The court held that: (1) although the commissioner had discretion, which should only be exercised in consistency with the purpose of the plates, with respect to the design of the plates, he had no authority to include the inscription "World's Fair" thereon or to issue plates containing such inscription; and (2) the licensee and other vehicle owners, who were required to register their vehicle in New York, were legally entitled to the issuance of a proper registration plate, and the commissioner was required to issue such a plate.


OUTCOME: The court granted the licensee's petition for mandamus relief to the extent of requiring the commissioner to issue to the licensee a registration plate containing distinctive numbers to be assigned to the licensee's vehicles.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- advertising on registration plates -- Commissioner of Motor Vehicles lacks power to advertise "World's Fair", private corporation, on motor vehicle registration plates for 1964 -- proceeding to compel issuance of plates without advertising is not class or taxpayer's action -- no relief given with respect to 1965 registration plates.

1. Subdivision 2 of section 402 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law empowers the Commissioner of Motor Vehicles to prescribe the material, form, design and dimensions of registration plates for motor vehicles and to set forth a distinguishing number or other identification mark. Such grant does not give the Commissioner power to prescribe that such plates for 1964 contain the inscription "World's Fair" or to issue such plates. "New York World's Fair 1964/1965, Inc.," is a private, nonprofit corporation. Matter advertising the World's Fair or any other private corporation in [***2] no way serves to further the intention of the law requiring display of registration plates. Petitioner is entitled to an order of mandamus directing the Commissioner to issue to him, upon application, registration plates with distinctive numbers but without the inscription "World's Fair" or plates from which said inscription shall have been obliterated.

2. This proceeding is neither a class action nor a taxpayer's action, although there are allegations in the complaint which may be compatible with either or both.

3. No relief is given with respect to 1965 registration plates.

 

2284 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Windsor Park Nursing Home v. Trussell


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special and Trial Term, New York County


41 Misc. 2d 1015; 247 N.Y.S.2d 189; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2164



January 27, 1964 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the complaint is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff nursing home operator brought an action for a judgment declaring defendant hospital commissioner's acts in refusing to renew the nursing home operator's license to be illegal, unauthorized, invalid, and unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The nursing home operator sought a declaratory judgment that the hospital commissioner's refusal to renew the nursing home operator's license was illegal, unauthorized, invalid, and unconstitutional. The hospital commissioner refused to renew the license until the nursing home, which was operated as a limited partnership, was changed to a general partnership. The nursing home operator contended that the applicable licensing statute did not prohibit a limited partnership from being licensed to operate a private nursing home. The court held that: (1) the spirit and intent of the applicable statutes were sufficiently clear to evince a legislative intent to require that all people forming the licensee of a nursing home bore the responsibility of a licensee vis-a-vis the department of hospitals as well as the patients in the home; and (2) the evil of allowing the treatment of the elderly sick and infirm to fall into the hands of unscrupulous operators removed from public regulation or control and immune from personal liability could easily be seen.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the action.


HEADNOTES

Licenses -- private nursing home -- operation of private nursing home by limited partnership violates New York City Hospital Code requiring that licensee operate and be responsible for home; only general partners can meet requirements.

The operation of a private nursing home by a limited partnership violates subdivisions (c) and (d) of section 2.01 of the Hospital Code, Part I (Proprietary Nursing Homes) of the Board of Hospitals, City of New York, which define a licensee as "persons who operate and who are responsible for a home" and define an applicant as "persons who intend to operate and be responsible for a home". Under these definitions, only the general partners can meet the requirements of an applicant for such a license. Accordingly, the complaint, in an action by a limited partnership for a declaration that the acts of the Commissioner of Hospitals -- refusing to issue a renewal license to operate their private nursing home unless the operation of the business was changed to a general partnership -- were illegal, is dismissed.

 

2285 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Wickham v. Trapani


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, Albany County


41 Misc. 2d 749; 246 N.Y.S.2d 137; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2181



January 21, 1964 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff Commissioner of Agriculture and Markets of the State of New York sought a declaratory judgment that N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law art. 25 was constitutional, as well as incidental relief. Defendant producer by counterclaim sought a declaration that N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 294(2)(d), an apple marketing order, and a consulting contract were unconstitutional or illegal.


OVERVIEW: The Commissioner was charged by the legislature with the promotion of agricultural products produced within the State. He was authorized to issue marketing orders towards that end. After a referendum of apple growers, in accordance with N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 294(2), the Commissioner made an apple marketing order. The producer contended that § 294(2)(d) was an unconstitutional delegation of power because of the referendum provision. The court found that was not the case because the decision to hold a referendum or to issue a marketing order was vested in the Commissioner. Furthermore, it was not necessary to for the legislative declaration in N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 292 to state that that the apple industry was of paramount importance. There was also no constitutional defect in the assessment against growers under N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 294(2)(d) because the funds were segregated for a specific purpose. Finally, consulting contracts were not illegal because N.Y. State Fin. Law § 174 dealt with goods and not with services. The court held that N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law art. 25 was constitutional and dismissed the producer's counterclaim.


OUTCOME: The court held that the Commissioner was entitled to the relief sought and to a dismissal of the producer's counterclaim.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Agriculture -- marketing of agricultural products -- apple marketing order -- paragraph (d) of subdivision (2) of section 294 of Agriculture and Markets Law empowering Commissioner of Agriculture to issue commodity marketing order is constitutional; no unlawful delegation of power to growers is involved -- letting of contracts to growers' associations without bidding is proper -- no payment of State funds to such associations is involved.

1. Article 25 of the Agriculture and Markets Law provides for the marketing of agricultural products and subdivision (2) of section 294 of the Agriculture and Markets Law, contained in such article, empowers the Commissioner of Agriculture to issue marketing orders and by paragraph (d) of such subdivision he is empowered to administer and enforce any marketing order and to prepare a budget for the administration and operating costs, including advertising and sales promotion when required and to provide for the collection of such necessary fees to defray such costs and expenses from each person engaged in the production, processing, distributing "or" handling of any commodity. The Commissioner issued a marketing order covering apples after [***2] a referendum showed that 75% plus of those participating voted in favor thereof. Paragraph (d) of subdivision 2 of section 294 is constitutional. There is no unlawful delegation of power to apple growers. The discretion to issue a marketing order is solely that of plaintiff commissioner even though he prefers that a petition for such an order be submitted, as a procedural step, before issuing such order. The delegation of power is to plaintiff which defendant concedes is proper. The referendum among apple growers was conducted after such adequate notice as was feasible was given. That fees are levied only against growers rather than processors, distributors or handlers is proper since the statute uses the disjunctive "or".

2. The letting of contracts with growers' associations without competitive bidding did not violate section 174 of the State Finance Law which deals with commodities and not services. Competitive bidding for the services here involved was not feasible.

3. No payment of State funds to the growers' associations is involved and no violation of section 8 of article VII of the State Constitution. Subdivision (8) of section 294 of the Agriculture and Markets Law [***3] expressly declares that the funds raised from the growers are not State funds.

 

2286 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Gombo v. Martise


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Kings County


41 Misc. 2d 475; 246 N.Y.S.2d 750; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2207



January 15, 1964 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] For the reasons hereinabove stated, and upon the facts in the record, and upon the law, the petitions are dismissed on the merits and the Clerk of this court is directed to return the moneys heretofore placed on deposit by the tenants, to the tenants.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landlord sued defendant tenants for nonpayment of rent. The tenants claimed entitlement to an abatement of rent due to the dangerous and uninhabitable condition of the leased premises.


OVERVIEW: The tenants deposited their rental moneys in to the court rather than pay it to the landlord. The court considered the issue of whether the landlord was entitled to his rents, or the tenants were entitled to the return of their rent money which they had deposited in court, by reason of the proof that they offered. By the virtual concession of landlord's counsel and the proof adduced, the court found that what was described as "homes" were in effect "pig-sties," and not only unfit for human habitation but dangerous to their lives, safety and health. The court concluded that counsel for the landlord was entitled to a ruling on a suggestion that he made that N.Y. Real. Prop. Acts. Law §§ 143-b and 755 were in derogation of the common law and that an earlier opinion was not the law. The court rejected such suggestion and repeated that the new statutes enacted were not in derogation of the common law, but in addition thereto.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petitions and directed the clerk of the court to return the moneys placed on deposit by the tenants, to the tenants.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- nonpayment of rent -- in proceeding to recover real property for nonpayment of rent, violations against premises not rendering them hazardous to life are not defenses in abatement of rent -- allegation in petition that no violations exist not necessary to jurisdiction -- no rents due to landlord who failed to correct violations, premises being hazard to life.

1. In a proceeding to recover possession of real property for nonpayment of rent, defended by tenants on the ground that landlord failed to supply heat and hot water, that roaches infested the building and that the apartments were not fit to be lived in by human beings, violations placed against the premises by the Building Department which are not a hazard to life and limb, are not defenses by tenants justifying an abatement of rent.

2. An allegation in landlord's petition that there are no violations on the property is not necessary [***2] to jurisdiction.

3. No rents are due a landlord who fails to correct violations placed against the premises, the conditions being hazardous or dangerous to life, limb or health. Accordingly, rent moneys paid into court by tenants are directed to be returned to them.

 

2287 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Scandifia


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Kings County


41 Misc. 2d 320; 245 N.Y.S.2d 679; 1964 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2248



January 2, 1964 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion is therefore denied in both its aspects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was indicted for conspiracy to commit murder, criminally buying and receiving stolen property, and criminally concealing and withholding stolen property. Defendant moved the court to suppress (a) all conversations of his obtained by use of an electronic device placed in defendant's premises and (b) two revolvers whose location the State allegedly knew from information obtained through use of the electronic device.


OVERVIEW: The revolvers were to be used by defendant and others in committing various crimes, including murder. Defendant argued that placing the electronic device within his premises without his knowledge constituted an intrusion that violated U.S. Const. amend IV, and that N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 813-a, empowering a court to issue an order permitting the attachment of an electronic device, was repugnant to U.S. Const. amend. IV. The court held that: (1) the revolver's were found in a co-defendant's truck pursuant to a search warrant; (2) as the validity of the search warrant had been upheld in the co-defendant's motion to vacate the search warrant and to suppress the revolvers, that decision was res judicata as to defendant; (3) if defendant's motion were granted, the State would be precluded from a proper presentation of the charges in the indictment; and (4) the State, in this instance, was entitled to the benefit of the doubt on a question of law, particularly of constitutional law.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion in both its aspects.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- unlawful search and seizure -- motion to suppress revolvers denied -- motion to suppress conversations obtained under wiretap order denied; section 813-a of Code of Criminal Procedure not unconstitutional.

1. A motion to suppress two revolvers taken in a search of codefendant's automobile pursuant to a search warrant is denied. The codefendant's motion to suppress was previously denied and the principle of res judicata operates adversely to defendant.

2. The motion to suppress conversations obtained as the result of wiretaps installed in defendant's premises by order of the court is denied. The contention that section 813-a of the Code of Criminal Procedure violates the Fourth Amendment of the Federal Constitution is rejected. The People are entitled to the benefit of the doubt on such question of law.

 

2288 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Wickham v. Champlain Creameries, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Trial Term, Albany County


41 Misc. 2d 552; 245 N.Y.S.2d 688; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1216



December 31, 1963 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, Commissioner of Agriculture and Markets for the State of New York, filed an action for injunctive relief pursuant to N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 258-e to compel defendant milk processor to deposit funds in an escrow account to be maintained to secure payment for purchased milk as provided in N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 258-b(7).


OVERVIEW: The milk processor contended that the Commissioner was not entitled to an injunction to compel payment into an escrow account because § 258-b(7) violated U.S. Const. amend. XIV and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 1, that the Commissioner's determination violated U.S. Const. amend. XIV and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 11, that he had misinterpreted the intention of the legislature when it enacted N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 258-b(7), and that his determination was arbitrary and capricious and unjustified upon the facts. The court granted injunctive relief because § 258-b(7) was merely an extension of legislation that had previously been determined to be constitutional. The fact that it provided the Commissioner with discretion was not a denial of equal protection under the law, and the standard by which security was required was applicable to all milk processors including cooperatives. The court concluded that N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 258-b was not applicable to out of state producers or cooperatives, that § 258-b(7) was constitutional, that injunctive relief was appropriate without deduction from deposited bonds, without proof of irreparable damage pursuant to N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 258-e.


OUTCOME: The court held that the Commissioner was entitled to injunctive relief.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Agriculture -- milk control -- subdivision 7 of section 258-b of Agriculture and Markets Law, which authorizes Commissioner of Agriculture and Markets to require milk dealers to establish escrow accounts to protect milk producers, is constitutional -- such subdivision is applicable to purchases from co-operatives -- but not to out-of-State producers and co-operatives -- defendant dealer was afforded procedural due process -- allegation of lack of adequate remedy at law not required; injunction granted to plaintiff.

1. In an action to compel defendant, a milk dealer, to deposit funds in an escrow account to be maintained to secure payment for milk purchased by defendant, subdivision 7 of section 258-b of the Agriculture and Markets Law, which authorizes plaintiff Commissioner of Agriculture and Markets to require milk dealers to establish such accounts as a protection to milk producers, is constitutional and does not deny due process of law to defendant nor equal protection of the laws. The subdivision empowers the Commissioner to require such escrow account as security in addition to the requirement that dealers file bonds where he feels that a bond alone will not afford adequate [***2] security. Equal protection is not denied defendant because more than one method of providing security was required of defendant. The standard for requiring the additional security is the financial condition of the dealer and the extent of his indebtedness for milk purchases.

2. Subdivision 7 is applicable to purchases from co-operatives.

3. Subdivision 7 is not applicable to purchases from out-of-State producers and co-operatives.

4. Plaintiff Commissioner, by requiring defendant to establish the escrow account and then commencing this action to enforce it, wherein defendant had a trial, afforded defendant procedural due process.

5. By the express provisions of section 258-e of the Agriculture and Markets Law, which authorizes this action for an injunction, lack of an adequate remedy at law need not be alleged or proven. Plaintiff is not required to allege and prove irreparable damage. Plaintiff is entitled to an injunction.

 

2289 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Application of Chin


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


41 Misc. 2d 641; 246 N.Y.S.2d 306; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1219



December 31, 1963 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent administrator filed a motion to dismiss a petition brought by petitioner guardian ad litem to prohibit the administrator from enforcing a requisition issued by another state for the return of infants to that state and to their parents under the Interstate Compact on Juveniles. The guardian ad litem also filed a petition for custody of the infants claiming that the grant of custody to the parents would endanger their welfare and safety.


OVERVIEW: Two nondelinquent infants, aged 15 and 17, went to live with their sister in another state. The sister was their guardian ad litem. The infants' parents obtained an order for a requisition for return of the infants under the Interstate Compact on Juveniles (compact). The guardian filed an action to prohibit return of the infants to the parents on the ground that their safety would be endangered and also sought custody of the infants. The guardian alleged that the compact was unconstitutional because it permitted an ex parte determination of whether the infants should be returned. The administrator filed a motion to dismiss the petition. The court acknowledged that due process concerns existed but held that the infants were not aggrieved. The constitutionality of the compact could not be challenged. The infants received an adjournment of the proceedings and would receive a hearing at which they could develop the facts regarding their welfare prior to a final determination. The court rejected the guardian's argument that enforcement of the compact was not within the jurisdiction of the family court. The court granted the motion to dismiss.


OUTCOME: The court granted the administrator's motion to dismiss the petition filed by the guardian ad litem to prohibit the administrator from enforcing a requisition issued by another state for the return of infants to that state and to their parents and for custody of the infants.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Infants -- Interstate Compact on Juveniles -- full hearing will be held by Family Court as to best interests of infants who have left their parents' home in Massachusetts and have come to live with their sister in New York State; prohibition petition to Supreme Court dismissed -- Interstate Compact on Juveniles does not interfere with constitutional jurisdiction of Supreme Court or of Family Court -- compact is constitutional -- infants will be given due process of full hearing, with counsel and guardian ad litem -- applicability, if any, of wayward minors statute (Code Crim. Pro., § 913-a) will be for Family Court to determine.

1. Two sisters, aged 17 and 15, left their parents' home in Massachusetts and went to the home of the older sister in Westchester County, New York. Pursuant to the Interstate Compact on Juveniles (which was executed on behalf of New York State pursuant to L. 1955, ch. 155), their parents obtained a requisition from a Judge of a Massachusetts court, demanding the return of the infants; and said requisition was presented by their counsel to our Family Court, Westchester County. Under article IV of said compact, our court may appoint counsel or guardian [***2] ad litem for such infants, and may allow a reasonable time in which to test the legality of the proceeding. Said older sister, as guardian ad litem of the infants, has instituted this proceeding in the Supreme Court, Westchester County, for an order in the nature of prohibition. She asserts that the infants' health, safety and morals will be endangered if they are returned to their parents, and she seeks to have their custody awarded to her. But these issues may be determined by our Family Court (N. Y. Const., art. VI, § 13). In the Family Court, the infants will receive a full hearing, in which their best interests must prevail even over the rights of their parents and the previous determination of the Massachusetts court and any inconsistent requirement of the compact. Accordingly, this prohibition proceeding is dismissed.

2. The Interstate Compact on Juveniles (L. 1955, ch. 155) does not interfere with the constitutional jurisdiction of our Supreme Court or of our Family Court (N. Y. Const., art. VI, §§ 6, 7, 13, 30).

3. The Interstate Compact on Juveniles (L. 1955, ch. 155) does not deal with a subject which requires the approval of Congress (U. S. Const., art. I, § 10).  [***3] Nor does it deny to the infants the equal protection of the laws, or abridge their privileges and immunities (U. S. Const., 14th Amdt.). Nor does it deny them constitutional due process of law, if the provision in article IV of the compact which provides that our court "may" allow a test of the legality of the proceeding is construed, as it properly should be, to require such a test. In any event, these infants are now being allowed to test the legality of the proceeding, and they will be given a full hearing on the merits; hence it is unnecessary to determine whether article IV of the compact should be thus construed. Nor is it necessary at this time to rule on petitioner's contention that the compact unconstitutionally gives judicial powers to the New York State Administrator of the compact.

4. Whether section 913-a of the Code of Criminal Procedure, concerning wayward minors, has any application to the facts of this case will be for the Family Court to determine.

 

2290 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Schroeppel, Oswego County v. Spector


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Oswego County


43 Misc. 2d 290; 251 N.Y.S.2d 233; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1224



December 30, 1963 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Submit decree accordingly, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff town sought to enjoin defendant property owners from continuing a nonconforming use in violation of the town's zoning ordinance. As an affirmative defense, the owners asserted that the ordinance was unconstitutional because its enactment did not meet the requirements of N.Y. Town Law § 264. The owners also asserted that any nonconforming use existed prior to the ordinance's enactment and did not extend after that point in time.


OVERVIEW: The zoning ordinance, as adopted, made the use of the owners' parcels of land as "junk yards" nonconforming uses, and proscribed their termination within three years of the date of the ordinance. At issue was whether the procedure followed by the town clerk in inserting a newspaper proof of the ordinance into the minute book complied with the requisites of § 264, which mandated that every zoning ordinance was to be entered in the minutes of the town board. Finding that such a procedure did, in fact, comply with the statutory requisites, the court granted the town's request for relief. The court did not believe that it was the legislative intent to have the ordinance written down word for word in the clerk's minutes. As long as a copy of the ordinance was attached physically to the minutes and the accompanying map was posted on the clerk's bulletin board, both available for inspection by the public, a compliance, in substance, with § 264 had occurred. Furthermore, defendants, having ignored the existence of the ordinance and having continued to pile more junks on the property, could not now be heard to complain of the "hardship" imposed by being required to remove the junks.


OUTCOME: The court directed the submission of an order, without costs, that provided for an 18-month period for the removal of autos and junk from the subject parcels of real property. The decree was also to limit the town's relief to the cessation of the nonconforming uses.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- Town Clerk complied with Town Law ( § 264) when Town Board adopted Zoning Ordinance by inserting newspaper proof thereof into minute book -- elimination of nonconforming uses within three years as provided for therein is constitutional -- defendants maintained automobile junk yard on their premises; at time ordinance was adopted, their hardship was slight; defendants cannot complain of multiplied hardship because they ignored ordinance -- defendants acquired no vested rights to continue business despite Zoning Ordinance -- defendants' use must cease and junked automobiles removed within 18 months -- proceeding for penalties against defendants barred.

1. Section 264 of the Town Law provides that every Zoning Ordinance adopted by a town shall be "entered" in the minutes of the Town Board. A Town's Clerk complied with such section by inserting a newspaper proof of the Zoning Regulations into the minute book. It was not the legislative intent that the Zoning Ordinance be written down word for word as long as a copy was attached physically to the minutes, and the accompanying map was posted on the [***2] Clerk's bulletin board and available for inspection by the public.

2. The Zoning Ordinance, effective April 13, 1953, made use of defendants' land as an automobile junk yard a nonconforming use and provided that such use be terminated within three years. Elimination of nonconforming uses is constitutional if reasonably provided for without producing too great a hardship on a property owner.

3. In April, 1953, defendants had 150 junked automobiles on the west side of the highway and 50 on the east side of the highway. There would have been slight hardship in removing those automobiles but defendants elected to ignore the ordinance and they estimate 2,500 are now on the premises. Defendants cannot complain of the hardship which they multiplied. In any event, the expense of removing the junked automobiles, using their figures, does not establish hardship.

4. The maintenance of 200 automobiles between 1951 and 1953 is not so substantial a business interest, nor has it been built up over such a period of years as to have acquired for defendants a vested right to continue despite the provisions of the Zoning Ordinance.

5. The present use of the easterly parcel is an extension of [***3] a nonconforming use. The present use of the westerly parcel is the continuation of a nonconforming use, both of which must cease and the automobiles removed within an 18-month period.

6. The town, by the decree, will be prevented from seeking relief by way of future proceedings against defendants under the penalties and remedies provisions of the Zoning Ordinance.

 

2291 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Brown v. McMorran


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


42 Misc. 2d 211; 247 N.Y.S.2d 737; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1232



December 27, 1963 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, New York State Superintendent of Public Works (superintendent) and others associated with the state, filed a motion to dismiss the amended petition filed by petitioner landowners in their action seeking review of an eminent domain determination of the superintendent. Respondents also filed a motion for summary judgment, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3211, 3212.


OVERVIEW: The landowners filed a petition challenging an eminent domain determination of the superintendent. Respondents filed a motion to dismiss the landowners' amended complaint and for summary judgment. The court denied the motions. The court held that the petition could not be maintained under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 because it sought review of a legislative determination, but that its improper form was not grounds for dismissal of the action and that, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 103(c), the court was authorized to make any order necessary for its prosecution. The court further held that the petition stated a cause of action based on the superintendent acting in excess of his authority or in a manner not authorized by law. The court concluded that the action could not have been decided on summary judgment because an issue of fact remained as to whether the superintendent had improperly delegated his power of eminent domain to a federal highway administrator. The court ordered that the proceeding continue as an action for a declaratory judgment and injunctive relief.


OUTCOME: The court denied respondents' motions to dismiss the amended complaint and for summary judgment and ordered that the landowners' action continue as an action for a declaratory judgment and injunctive relief, that the amended petition was deemed the complaint, and that respondents were required to answer within 10 days.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Highways -- determination laying out highway -- petition seeking review of such determination not maintainable -- petition alleges cause of action for declaratory judgment and is not dismissed but treated as complaint -- Legislature did not authorize Superintendent of Public Works to delegate duty of selecting property for highway construction to Federal Highway Administrator -- claims not barred by Statute of Limitations -- standing of petitioners to sue not determined on motion -- petitioners not entitled to judgment on ground that Highway Law ( § 85) unconstitutional.

1. A petition whether it seeks the relief heretofore available in a certiorari or mandamus proceeding, still seeks review of a determination and may not be maintained either under article 78 of the Civil Practice Act or under article 78 of the Civil Practice Law and Rules.

2. Since the court has jurisdiction of the parties and the petition alleges a cause of action, it is not dismissed because brought in improper form, but is deemed a complaint seeking a declaratory judgment and injunctive relief.

3. Power to acquire by appropriation and to select the property to be acquired in the construction of highways [***2] as part of the Federal Aid Highway System has been delegated by the Legislature to the Superintendent of Public Works. The Legislature has not authorized the Superintendent to delegate that power to the Federal Highway Administrator. Whether the delegated authority was exercised by the Superintendent in the manner authorized by law, as the Superintendent asserts, or whether it was surrendered to the Federal Highway Administrator, as contended by petitioners may not be determined on consideration of the papers and proof submitted.

4. The claims asserted in the amended petition are not barred by the four months' Statute of Limitations contained in section 217 of the Civil Practice Law and Rules. The original petition gave notice of the transactions to be proved under the amended petition which asserts no remedy not sought in the original petition.

5. Whether all petitioners have standing to sue may not be determined on this motion.

6. Petitioners are not entitled to judgment on the ground that section 85 of the Highway Law contravenes section 16 of article III of the Constitution in letter or spirit.

 

2292 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Scales v. Board of Education


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


41 Misc. 2d 391; 245 N.Y.S.2d 449; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1244



December 23, 1963 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner father filed a writ of mandamus under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 7801 to obtain an order requiring respondents, board of education and superintendent of schools, to provide home teaching for his infant daughter.


OVERVIEW: The father filed an action seeking to compel respondents to provide home teaching to the infant, who suffered from a heart condition. On an order to show cause, the court ordered respondents to provide home teaching to the infant, holding that N.Y. Educ. Law § 1709(24) as applied to the case was not unconstitutional as violative of N.Y. Const. art. XI, § 4, and that N.Y. Educ. Law § 1709(24) required respondents to furnish the home teaching requested. The court found that § 1709(24), which required boards of education to provide home teaching to physically handicapped children, benefited all children, including those attending parochial school. The court noted that it need not determine whether the infant was "physically handicapped" under § 1709(24) because respondents conceded that fact. The court further found that providing home teaching to a parochial student did not violate N.Y. Const. art. XI, § 4, which prohibited state aid to a school wholly or in part under the control or direction of a religious denomination, because the benefits of home teaching would inure solely to the student and would be of no financial aid to the parochial school.


OUTCOME: The court ordered respondents to provide home teaching for the infant.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Schools -- home teaching for physically handicapped children -- parochial school child, ill at home for months, is entitled to home teaching by union free school district ( Education Law, § 1709, subd. 24); subdivision applies "irrespective of the school they legally attend" -- Legislature may provide for education of physically handicapped; public moneys are not thereby used directly or indirectly in aid of parochial school (N. Y. Const., art. VII, § 8; art. XI, § 3) -- appeal to Commissioner of Education is not sole remedy -- civil division of State may question constitutionality of State statute.

1. The Education Law ( § 1709, subd. 24) provides that the "board of education of every union free school district" shall "provide * * * home-teaching * * * for physically * * * handicapped * * * children" and that "Such * * * home-teaching * * *, when provided pursuant to this subdivision, shall be granted to all such children irrespective of the school they legally attend." This wording includes pupils of parochial or denominational schools. Petitioner's child attends a parochial school, in the sixth grade. She has a rheumatic heart condition and will be confined to her bed [***2] for the next three to six months. The parochial school has no home-teaching program. The board of education of the union free school district wherein she resides with her father concedes that the above subdivision would require it to furnish home teaching to this child if she attended a public school. Accordingly, the court orders ( CPLR 7801) said board and its Superintendent of Schools to provide home teaching for the child.

2. The statute ( Education Law, § 1709, subd. 24), as thus construed, is authorized by and is not violative of the State Constitution, which provides that "nothing in this constitution contained shall prevent the legislature from providing * * * for the education * * * of * * * the physically handicapped * * * or for health and welfare services for all children" (art. VII, § 8), and that "Neither the state nor any subdivision thereof shall use its property or credit or any public money, or authorize or permit either to be used, directly or indirectly, in aid or maintenance * * * of any school or institution of learning wholly or in part under the control or direction of any religious denomination" (art. XI, § 3).

3. Section 310 of the Education Law, giving [***3] the State Commissioner of Education jurisdiction of appeals from school board decisions, does not bar a proceeding in the nature of mandamus to compel the performance of a duty specifically imposed by law.

4. A civil division of the State may attack the constitutionality of a State statute.

 

2293 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Real Properties in Easthampton


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Suffolk County


42 Misc. 2d 376; 248 N.Y.S.2d 187; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1264



December 17, 1963 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A motion came for hearing to confirm the final report of commissioners appointed pursuant to § 62 of the Suffolk County Improvement Act, 1927 N.Y. Laws ch. 190, 1945 N.Y. Laws ch. 746, 1954 N.Y. Laws ch. 796, in order to take testimony regarding the matter of the county's acquisition of certain real properties for county purposes.


OVERVIEW: The key question on the motion for confirmation was the effect of the resignation of one of the three appointed commissioners prior to the taking of testimony. The court found that: (1) the hearings and the provisions of § 64 of the Suffolk County Improvement Act were violative of N.Y. Const., art. I, § 7(b); (2) it had been mandatory that there be no less than three commissioners at the hearings; (3) the constitutional statutory provision for the three commissioners was jurisdictional and could not be waived; and (4) any majority decision regarding compensation in condemnation cases had to be made at a meeting at which all three commissioners had been present.


OUTCOME: The motion to confirm the final report of the commissioners appointed to rule on compensation in the condemnation action was denied.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Eminent domain -- Commissioners of Appraisal -- three Commissioners are required (N. Y. Const., art. I, § 7, subd. [b]); if one resigns, other two cannot act (cf. Suffolk County Improvement Act, § 64).

When one of three Commissioners in condemnation resigns, the other two cannot take testimony and make awards (N. Y. Const., art. I, § 7, subd. [b]). Section 64 of the Suffolk County Improvement Act is, to that extent, unconstitutional and void. Of course, a majority may make awards, but all must be present. A final report by only two Commissioners, one having resigned before any testimony was taken, will not be confirmed.

 

2294 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Catrambone


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Queens County


41 Misc. 2d 282; 245 N.Y.S.2d 742; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1320



December 4, 1963 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motion is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant had filed a motion for an order for declaring N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. §§ 177(1), 813-c unconstitutional, for directing the prosecution to bear the burden of refuting the contents of certain papers, for controverting a search warrant, and for directing the suppression of evidence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted for operating a policy business, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 974, and for possessing policy slips. Defendant, by his motion, argued that N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 177(1) was unconstitutional because it circumvented the intent of the fourth amendment by affording police officers an opportunity to search and seize without any sufficient warrant or basis. He further claimed that N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 813-c was unconstitutional by compelling him to controvert the search warrant, which was in violation of his rights against self-incrimination. The court declined to declare these statutes unconstitutional because every statute had a presumption of constitutionality. The court also noted that the state legislature in exercising its police power had the right and duty to enact such laws for the protection of the people in the state. Finally, the court declined to grant defendant's motion on the remaining requests. The court found that sufficient probable cause had supported the search warrant and concluded that suppression of evidence was inappropriate in this case.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion for an order declaring certain sections of the criminal code of procedure unconstitutional, directing the prosecution to bear the burden of refuting the contents of certain papers, controverting a search warrant, and directing the suppression of evidence.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- search warrant -- sections 177 and 813-c of Code of Criminal Procedure are constitutional -- defendant's motion to suppress evidence and controvert search warrant denied since no facts are presented creating issue warranting hearing.

1. Sections 177 and 813-c of the Code of Criminal Procedure are constitutional.

2. A police officer received information that defendant had taken wagers at a certain location. The police officer observed defendant at that location on two different days receiving slips of paper and money in bill form. He obtained a search warrant on the third day and, after observing defendant take envelopes from three different men, he showed defendant the warrant, searched him and placed him under arrest. The affidavit of the police officer established that there was probable cause for the issuance of the warrant and apprehension of defendant. Defendant's affidavit denying that the transactions as described by the police officer occurred does not entitle defendant to a hearing. Defendant having moved to suppress the evidence and to controvert the search warrant failed to come forward with facts creating an issue. His motion [***2] is denied.

In re Estate of Salmon


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, New York County


41 Misc. 2d 190; 245 N.Y.S.2d 531; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1326



December 3, 1963 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Upon the authority of the cases cited and the clear direction of the statute, it is plain that the respondent as a person whose legacy under the prior will is threatened by the propounded paper, is entitled to challenge the later instrument in this proceeding and the motions to dismiss her objections are accordingly denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a proceeding for the probate of a will, two nominated fiduciaries had filed separate motions that sought to dismiss the objections of respondent testator's former wife upon the ground that she was not a person interested in the estate within the meaning of N.Y. Surr. Ct. Proc. Act § 147.


OVERVIEW: In a proceeding for the probate of a will, the testator's former wife had filed certain objections. The two nominated fiduciaries had filed motions to dismiss the objections upon the ground that the testator's former wife was not a person interested in the estate within the meaning of N.Y. Surr. Proc. Act § 147. The court agreed that the former wife, upon obtaining a divorce, became disqualified from sharing in the estate as a surviving spouse, and the separation agreement constituted a complete release and waiver of any other statutory right to participate in the distribution of the testator's property. However, the will in question had provided a legacy for the former wife's benefit, which was purportedly revoked by a codicil. The court found that the former wife had a right to a hearing upon the validity of the codicil where the codicil had affected her proprietary rights and interests. The court concluded that the former wife was a person who had the capacity to challenge the probate of the will within the meaning of § 147.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motions of the two nominated fiduciaries that sought to dismiss the objections of the testator's former wife upon the ground that she was not a person interested in the estate within the meaning of the surrogate procedure act.


HEADNOTES

Wills -- probate -- former wife of testator, as legatee under prior will, is entitled to challenge later will.

Testator's former wife, as a person whose legacy under a prior will is threatened by the propounded papers, is entitled to challenge them in this proceeding under section 147 of the Surrogate's Court Act. The motion to dismiss her objections is denied.

Totero v. World Telegram Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


41 Misc. 2d 594; 245 N.Y.S.2d 870; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1341



December 2, 1963 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion is denied. In this circumstance, the cross-motion upon the basis of its presentation by the plaintiff is academic and it is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant writer asked a trial court to dismiss, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3211, the defamation complaint filed against him by plaintiff subject on the ground that he was not subject to the jurisdiction of the court, that service of process was insufficient, and that N.Y. C.P.L.R. 308(a) was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The writer was a resident of Spain and his column was mailed by him from Spain to the United States. The columns were distributed by a syndication company located in New York. The writer was also represented in the United States by virtue of a contract with his literary agent in relation to other matters, and it was upon this literary agent that the summons was served. The writer argued that service upon the agent was a mere gesture and violated his right to due process. The court first held that by virtue of the distribution of his column to syndication members, the writer conducted business in the United States and that the subject's action against the writer arose out that business. The court further held that service was properly made on a nondomiciliary individual by service on his actual agent; that the particular agent upon whom such service was made need not have been the writer's agent in respect of the matter out of which the action arose; that upon service on the actual agent a mailing to the writer was not required; and that under the circumstances of this case no real constitutional question arose under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 308.


OUTCOME: The court denied the writer's motion to dismiss the defamation complaint filed against him by the subject.


HEADNOTES

Process -- service on nonresident -- defendant writer, resident of Spain, sent articles to syndicater in New York who mailed them to codefendant, newspaper, which published articles; summons in action for libel properly served on writer's literary agent with whom writer had contract even though he was not agent in respect of business transaction out of which libel arose -- single act statute is constitutional -- cause of action arises out of business transaction conducted within New York.

1. Articles, written by a resident of Spain, are forwarded to a distributor of syndicated features, with which distributor the writer has a contract. The distributor forwards copies of the articles to syndicate members specifying the release date. Codefendant published the article in question in its newspaper. In this action for libel, service of process upon defendant writer was properly effected upon a literary agent with whom defendant writer has a contract. Service may be made on a nondomiciliary individual by service on his actual [***2] agent. The agent need not be defendant's agent in respect of the matter or business transaction out of which the action arose. A mailing to defendant is not required to meet due process.

2. The single act statute ( CPLR 302) is constitutional.

3. The cause of action arises out of a business transaction conducted within the State.

 

2297 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Bookcase, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


40 Misc. 2d 796; 244 N.Y.S.2d 297; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1406



November 14, 1963 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motions denied, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the corporate owner of a bookstore, its president, and an employee, filed a motion to dismiss a prosecution under N.Y. Penal Law § 484-h, which prohibited the sale of obscene books to minors, on the grounds that the statute unconstitutionally abridged freedom of speech and was vague and indefinite.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged with violating N.Y. Penal Law § 484-h after a 16-year old girl purchased a copy of "Fanny Hill." Defendants moved to dismiss the information on the grounds that § 484-h inhibited freedom of expression in violation of U.S. Const. amends. I, XIV, and that the statute was vague and indefinite. The court denied the motions and found each defendant guilty, holding that the book was obscene and that § 484-h was a valid exercise of the state's police power to protect minors. The judges found that the publication was indecent and violated a valid statute enacted to protect the morals of young people. The statute was constitutional and was not vague or indefinite where it was sufficiently specific to apprise anyone of what the legislature intended to prohibit.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motions to dismiss and found each defendant guilty.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- obscene books -- "Memoirs of A Woman of Pleasure" is book made up of descriptions of sexual immorality whose sale to minors under 18 years of age is forbidden by Penal Law ( § 484-h); defendants guilty of violating section -- section not unconstitutional -- and not vague.

1. "Memoirs of A Woman of Pleasure" is a book which, in the language of section 484-h of the Penal Law banning the sale or advertising for sale of such books to minors under 18 years of age, "exploits, is devoted to, or is principally made up of descriptions of illicit sex or sexual immorality" and defendants who sold such book to a minor under 18 years of age are guilty of violating the section. ( Larkin v. Putnam's Sons, 40 Misc 2d 28, not followed.)

2. Section 484-h of the Penal Law is not unconstitutional because, as defendants contend, it limits the sale of certain books to persons over 18 years of age and thus is a form of censorship. The Legislature may provide, without violating the Constitution, that certain literature not be sold to adolescents or minors of immature minds.

3. The section is not vague but is sufficiently specific to apprise anyone of [***2] what is prohibited.

 

2298 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Robert B. Blaikie & Co. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


41 Misc. 2d 371; 245 N.Y.S.2d 121; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1432



November 8, 1963 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly the motion by the defendant, City of New York, for summary judgment is granted and the cross motion denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff company instituted the action for a declaratory judgment against defendant city on the grounds that the New York City Commercial Rent or Occupancy Tax chapter 257 (1963) and New York City, N.Y., Local Law 38 (1963), which imposed taxes, were void, unconstitutional and ineffectual.


OVERVIEW: The New York City Commercial Rent or Occupancy Tax chapter 257 (1963) enabled any city having a population of 1,000,000 or more to impose and collect taxes on rent or occupancy. The company contended that the tax was unconstitutional, based principally on the grounds that it imposed a real estate tax, which exceeded the limit set forth in New York Const. art. VIII, § 10 and imposed an ad valorem tax in violation of N.Y. Const. art. XVI, § 3. On review, the court disagreed. The tax did not constitute a tax on real estate, and therefore did not violate the constitution. The tax was neither an ad valorem tax nor an excise tax levied solely because of ownership or possession. The company further alleged that the tax violated the "Due Process" and "Equal Protection" clauses of the Fourteenth Amendment. The court disagreed and held that the power of taxation was legislative and the legislature's determination was final.


OUTCOME: The court granted the city's motion for summary judgment and denied the company's cross motion.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- constitutional law -- New York City Commercial Rent or Occupancy Tax is constitutional -- not considered to be real property tax or excise tax on intangibles (N. Y. Const., art. VIII, § 10; art XVI, § 3) -- distinctions made in tax act do not violate constitutional due process or equal protection (U. S. Const., 14th Amdt.).

1. The New York City local law requiring every tenant who uses or occupies premises for any trade, business, profession, vocation, or commercial activity to pay a tax of 2 1/2% of his base rent if such base rent does not exceed $ 2,500 a year, or of 5% of his base rent if such base rent exceeds $ 2,500 a year (Administrative Code of City of New York, § L46-1.0 et seq.), and the enabling statute (L. 1963, ch. 257), are valid and constitutional.

2. A tax on a leasehold is not considered to be a real property tax within the meaning of the State Constitution (art. VIII, § 10); nor is this an excise or ad valorem tax levied solely because of the ownership or possession of "moneys, credits, securities and other intangible [***2] personal property" (N. Y. Const., art. XVI, § 3).

3. The "due process" and "equal protection" provisions of the United States Constitution (14th Amdt.) are not violated by the facts that the tax applies only to cities having a population of 1,000,000 or more, and that the tax is imposed at different rates depending on whether the tenant's rent is more or less than $ 2,500 a year, and that the tax is not imposed on an owner of premises who is also a lessee therein. All these distinctions are within the constitutional taxing powers of a Legislature.

 

2299 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Paterson v. University of State of New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


40 Misc. 2d 1023; 244 N.Y.S.2d 394; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1477



October 31, 1963 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] In light of the foregoing, the complaint is dismissed and judgment is granted in favor of defendants.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, group of individuals and a corporation, who held themselves out to be "landscape architects," filed an action against defendant New York Board of Examiners, seeking a judgment declaring that, N.Y. Educ. Law §§ 7320-7332 (1960), which pertained to the licensing of landscape architects and the regulation of the practice of landscape architecture in the state of New York, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: After defendants denied plaintiffs' a landscape architect license on the ground that plaintiffs lacked the required qualifications and experience for a license, plaintiffs filed an action challenging the constitutionality of the licensing provisions of §§ 7320-7332. The court dismissed plaintiffs' action, granted judgment in favor of defendants, and declared the statute constitutional. The court held that (1) a justiciable controversy existed because plaintiffs held themselves out to be landscape architects and a declaration of plaintiffs' rights was essential to their ability to earn a livelihood; (2) although certain activities of those required by the statute to be licensed did not touch upon the health, safety and welfare of the community, the statute's regulation of aesthetics was a proper exercise of the police power and the restrictions imposed by the statute did not present an arbitrary or irrational method of achieving a proper legislative goal; and (3) the statute was not an unlawful delegation of legislative authority and did not deprive plaintiffs of property without due process of law. Thus, the court held that the statute was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed plaintiffs' action seeking a judgment declaring that a state statute, which pertained to the licensing of landscape architects and the regulation of the practice of landscape architecture in the state of New York, was unconstitutional. The court granted judgment in favor of defendants and declared the statute constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Licenses -- landscape architects -- justiciable controversy exists between plaintiffs, holding themselves out as landscape architects, and defendant, which refused to license them under article 148 of Education Law, warranting declaration of their rights -- no unlawful delegation of legislative power given to Board of Examiners -- article 148 does not violate due process of law; article not vague -- regulation of activities of landscape architects is proper exercise of police power; activities affect public health -- article 148 is constitutional.

1. Article 148 of the Education Law ( §§ 7320-7332) prohibits, on and after July 1, 1961, the practice of landscape architecture, as defined in the statute, and one's holding himself out to be a "landscape architect" without a license having first been issued by the Education Department (§ 7321) upon certification by the Board of Examiners established by the statute (§ 7322). A violation thereof is declared to be a misdemeanor, punishable by fine and imprisonment (§ 7328). A justiciable controversy exists between plaintiffs,  [***2] who hold themselves out to be landscape architects and perform the work included in the definition contained in the statute, and defendant, which declined to grant them licenses. Plaintiffs are aggrieved parties and entitled to a declaration of their rights under article 148.

2. The discretion vested in the Board of Examiners under section 7323 to grant or refuse admission to a licensing examination and to grant or deny an application for a license does not constitute an unlawful delegation of legislative authority, despite the use of phrases in the statute that proposed licensees have educational backgrounds or experience "of a grade and character acceptable to the board". The Legislature here specifically defined the cases in which the board is to exercise its discretion and what factors are to be considered.

3. Article 148 of the Education Law does not deprive plaintiffs of their property without due process of law. The activities regulated are those which are restricted to the furnishing of trained professional services for consultation, research, planning, design, etc., for the development, preservation and enhancement of land, as distinguished from the furnishing of the [***3] labor, equipment, or supplies whereby the development of land areas is to be realized. The statute sets forth a clear standard of conduct giving notice to one concerned with or engaged in the activities regulated as to what acts are criminal. The statute is neither vague nor uncertain.

4. The interests of public health, safety and welfare require the regulation of the activities performed by a landscape architect.
