There was also a relationship between serum hepatitis and tattooing under unsafe conditions. The court found that tattooing could be performed in a manner that presented no health problems with regard to serum hepatitis. Further, there was evidence that tattooing machines and equipment could be entirely sterilized. The court found that the agency abandoned its previously adopted regulation of tattooing, in favor of prohibition, without ever having actually enforced the regulation by fine or criminal penalty. The court concluded that the law was violative of due process. Therefore, the court held that the law was unconstitutional and void.


OUTCOME: The court granted injunctive relief to the tattoo parlor operators.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Health -- tattooing -- regulation forbidding tattooing except by doctors, declared unconstitutional.

Section 181.15, as amended, of the New York City Health Code, which prohibits any tattooing of a human being except for medical purposes by doctors, and which repeals an earlier section 181.15 which merely required tattooers to use sterile equipment, is not shown to be reasonably related to the protection of the public against serum hepatitis, and hence the amended section is unconstitutional and void.

 

2324 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Logan


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Queens County


39 Misc. 2d 593; 241 N.Y.S.2d 344; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1871



June 25, 1963 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion for an order to suppress evidence premised on the ground that it was obtained by an illegal search and seizure.


OVERVIEW: The question before the court on defendant's motion to suppress evidence was whether the court had the power to pass upon the legal and factual validity of a confession by a defendant, allegedly coerced, pursuant to which confession he made disclosure of the existence of certain contraband, a revolver, disclosed its hiding place, and turned it over to the police. The court held that the New York suppression procedure of N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 813-c, which operated in advance of trial to exclude the physical and tangible results of property obtained by the police as a result of an unlawful search and seizure, was broad enough to encompass, within its scope, the suppression of such tangible evidence when it was obtained by the police as a result of a confession extracted from a defendant in violation of his constitutional rights. The court's basic consideration for so holding was the expedition of the business of the courts by avoidance, whenever possible, of the trial, error, appeal, and retrial formula. The court granted the motion to suppress the evidence because the production of the revolver under coercive circumstances was at least factually of an involuntary nature.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion to suppress evidence produced following and as a direct result of a confession coercively obtained by the police.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- unlawful search and seizure -- confession that defendant had gun and his production of gun were coerced by threat of police to arrest defendant's acquaintances, relatives and mother and were involuntary -- suppression procedure (Code Crim. Pro., § 813-c) applicable to suppress evidence obtained from defendant in violation of his constitutional rights; defendant's motion to suppress gun was granted.

1. Defendant, then under arrest, was questioned by police officers as to the whereabouts of a gun. After denying its existence, and when the police officers stated that everyone in the apartment, including his mother, would be taken into custody and taken to the police station if it were not produced, defendant confessed possession, led the officers to the hiding place and delivered it to them. The threat to detain and take his relatives and acquaintances to the police station, in effect, to arrest them, was coercive and the confession was therefore involuntary.

2. The suppression procedure (Code Crim. Pro., § 813-c) operating in advance of trial to exclude evidence obtained as the result of an unlawful search and seizure was broad enough to apply and to suppress evidence [***2] obtained by the police as a result of a confession extracted from defendant in violation of his constitutional rights. The motion to suppress the gun was granted.

 

2325 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Bobandal Realties, Inc. v. Worthington


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


39 Misc. 2d 756; 241 N.Y.S.2d 1000; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1872



June 25, 1963 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landowner filed an action with the court, claiming that the refusal of respondent building inspector to issue a building permit pursuant to Greenburg, N.Y., Zoning Ordinance § 8(E) was unconstitutional and invalid.


OVERVIEW: The landowner ran a country club that was partially destroyed by fire. He filed with the inspector an application for a permit to repair the facilities that were destroyed. The inspector denied the application stating that the country club was deemed to be a nonconforming use and could not be rebuilt pursuant to the zoning ordinance. Prior to the adoption of the ordinance the landowner had a vested nonconforming use. The landowner then filed an action to have the ordinance declared unconstitutional and invalid. On review, the court ruled in favor of the landowner. The court held that the ordinance was unreasonable and unconstitutional in that it permitted the rebuilding of nonconforming structures only if their damage or destruction did not exceed 50 percent. The court stated that the volume of the structure that was destroyed could not be substituted for the value of the structure or the deprivation that would result if the reconstruction was not permitted. The court noted that the landowner properly filed his action attacking the validity of the ordinance not with the zoning board but with the court because to do the former would have assumed the validity of the ordinance.


OUTCOME: The court directed the building inspector to revoke his denial of the landowner's application for a building permit to rebuild the country club and permitted the landowner to proceed with his application.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- zoning -- noncomforming uses -- in proceeding to compel issuance of permit for rebuilding of nonconforming structure, constitutionality of zoning ordinance was properly challenged -- provision of ordinance permitting restoration of noncomforming building only if damage did not exceed 50% of volume of building was unconstitutional -- petitioner had right to challenge ordinance even if application did not show what percentage of volume was destroyed -- provision that rebuilt premises be in same location and not larger was reasonable -- requirement that rebuilt structure comply with zoning restrictions might be in conflict with petitioner's right to rebuild.

1. Petitioner owned land in a residence district operated as a recreation center as a nonconforming use. A portion of a building housing the restaurant and bar was destroyed by fire, and when petitioner applied for a building permit it was denied because it was a nonconforming use. In this proceeding to compel the issuance of a building permit, the constitutionality of the ordinance was properly challenged. Petitioner was not required to first apply to the Zoning Board of Appeals for a variance.  [***2] By doing so, petitioner would concede that the ordinance was constitutional.

2. The ordinance which, as a matter of right, permitted petitioner to restore a nonconforming building only if the damage did not exceed 50% of the volume of the building above the foundation, was unreasonable, confiscatory and unconstitutional. Measuring damage on the basis of volume rather than value afforded the property owner inadequate protection. The important consideration was not what was destroyed, but that what was left might be valueless if the reconstruction were not permitted.

3. Even though petitioner did not show in his application what percentage of the volume of the building was destroyed and even though it could show that 50% of the volume was not destroyed, petitioner still had the right to determine the constitutionality of the ordinance.

4. The provision that the premises, if rebuilt or restored, be in substantially the same location provided it were not enlarged or extended was a reasonable requirement.

5. The requirement that the rebuilt structure comply with height, yard, area or other restrictions of the ordinance applicable to the district might be in conflict with the right [***3] of petitioner to rebuild or restore. The Zoning Board of Appeals, in granting variances, was instructed to consider this right.

 

2326 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Davis v. Union Free School Dist.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


39 Misc. 2d 671; 241 N.Y.S.2d 799; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1895



June 19, 1963 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition is dismissed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner individual commenced a proceeding by an order to show cause against respondents, a school district and a town, to enjoin them from conducting a school budget election, or in the alternative to order them to permit him to register for the election under N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act art. 78 and N.Y. Educ. Law § 2016.


OVERVIEW: The school district was a district that had personal registration of voters at school meeting or elections as prescribed by § 2014. On the last day to register for a special district meeting, the individual attempted to register with the New York Board of Registration. He was informed that he was not qualified to vote under § 2012 and his name was not entered upon the register of qualified voters. The individual contended that the qualifications for voters set forth in § 2012 were unconstitutional. The court dismissed the individual's petition. The court held (1) that the procedure chosen by the individual was fatally defective, as the individual had utilized a special proceeding, not an action, and the injunctive relief that he had sought was not available; (2) that as the individual did not and could not make satisfactory proof to the Board that he was entitled to vote at a district meeting or election, it was the Board's duty by law not to register him; and (3) that relief under § 2016 was not available to the individual, as he concededly did not fulfill the requirements of § 2012.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the individual's petition.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- vote upon school budget -- injunction against school election not available in proceeding (Civ. Prac. Act, art. 78) -- petitioner failed to establish clear right to compel school Board of Registration to perform duty enjoined by law to register him as "qualified voter" -- petitioner had no right to relief under section 2016 of Education Law which provided review of registration procedure, since Board of Registration was not party respondent.

1. A proceeding under article 78 of the Civil Practice Act was not the proper vehicle to enjoin a school budget election upon the ground that the register of eligible voters was compiled under an unconstitutional statute.

2. If the proceeding was a special proceeding to compel performance of a duty specifically enjoined by law, petitioner failed to establish a clear right to such relief. Petitioner did not satisfactorily prove to the Board of Registration that he was entitled to vote at a school district meeting or election. He lacked the qualifications of a voter set forth in section 2012 of the Education Law. In such circumstances it was the duty specifically enjoined upon [***2] the Board of Registration by law not to register petitioner.

3. Petitioner had no right to relief under section 2016 of the Education Law which provided a method to review registration procedure when a voter was denied registration, since the Board of Registration of the school district was not a party respondent. Moreover, the remedy of section 2016 was available to a "qualified voter", and petitioner was not a qualified voter under section 2012 of the Education Law.

 

2327 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Jamaica Water Supply Co. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Queens County


39 Misc. 2d 866; 242 N.Y.S.2d 275; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1910



June 18, 1963 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendants' motions for summary judgment are granted. Plaintiff's request for summary judgment in its favor is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff waterworks corporation filed a cause of action for a judgment declaring a certain agreement regarding the supply of water to a co-operative housing project between defendant City of New York, New York and defendant limited-profit housing corporation to be unconstitutional, illegal, and void. All parties filed motions for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The waterworks corporation had a franchise contract to supply water to the area where the limited-profit housing corporation was building a project. The waterworks corporation anticipated the additional need for water and purchased land in order to construct a water storage tank. Subsequently, the Board of Estimate of the City of New York adopted a resolution approving plans and specifications for hauling and laying water mains in various streets in the waterworks corporation's franchise area. Certain monies were to be paid by the limited-profit housing corporation over a five year period. The waterworks corporation contended that the resolution adopted paralleled its existing water mains with consequent loss of revenue to the waterworks corporation. Furthermore, the $ 250,000 owed by the limited-profit housing corporation was a loan and illegal under N.Y. Const. art. VIII, § 1 because the City could not loan money. Granting defendants' motions, the court held that the waterworks corporation's franchise was not exclusive and was not being revoked. The court held that the money owed by the limited-profit housing corporation was a contribution and not a loan.


OUTCOME: Defendants' motions for summary judgment were granted and the waterworks corporation's request for summary judgment in its favor was denied.


HEADNOTES

Waters and watercourses -- water supply -- contribution by limited-profit housing company of $ 250,000 to cost of construction of water mains by city was not made loan by city to customer because contribution was evidenced by note -- no statute barred defendant city from duplicating plaintiff water supply company's mains -- approval by Water Resources Commission was not required -- city was not revoking plaintiff's franchise -- action of defendant city was not illegal -- defendants' motions for summary judgment dismissing complaint were granted.

1. A limited-profit housing company which contributed $ 250,000 to the cost of construction of water mains by a city so that the city could supply its project with water was not a loan by a city which the city was prohibited from making. It did not become a loan merely because payable over a five-year period and was evidenced by a note.

2. Section 89-e of the Public Service Law which barred paralleling or duplicating of mains of an existing water supply company was rendered inapplicable to defendant city by [***2] section 89-l.

3. Subdivision (1) of section 450 of the Conservation Law which provided that a public corporation might not supply water until the Water Resources Commission approved maps and plans was made inapplicable to defendant city by subdivision (2) of said section.

4. Defendant city was not revoking plaintiff water supply company's franchise nor was it condemning plaintiff's property.

5. The action of defendant city was neither illegal nor unconstitutional even though it had an unfortunate impact upon plaintiff's business.

6. Defendants' motions for summary judgment dismissing the complaint were granted.

 

2328 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


New York v. Claflington, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


40 Misc. 2d 547; 243 N.Y.S.2d 437; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1934



June 12, 1963 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] This motion is therefore granted, with leave to plaintiff to serve an amended complaint within 20 days after service of a copy of the order with notice of entry thereof.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant building owners filed a motion to dismiss plaintiff city's complaint charging that the owners refused to rent an apartment to the complainant solely because of her race. The owners based their motion on the ground that the court had no jurisdiction over the subject matter of the action and on the ground that the city did not have the legal capacity to sue.


OVERVIEW: A complainant was filed with the New York City Commission on Intergroup Relations, which charged that the owners refused to rent an apartment to the complainant solely because of her race. The city sought to restrain the owners from further discrimination. The owners moved to strike the complaint pursuant to N. Y. C. P. L. R. 107(1) on the ground that the court had no jurisdiction over the subject matter, and pursuant to N. Y. C. P. L. R. 107(2) on the ground that the city did not have the legal capacity to sue. The court found that by enacting 1963 N. Y. Laws, ch. 376, which permitted counties, cities, villages, and towns to create a commission on human relations, the Legislature conclusively demonstrated its intention not to grant exclusive jurisdiction in this field to the State. The court found that under the grant of police power, the city had authority to enact laws prohibiting discrimination in housing. The court denied the motion based upon lack of jurisdiction, but granted the motion on based on N. Y. C. P. L. R. 107(2), finding that the complaint did not allege that the city had a legal capacity to sue.


OUTCOME: The court granted the owners' motion to dismiss for lack of legal capacity to sue and granted the city leave to serve an amended complaint within 20 days after service of a copy of the order with notice of entry thereof.


HEADNOTES

Civil rights -- discrimination in housing -- State of New York has not pre-empted field of discrimination in housing; accordingly complaint based on local laws to restrain defendants from discriminating against person by refusing to rent apartment because of her race gives court jurisdiction.

The Legislature has not indicated an intent to pre-empt the field of discrimination in housing by the enactment of article 15 of the Executive Law. Its subsequent enactment of legislation permitting counties, cities, villages and towns to create a commission on human relations demonstrates its intention not to grant exclusive jurisdiction to the State. Accordingly, legislation of the City of New York dealing with the subject of discrimination in housing supplements the State law and the complaint in this action to restrain defendants from further descrimination against a person to whom they allegedly refused to rent an apartment because of her race, based on local laws of the City of New York, is within the jurisdiction [***2] of the court.

 

2329 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Cerbone v. Pelham Manor


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


39 Misc. 2d 320; 240 N.Y.S.2d 523; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1993



May 27, 1963 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the defendants' motion is granted and the complaint dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff dentist filed a complaint for a declaratory judgment against defendant village to have declared void as unconstitutional certain provisions of the village's zoning ordinance and for an injunction restraining the village from enforcing the zoning ordinance as applied to the dentist. The village filed a motion to dismiss the complaint pursuant to N.Y. R. Civ. Prac. 106(4).


OVERVIEW: The dentist maintained a practice in another city and purchased a family house in a district of the village with the intent of using a small portion of the residence as a dental office. Village of Pelham Manor, N.Y., Zoning Ordinance of 1953 prohibited the dentist's proposed use. The dentist filed a complaint seeking to have the ordinance declared void as unconstitutional, and also sought an injunction restraining the village from enforcing the zoning ordinance as applied to him. The village filed a motion to dismiss the complaint pursuant to N.Y. R. Civ. Prac. 106(4), on the ground that the complaint did not state a cause of action. The court granted the motion and dismissed the complaint. The dentist had no standing to challenge the constitutionality of Village of Pelham Manor, N.Y., Zoning Ordinance of 1953, art. IV, § 43(1b) insofar as it excluded district professions other than physicians and dentists on the ground that it may be discriminatory as to professions of which the dentist was not a member. The limitation of the ordinance did not invalidate legislation otherwise tending to fulfill the zoning purposes specified in Village of Pelham Manor Law § 177.


OUTCOME: The court granted the village's motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- zoning ordinance which prevented dentist from practicing in home in low density residential zone was not invalid.

The complaint, in an action by a dentist to declare invalid provisions of a village zoning ordinance forbidding the practice of a profession in a home in a low density residence zone, was dismissed. The circumstance that the zoning ordinance regulating the use of real property had a limited impact on plaintiff, preventing him from maintaining an office for the practice of dentistry in his home in a low density residence zone, was not sufficient to invalidate legislation tending to fulfill the zoning purposes specified in section 177 of the Village Law.

 

2330 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Milchman v. Rivera


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Civil Court of the City of New York, Bronx County


39 Misc. 2d 347; 240 N.Y.S.2d 859; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2055



May 10, 1963 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, final order is directed in favor of the tenant in each case dismissing the landlord's petition, but without prejudice to the landlord instituting a new proceeding in the event the rent is not paid to him after he has complied with the law and removed the violations hereinabove designated which are dangerous, hazardous or detrimental to life or health.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landlord filed dispossession proceedings against defendant tenants for failure to pay rent. The tenants argued that N.Y. Soc. Welfare Law § 143-b permitted a tenant who was a welfare recipient to withhold payment of rent where there existed conditions in their accommodations that were dangerous, hazardous or detrimental to life or health. The tenants proved that the city inspector had uncovered such conditions in their buildings.


OVERVIEW: The tenants alleged that the violations uncovered by the city inspectors continued unabated, although the landlord contradicted their allegations without offering proof of repairs. The court held that such unsupported denials were insufficient to defeat the tenants' claim. The court rejected the landlord's argument that N.Y. Soc. Welfare Law § 143b was unconstitutional in that it permitted a taking of property. The court recognized that the government could not take one's property without due process, but held that due process did not mean the complete absence of restraint on property. The court stated that an owner held his property under the implied liability that his use could be regulated and that his use of it could not be injurious to the community. The court found that the government's police power extended to protection of housing and held that § 143b was constitutional and that there was no "taking of property" involved in its operation, because the landlord had only to keep his property in good condition to prevent a tenant from withholding the rent pursuant to the law's provisions. The court dismissed the dispossession proceedings without prejudice.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed without prejudice the dispossession proceedings filed by the landlord against welfare recipient tenants for failure to pay rent.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- withholding of payment of rent of tenant who was recipient of public welfare -- section 143-b of Social Welfare Law authorized public welfare official to withhold payment of rent of tenant who was recipient of public assistance if violations existed in building containing such recipient's housing accommodations; in summary proceedings, defense was that violations existed in building which were dangerous to health; adequate proof of existence of violations made by receipt of official records of Department of Buildings -- section 143-b was constitutional -- no basis for declaring statute unconstitutional by fact that Welfare Department took over burden of enforcement of section.

1. Section 143-b of the Social Welfare Law provided that public welfare officials might withhold payment of rent [***2] to a landlord for a tenant who was a recipient of public assistance where there was a violation of law in the building containing the housing accommodations occupied by the recipient of public welfare, which violation was dangerous or detrimental to health. In these summary proceedings for nonpayment of rent, the defense was that violations existed in the building which were dangerous to health. Adequate proof of the violations was made by the official records of the Department of Buildings of the City of New York, which were admissible pursuant to section 367 of the Civil Practice Act, and as an exception to the hearsay evidence rule as records kept in the regular course of business.

2. Section 143-b was constitutional. There was no taking of property without due process of law. Landlord was not permanently deprived of his rent. It was withheld until the violations were removed.

3. That the Welfare Department took over the burden of enforcement was no basis for declaring section 143-b unconstitutional.

 

2331 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Terrace Hotel Co. v. State


Claim No. 39895


Court of Claims of New York


40 Misc. 2d 978; 244 N.Y.S.2d 554; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2065



May 7, 1963 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion is granted upon the ground that the court does not have jurisdiction of the subject of the action.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant State moved for an order dismissing claimant hotel's claim for damages due to a permanent negative easement over its lands by the Superintendent of Public Works on the grounds that the court does not have jurisdiction of the subject of the action and that the claim does not set forth facts sufficient to constitute a cause of action.


OVERVIEW: The hotel filed a claim alleging that the State through the superintendent instituted appropriation proceedings against its predecessors in title, taking a permanent negative easement over the lands of the hotel, with the right of entry to remove and demolish any signs which might be placed thereon in violation of such easement. The hotel sought damages from the allegedly unlawful act. The court held that it had no authority to allow for recovery for the portion of the claim seeking compensation for legal services. The court found that that the filing of a taking map created a cloud upon title, even though the filing occurred because of the superintendent's misinterpretation of the extent of the powers granted to him. However, although the claim set forth facts sufficient to constitute a cause of action, the filing of the hotel's notice of intention and of the hotel's instant claim were belated. Regardless of the reason for filing the claim beyond the statutory two-year period, because the claim was belatedly filed, the court lacked jurisdiction of the subject of the action.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion.


HEADNOTES

State -- Court of Claims -- limitation of time -- recovery for legal services as part of claim cannot be granted by court -- Superintendent of Highways filed map for negative easement under section 30 of Highway Law; claim based on Superintendent's erroneous interpretation of section sufficient in law -- claim filed more than two years after filing of appropriation map is barred; claim is dismissed.

1. The State filed a map on May 16, 1959 taking a permanent negative easement over the lands of claimant's predecessors with the right of entry to remove and demolish any signs placed thereon in violation of the easement. By this claim, claimant sought damages resulting from the filing. That portion of the claim seeking recovery for legal services is dismissed. The court has no authority to allow it.

2. The Superintendent of Highways filed a map under an erroneous interpretation of the extent of the authority conferred by section 30 of the Highway Law. A cloud on title was thereby created. The Superintendent, while acting in the scope of his duties, is exonerated [***2] from civil liability by his erroneous interpretation of the statute, but the State is not exonerated for an unauthorized appropriation proceeding. The claim sufficiently alleges a cause of action.

3. The original claim for compensation for the damages resulting from the appropriation was timely filed on June 13, 1960. It was dismissed on September 18, 1961 without prejudice to filing claims for damages sustained by claimants. A notice of intention to file a claim was filed on October 3, 1961 and the claim filed on November 30, 1961. Whatever reason may exist or be apparent for the filing beyond the statutory two-year period, it was not timely filed more than two years after the accrual of the claim. The claim is dismissed.

 

2332 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


378 Realty Corp. v. New York City Rent & Rehabilitation Administration


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


39 Misc. 2d 30; 240 N.Y.S.2d 58; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2073



May 3, 1963 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Plaintiffs' cross motion is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the New York City, New York Rent and Rehabilitation Administration and others, filed a motion for judgment on the pleadings under N.Y. R. Civ. Prac. 112 or for a declaratory judgment in their favor pursuant to N.Y. R. Civ. Prac. 113 in an action of plaintiff, a realty corporation and others, seeking a declaration that multiple laws and regulations relating to rent and eviction were unconstitutional. Plaintiffs sought summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs filed a complaint under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 seeking declarations that N.Y. Emergency Housing Rent Control Law §2(2)(b), 1946 N.Y. Laws 274, as amended by 1950 N.Y. Laws 250, and N.Y. Rent & Eviction Regs. § 3(7) of the New York Temporary State Housing Rent Commission violated U.S. Const. amend. XIV and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6, and that New York City, N.Y., Local Law No. 20, § Y41-3.0(e)(2)(c) and New York City, N.Y., Rent, Eviction, & Rehabilitation Regs. § 3(d) were enacted without a basis and violated U.S. Const. amend. XIV and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6. Plaintiffs also sought a permanent injunction. The parties filed cross-motions. The court held that that based on prior adjudications, the declarations sought by plaintiffs were without merit. The legislature had a right to exercise its police powers in what it deemed was an emergency situation. That was true even if plaintiffs' properties had not been subject to regulation with regard to rent in the past. The fact that plaintiffs could not in the future run their operations free from control was not the denial of due process. The court granted defendant's application for declaratory relief.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendants' motion for a declaratory judgment in their favor and denied plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- sections of New York City and State rent control laws and New York City and State regulations defining hotels were constitutional.

In an action to declare unconstitutional (1) so much of section 2 (subd. 2, par. [b]) of the Emergency Housing Rent Control Law (L. 1946, ch. 274, as amd. by L. 1950, ch. 250) and subdivision 7 of section 3 of the State Rent and Eviction Regulations of the Temporary State Housing Rent Commission as adopted and in effect on April 30, 1962; and (2) section Y41-3.0 (subd. e, par. 2, cl. [c]) of Local Law No. 20 of the Local Laws of the City of New York for 1962 and subdivision d of section 3 of the City Rent, Eviction and Rehabilitation Regulations, all defining hotels, defendants were granted summary judgment declaring the sections constitutional.

 

2333 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Koltun v. Board of Education


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


39 Misc. 2d 985; 242 N.Y.S.2d 246; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2114



April 22, 1963 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Application for an order denied. The petition is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner teacher commenced a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding, which contended that N.Y. Educ. Law § 2569-a was unconstitutional and sought an order requiring respondents, the Board of Education of the City of New York and certain officials, to discontinue licensing examinations, to establish eligible lists, and to make appointments to regular teaching positions pursuant to § 2569-a.


OVERVIEW: The teacher maintained that § 2569-a was unconstitutional and sought an order requiring the Board and the officials to discontinue its licensing examinations, to establish eligible lists, and to make appointments to regular teaching positions. Section 2569-a allowed the board of examiners in city school districts having a population of 1,000,000 or more to conduct examinations for licenses as regular teachers that were limited to experienced substitute teachers. N.Y. Const. art. V, § 6 provided that all appointments and promotions in the civil service had to be made according to merit and fitness, which was to be ascertained, so far as practicable, by competitive examinations. The court found that N.Y. Const. art. V, § 6 did not prohibit the legislature from enacting a law that opened a competitive examination to only experienced substitute teachers. In that regard, it noted that N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 50 gave municipal commissions and the state civil service department the authority to set minimum qualifications for competitive examinations. Moreover, the tests conducted pursuant to N.Y. Educ. Law § 2569-a were competitive in that they utilized an objective standard.


OUTCOME: The court denied the teacher's application for an order and dismissed his petition.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- examinations -- Education Law ( § 2569-a) providing for civil service examination for position of teacher in New York City schools was not unconstitutional -- limitation of examination to "experienced substitute teachers" did not make section unconstitutional -- no constitutional requirement that test be written.

1. Section 2569-a of the Education Law, applicable to New York City, directed the board of examiners to conduct examinations for licenses as regular teachers "open only to experienced substitute teachers", the examination to be based "upon an appraisal of record, classroom visits, an oral interview and physical and medical examination". Such section was constitutional.

2. The limitation of the examination to "experienced substitute teachers" did not make it a "closed examination". The State Civil Service Commission and municipal comissions had power to set minimum qualifications for competitive examinations. The Constitution (art. V, § 6) did not prohibit the Legislature from enacting a law opening a competitive examination only to experienced substitute teachers.

3. There was no constitutional [***2] requirement for a written test. The statute required the board of examiners to conduct a competitive examination. The section did not require the board of examiners to depart from an objective standard in conducting the examination.

 

2334 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Fogg v. Schenectady


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Schenectady County


38 Misc. 2d 874; 239 N.Y.S.2d 37; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2150



April 4, 1963 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motion denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner building owner filed suit against respondent city seeking to challenge the constitutionality of N.Y. Mult. Resid. Law § 31 and moving to set aside an order of the city's building inspector requiring the building owner to comply with the provisions of the statute for fire safety. He argued that the expenditures required by the statute and order constituted a deprivation of property.


OVERVIEW: The statute in question required that stairs leading from the cellar to the floor above in old multiple dwellings had to be enclosed with fire-retarded partitions and equipped with fire-retarded self-closing doors. The building owner argued that the statute and order required purposeless acts because the cellar of his property contained no fire hazard. The court found that: (1) there were no exceptions to the statute, which covered all old multiple dwellings; (2) the fact that the statute might affect the building owner adversely was not sufficient by itself to render the statute unconstitutional; (3) the proper remedy for an grievance about the nature of the statute was through legislative action; and (4) it was improper to intervene in the proper application of the constitutional statute.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion to set aside the order and held the statute constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- multiple residences -- section 31 of Multiple Residence Law was constitutional.

 Section 31 of the Multiple Residence Law which provided that "Every stair leading from a cellar to the floor above in all old multiple dwellings shall be enclosed with fire-retarded partitions and shall be equipped with a fire-retarded self closing door located as the department may approve" was constitutional.

 

2335 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Doundoulakis


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York


38 Misc. 2d 984; 239 N.Y.S.2d 452; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2173



March 28, 1963 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, defendant's motion to dismiss, made at the close of the entire case, is granted with respect to Docket No. 2135, and the defendant is acquitted on that information. The motion is denied with respect to Docket No. 2134 and Docket No. 2136 and defendant is found guilty on both informations.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved to dismiss three informations, which charged him with violating N.Y. Penal Law § 962-a. That statute provided that any employer (or its president, secretary, or treasurer) who was party to an agreement to pay benefits, or wage supplements as defined in N.Y. Lab. Law § 222, and who failed to pay such benefits or furnish such supplements within 30 days after such payments were required to be made was guilty of a misdemeanor.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was a former president of a corporation that was a party to two agreements, requiring it to pay benefits and wage supplements. One information charged that the corporation failed to pay an amount due to an annuity fund of the electrical industry's joint industrial board. Another information charged that the corporation failed to pay an employee an amount due him in vacation pay. The third information charged that the corporation failed to pay an amount due to the electrical manufacturing industry's employees' retirement fund. The court rejected defendant's attack on the constitutional validity of the statute, finding it sufficiently definite to meet due process requirements. It also found no violation of N.Y. Const. art. III, §16 because there was no incorporation of N.Y. Lab. Law § 220 in N.Y. Penal Law § 962-a. The court denied the motion to dismiss the informations relating to the amounts due the annuity fund and the retirement fund because it was conceded that such amounts were due and owing for more than 30 days. It dismissed the indictment relating to vacation pay because the record demonstrated that the employee had been paid for his vacation.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the two indictments relating to payments the corporation owed to the annuity fund and retirement fund and found defendant guilty on both informations. It granted defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment relating to vacation pay the corporation owed to an employee and acquitted defendant on that information.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- failure to pay wage supplements -- section 962-a of Penal Law provided that it was misdemeanor to fail to pay wage supplements as defined in section 220 of Labor Law; two statutes gave reasonable notice of things to be done and avoided; statute not in conflict with requirements of due process -- section 962-a not unconstitutional as incorporating section by reference -- defendant, president of corporation, was guilty of violating section by company's failing to pay amounts to Annuity Fund and Retirement Fund -- not guilty of failing to pay vacation benefits to named employee.

1. Section 962-a of the Penal Law provided that any employer who was a party to an agreement to pay or provide benefits, or wage supplements as defined in section 220 of the Labor Law, and who failed to pay the amounts necessary to provide such benefits within 30 days after they were required [***2] to be made, was guilty of a misdemeanor. The supplements defined in section 220 of the Labor Law were the same wage supplements referred to in section 962-a of the Penal Law. Read together, the statutes gave reasonable notice of what they required be done and avoided. The statute did not conflict with the requirements of due process.

2. Section 962-a was not unconstitutional as incorporating therein section 220 of the Labor Law, in whole or in part. There was merely the borrowing of a definition.

3. Defendant was president of a company which entered into an agreement with a labor union providing, among other things, for the payment of moneys into an Annuity Fund and a Retirement Fund and for vacation pay for employee members of the union. It was alleged that all payments referred to were unpaid for more than 30 days. Section 962-a further provided that, where the employer was a corporation, the president, secretary, treasurer or officers exercising corresponding functions was each guilty of a misdemeanor. With respect to moneys due an Annuity Fund, the corporation, while defendant was president, entered into an agreement with the union to amortize its delinquency at the rate [***3] of $ 700 per month, which was paid while defendant was president. The agreement had no effect on the nature or amount of the underlying obligation. At the time the agreement to amortize was made, the whole amount was due. Defendant's claim that he was not guilty of violating section 962-a because the amounts for amortizing were paid while he was president was therefore rejected. He was found guilty.

4. He was also guilty with respect to the sums due the Retirement Fund.

5. With respect to the charge that defendant failed to provide vacation pay for a named employee, defendant was not guilty. The employee took a vacation and was paid therefor. No explanation was offered as to what the sum of $ 84 claimed represented.

 

2336 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Follar v. Finkelstein


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, Kings County


38 Misc. 2d 791; 239 N.Y.S.2d 835; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2175



March 27, 1963 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The defendant's motion to dismiss the information is denied and the defendant is found guilty.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with a violation of the Sabbath or Blue Laws, N.Y. Penal Law § 2147. Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the information.


OVERVIEW: Defendant owned and operated a grocery store. He was a follower of the Hebrew Faith, which required him to observe his holy time between sunset on Friday and darkness on Saturday. Defendant sold grocery items to two individuals on Sunday afternoon in violation of the Sabbath Law. Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the information. The court denied the motion. The court found that it was not a defense to the Sabbath Law that defendant observed a day other than Sunday as his Sabbath. The court found that although people who observe Sunday as their Sabbath were not offended, defendant's actions were still prohibited. The court found that the statute was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the information and found defendant guilty of violating the Sabbath or Blue Law.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- sales on Sunday -- defendant, who sold milk, bread, soap, canned foods, corn flakes and crackers to customers on Sunday is found guilty of violating section 2147 of Penal Law, although he observes period from sunset on Friday to darkness on Saturday as his holy time and cannot survive economically without conducting such business on Saturday after sundown and Sunday.

Defendant who sold milk, bread, soap, canned foods, corn flakes and crackers to customers on Sunday is found guilty of a violation of section 2147 of the Penal Law, although he keeps, as his holy time, the period from sunset on Friday to darkness on Saturday, and, upon the evidence, cannot survive economically without conducting business on Saturday after sundown and Sunday. The statute has been held to be constitutional, and the court may not sit in review of the discretion of the Legislature in exercising its constitutional powers nor may it overlook the established law on the subject. The court may not usurp the functions of the Legislature.

 

2337 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Estate of Finkelstein


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Surrogate's Court of New York, Rockland County


40 Misc. 2d 910; 245 N.Y.S.2d 225; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2201



March 18, 1963 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Petition denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner estate of decedent partner applied for an order to modify the pro forma taxing order (order) issued concerning the deceased partner's estate that was granted in favor of respondent New York Tax Commission (commission).


OVERVIEW: After the court issued the order, the estate's attorneys' realized that certain parcels of foreign realty owned by the partnerships were titled in the names of individual partners. Accordingly, the estate sought modification of the order to reduce the valuation of decedent's interests in three partnerships in order to remove from consideration the value of the realty. The court found that this was an immaterial mistake of fact because the parcels were partnership property. The court declined the estate's petition that sought to correct this error. The court distinguished between the property of the partnerships, the partner's interest in specific partnership property, and the partner's interest in the partnership. The court considered the development of the law respecting these categories of property interests. The court found that the partner's interest was not worth taxing and went to the surviving partners under The Uniform Partnership Law (Law) § 51(2)(d) adopted in New York. The court found that the partner's interest was not taxable under the Law §§ 50, 51, 52. The court found that the partner's interest in the partnership existed in intangible personal property.


OUTCOME: The court denied the application of the estate for the order for modification of the pro forma taxing order rendered regarding the deceased partner's estate, which was entered for the commission.


HEADNOTES

Partnership -- nature of partnership property -- interest of partner in partnership property, including real property owned by partnership, is personal property; in estate tax proceeding, what was taxed was decedent's interest in partnership property, despite fact that title to partnership's real property was held in individual names -- not unconstitutional to tax decedent's indirect interests in foreign realty.

1. An application to modify a pro forma taxing order so as to reduce the valuation of decedent's interests in three partnerships by eliminating from consideration the value of foreign realty owned by those partnerships, title to which was held in the individual names of the partners is denied. What is taxed is the partner's interest in the partnership and such interest is personal property.

2. It is not unconstitutional to tax decedent's indirect interests in foreign realty, which are being taxed through inclusion of the realty in the partnership balance sheets.

 

2338 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Ricapito v. People


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


38 Misc. 2d 710; 238 N.Y.S.2d 864; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2207



March 13, 1963 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner husband was indicted for assault in the first degree after he stabbed his wife with a knife. The husband was arraigned in the county court. He pleaded not guilty and was remanded pending trial. He filed a petition against respondents, the State of New York and others, seeking to compel the county court to transfer the proceeding to the family court and to prohibit the State from prosecuting him in the county court.


OVERVIEW: N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 812 provided that the family court had exclusive jurisdiction over any proceeding concerning acts that would constitute an assault between spouses. N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 813 provided for the transfer to family court of any criminal complaint charging an assault between spouses. The husband argued that these statutes required the State to transfer his criminal assault case to family court. The court disagreed. The stated legislative intention of these statutes was to substitute a civil proceeding for criminal cases not because the State had been affronted, but because the complainant needed help. This objective would not be consistent with the substitution of a civil proceeding for a criminal trial in cases where the crime was infamous and would have found its way into the criminal court because of the very nature of the offense. Moreover, the term "exclusive jurisdiction" as applied to inferior courts had been held not to limit the jurisdiction of courts of general jurisdiction and the identical term, as used in the Family Court Act, should be considered merely as defining jurisdiction between that court and other inferior courts.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petition.


HEADNOTES

Courts -- Family Court -- Family Court lacked jurisdiction of crime of assault with knife with intent to kill, despite fact that victim was petitioner assailant's wife; grant of exclusive jurisdiction of acts constituting assault between spouses did not apply to assault with knife for which petitioner assailant was indicted.

The new Family Court had no jurisdiction of the crime of assault with a knife with intent to kill, for which crime petitioner was indicted by the Grand Jury and the case transferred to the County Court for trial despite the fact that the victim was petitioner's wife. Sections 812 and 813 of the Family Court Act granting exclusive jurisdiction to the Family Court concerning acts which would constitute an assault between spouses and requiring transfer to the Family Court of criminal complaints charging assaults did not apply to the crime for which petitioner was indicted.

 

2339 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Pam


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York


38 Misc. 2d 296; 238 N.Y.S.2d 363; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2233



February 28, 1963 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The application to dismiss or quash the proceedings is, in all respects, denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to quash or dismiss a charge against him for selling canned goods on Sunday in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 2147.


OVERVIEW: Defendant operated a grocery store. He was charged with selling canned goods on Sunday in violation of § 2147. Defendant filed a motion to quash or dismiss the charge. Upon review, the court denied the motion. The court held that Sunday laws were not unconstitutional. The court ruled that an individual claiming that a Sunday law was enforced in a discriminatory manner had a heavy burden of establishing conscious, intentional discrimination. The court determined that defendant had alleged that other unrelated businesses were permitted to do business on Sundays. However, the court held that defendant failed to offer any proof that competing grocery stores were permitted to do business on Sundays. Therefore, the court ruled that defendant failed to prove conscious, intentional discrimination in the enforcement of § 2147.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to quash or dismiss the charge against him for selling canned goods on Sunday.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- sale on Sunday -- law prohibiting sales on Sunday was constitutional -- defendant failed to prove discriminatory enforcement of Sunday Law.

1. The law rendering illegal the sale of certain goods on Sunday (Penal Law, § 2147), here, canned goods, was constitutional.

2. Defendant failed to sustain the burden of proving such discriminatory enforcement of the statute as to require quashing of the prosecution. Defendant failed to show that some stores were permitted to sell canned goods on Sunday while others were not permitted to make such sales. All that defendant proved was some nonenforcement of the statute and that some other and nonrelated businesses were permitted to operate on Sundays.

 

2340 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Carroll


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Criminal Term, Kings County


38 Misc. 2d 630; 238 N.Y.S.2d 640; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2234



February 28, 1963 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to suppress evidence of property seized pursuant to a search warrant from his trial for homicide.


OVERVIEW: Police officers seized property from defendant pursuant to a search warrant. Defendant was charged with homicide. He filed a motion to suppress evidence of the seized property from his trial. Upon review, the court denied the motion and ordered the State to return to defendant all property that was not described in the warrant. The court held that an affidavit established the requisite probable cause for the issuance of the warrant. The court ruled that N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 792(4) extended the right of search and seizure to property constituting evidence of a crime or tending to show that a particular person had committed a crime. The court determined that § 792(4) was not unconstitutional and was a legitimate exercise of the State's police power. The court held that the same prerequisite of probable cause required for all searches and seizures was fully applicable to evidence seized pursuant to § 792(4). The court ruled that the officers who executed the warrant improperly seized photographs and other items that were not described in the warrant.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to suppress the evidence from his trial for homicide. The court ordered the State to return to defendant property that was not described in the warrant.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- unlawful search and seizure -- search warrant validly directed seizure of shotgun shells used in homicide (Code Crim. Pro., § 792, subd. 4) -- but other pellets, gun case, photographs, etc., not specifically designated in warrant, must be returned.

1. A search warrant validly, reasonably, and constitutionally (Code Crim. Pro., § 793; § 792, subd. 4, as amd. by L. 1962, ch. 542; U.S. Const., 4th Amdt.; N. Y. Const., art. I, § 12) authorized the seizure of "a quantity of 20 gauge shells used in a 20 gauge shot-gun, which was used as a weapon in committing a homicide".

2. With the District Attorney consenting thereto, the court orders the return of all property not specifically designated in the search warrant, namely two photographs, a sales order blank, a leather gun case, a gun cleaning rod, and 10 pellets found in cellar.

 

2341 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Jenad, Inc. v. Scarsdale


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


38 Misc. 2d 658; 238 N.Y.S.2d 156; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2235



February 28, 1963 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Thus, plaintiff's motion is denied, and defendants' cross motion for summary judgment dismissing the complaint, is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff developer and defendant village filed motions for summary judgment in the developer's action seeking to recover money that it voluntarily paid to the village pursuant to N.Y. Village Law § 179-1.


OVERVIEW: The developer paid money to the village under § 179-1 as a condition to obtain the village planning commission's approval of its proposed plan for development of land. The developer thereafter alleged that the statute was illegal and sought to recover the money it paid and to have the statute declared null and void. Both parties filed a motion for summary judgment. The court granted summary judgment in favor of the village. The court held that whether or not the statute was illegal, the developer could not recover its payment. The court held that money paid under an unconstitutional or invalid statute or ordinance, without circumstances of compulsion, and paid under a mistake of law could not be recovered.The court held that the developer paid the money without duress and without protest. The developer thereafter received benefit because its lands were freed from any reservations by the village. The court therefore held that the developer was estopped from questioning the statute's validity.


OUTCOME: The court granted summary judgment to the village and dismissed the developer's complaint.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- land subdivider required to reserve recreational area -- whether or not village validly (Village Law, § 179-1) required $ 250 per lot for waiver of its resolution requiring 10% of subdivision to be reserved for park purposes, developer cannot recover $ 6,000 which he thus voluntarily paid for his own profits.

Pursuant to the Village Law ( § 179-1), a village's code required developers of proposed subdivisions to set aside 10% of the acreage for park and playground purposes. A developer's map of section 1 of his lots, with 10% of the area marked for park purposes, was thus approved by the village's planning commission, with the understanding that a further decision would be made if and when he filed a plat of section 2 of his lots. Subsequently he filed his map section 2; and the village and its planning commission waived their requirement that 10% of the acreage be reserved for park purposes, upon condition that he deposit with the village $ 250 a lot. Thereupon he paid, for such waiver of his 24 lots, $ 6,000. He has [***2] since sold 19 lots. It is unnecessary to determine whether the village's resolution is arbitrary and unconstitutional. The developer cannot attack the validity of the resolution under which he voluntarily made a profitable business arrangement. His action to recover his $ 6,000 payment is summarily dismissed (Rules Civ. Prac., rule 113).
