People v. Considine


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Seneca County


38 Misc. 2d 625; 237 N.Y.S.2d 749; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2248



February 20, 1963 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to set aside an indictment found against him by the Grand Jury of Seneca County (New York), claiming that the grand jury stenographer was not a citizen and resident of Seneca County. Defendant claimed that county citizenship was a prerequisite qualification and that the taking of testimony by that stenographer violated N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 313(2).


OVERVIEW: The Grand Jury of Seneca County found an indictment against defendant, which he sought to set aside upon the ground that the grand jury stenographer was not a citizen and resident of Seneca County. The court declined to set aside the indictment, finding that the stenographer was acting under at least some color of title as grand jury stenographer even though his appointment was open to criticism and attack regarding its legality. The court found that the stenographer resided in the county that adjoined Seneca County and that he had taken an oath to be the temporary grand jury stenographer for Seneca County. His term was to expire at "judge's discretion." The court reviewed past decisions that held that a stenographer's lack of county citizenship or residency did not invalidate an indictment and then concluded that the stenographer was a de facto official. Lastly, it noted that in so deciding, it was not giving its approval of a continuance of the use of nonresident county stenographers.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- indictment -- indictment was not rendered invalid by presence, at Grand Jury session, of temporary Grand Jury stenographer who was not citizen and resident of county.

An indictment was not rendered invalid by the presence of a stenographer at the session of the Grand Jury who was not a citizen and resident of the county wherein the indictment was rendered. The stenographer took an oath of office as temporary Grand Jury stenographer but the oath did not contain the proviso (Code Crim. Pro., § 952-r) "that he will keep secret all matters and things occurring before such grand juries". He was a de facto official.

 

2343 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Codarre


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Dutchess County


38 Misc. 2d 445; 237 N.Y.S.2d 389; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2277



February 8, 1963 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a juvenile, sought a ruling on his third application for coram nobis relief, seeking an order granting him a hearing on the question of whether a State procedure which permitted a plea of guilty to murder in any degree by an epileptic 13-year-old violated the due process clause of the Fourteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a 13-year-old epileptic, pled guilty to murder in the second degree. He was convicted on his plea and sentenced. Defendant filed three separate applications for coram nobis relief. The first two were denied in separate proceedings. The court denied the third application, holding that he was legally sane, as defined by N.Y. Penal Law § 1120, when he committed the murder. The court held that defendant knew the nature and quality of the act, and he knew that the act was wrong. The court held that the trial court's acceptance of a plea of guilty to murder in the second degree did not violate the due process clause of the Fourteenth Amendment of the Constitution of the United States. The court held that defendant was represented by two competent counsel with full knowledge of the facts at the time the plea of guilty was entered. The court held that the trial court accepted the plea after a full disclosure of all of the evidence and after due consideration of the opinions of four psychiatrists as to defendant's mental condition.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's application for coram nobis relief.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- coram nobis -- 13-year-old boy, who pleaded guilty to murder in second degree, was not entitled to benefit of statute subsequently enacted which provided that such boy be treated as juvenile delinquent -- though defendant was suffering from epilepsy, crime was not committed in epileptic furor; defendant knew nature and quality of act and knew it was wrong -- acceptance of plea did not violate defendant's right to due process of law -- no errors not on face of record occurred; application for writ of error coram nobis denied.

1. A 13-year-old boy, who was convicted of murder in the second degree on his plea of guilty, the indictment charging murder in the first degree, was not entitled to the benefit of section 486 of the Penal Law, subsequently enacted, which provided that a child of 13 years of age be treated as a juvenile delinquent.

2. On an application by defendant for a writ of error coram nobis, the evidence at the hearing showed that, even though defendant suffered from epilepsy, the act of murder was not committed during an epileptic furor for there was no amnesia or lack of memory of the events. Defendant then knew the nature and quality of the act [***2] and knew that the act was wrong.

3. At the time defendant pleaded guilty he was represented by two competent attorneys. The acceptance of the plea did not violate the due process clause of the Fourteenth Amendment of the Constitution of the United States. It was accepted after full disclosure of all the evidence and after considering the opinions of four psychiatrists as to defendant's mental condition.

4. No errors occurred not appearing on the face of the record, and there was no denial of due process. The application for the writ was denied.

 

2344 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Caparco v. Kaplan


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Monroe County


38 Misc. 2d 1058; 237 N.Y.S.2d 448; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2284



February 6, 1963 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city civil service commission sought judgment in their action seeking an order compelling respondents, a state civil service commission and a county civil service commission, to accept the city civil service commission's rules, to approve said rules, to terminate its administration of the city's civil service, and to turn over the city's civil service employees' records under N.Y. Opt. Cty. Gov't. Law § 1008 (1961).


OVERVIEW: A city council, pursuant to § 1008, elected to withdraw its civil service system from the city's civil service commission and to allow the county civil service commission to administer its civil service. The city civil service commission was abolished. Thereafter a new city council appointed a new city civil service commission and purported to retransfer the civil service administration to it. The state civil service commission refused to accept the new city civil service commission's rules on the ground that there was no authority for the re-establishment of a municipal civil service commission. The city commission filed suit to compel respondents to accept the rules and terminate the county's administration of the city's civil service system. The court dismissed the suit, holding that § 1008 was constitutional. The court held that the action of the city in electing to have its civil service administered by the county civil service commission was both valid and properly taken. The court held that § 1008 did not violate N.Y. Const. art. III, § 15, N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 11, or N.Y. City Home Rule Law § 11(1)(b).


OUTCOME: The court dismissed petitioner's suit.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- civil service system -- section 1008 of Optional County Government Law authorized city council in city wholly within county to elect to transfer administration of city civil service system to County Civil Service Commission; section did not relate to property, affairs or government of city, was general law, and was constitutional -- city message of necessity adopted by City of Rochester for section was proper; petitioners could not attack adequacy of city message -- City of Rochester's council, in December, 1961, transferred administration of civil service to county commission; new council, in January, 1962 purported to reinstate City Civil Service Commission; State commission properly ruled that city lacked authority to re-establish city commission.

1. Section 1008 of the Optional County Government Law was enacted by chapter 565 of the Laws of 1961 and provided that the council in a city wholly within a county might change the existing civil service administration and elect that the Civil Service Law in the city be administered by the County Civil Service Commission. Section 11 of article IX of the Constitution provided that the Legislature act "in [***2] relation to the property, affairs or government of any city only by general laws which shall in terms and in effect apply alike to all cities" but was not restricted to enact laws "relating to matters other than the property, affairs or government of cities". An act dealing with the civil service system was a matter of general, State-wide concern rather than a matter of purely local concern. Accordingly, section 1008 of the Optional County Government Law did not relate to the "property, affairs or government of any city", and the Legislature was free to enact the section without a city message. Moreover, section 1008 was not a law local in effect but applied to any county, even assuming that Monroe County was presently the only county within the scope of the section. The section was constitutional.

2. Moreover the city message of necessity adopted by the City of Rochester was proper. Section 23 of the City Home Rule Law provided that the validity of an act passed pursuant to such request "shall not be subject to review by the courts on the ground that the necessity alleged in the request did not exist or was not properly established by the facts recited." Petitioners therefore [***3] could not attack the adequacy of the city message.

3. Pursuant to section 1008, the outgoing City Council of the City of Rochester in December, 1961 elected to place its civil service system under the administration of the County Civil Service Commission. In January, 1962 the new City Council, by a local law and ordinance, appointed a new City Civil Service Commission and purported to revoke the action of the prior council. The State Civil Service Commission properly declined to accept new rules adopted by the new City Civil Service Commission on the ground that there was no authority for the re-establishment of a municipal civil service commission. Section 11 (subd. 1, par. [b]) of the City Home Rule Law gave the local Legislature power to amend local laws dealing with compensation, number, terms of office of officers and employees and provided that such local laws may change any provision of an act of the Legislature theretofore enacted. Such section did not give rise to the right to supersede section 1008. Section 1008 did not deal with the matters covered by section 11.

 

2345 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Mary Chess, Inc. v. Glen Cove


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


38 Misc. 2d 555; 237 N.Y.S.2d 607; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2291



February 4, 1963 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, the landowners and the tenants, sought a declaration that an amendment to a city's building zone ordinance was unconstitutional and void insofar as it purported to upzone plaintiffs' properties from a light manufacturing district to a single family residence district in their suit against defendants, a city and others.


OVERVIEW: The city amended its building zone ordinance so as to upzone plaintiffs' properties from a light manufacturing district to a single family residence district. Plaintiffs brought a declaratory judgment action against the city and others, seeking a declaration that the amendment was unconstitutional as confiscatory as applied to their properties. The court declared the amendment unconstitutional as to the unimproved properties and dismissed the suit as to the improved properties. The court held that the value of the unimproved properties, which were surrounded by properties used for industrial properties, was completely destroyed because it was not suitable for use as residential property or for small businesses. The court held that the amendment was confiscatory as applied to those parcels. The court held that the landowners of the improved properties had not sustained their burden to prove that the buildings thereon could not have been reasonably and economically converted to such use for scientific or research laboratories.


OUTCOME: The court declared the zoning ordinance amendment unconstitutional as applied to unimproved properties. The court dismissed the suit as to improved properties.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- zoning -- city amended zoning ordinance so as to change zone of 19 1/2-acre oval tract from light manufacturing district to one-half acre single-family district; owners of industrial buildings therein acquired nonconforming uses -- amendment of ordinance was confiscatory as to unimproved parcels surrounded by industrial plants -- amendment was not confiscatory as to improved parcels.

1. Defendant city amended its zoning ordinance so as to change the zone of a 19 1/2-acre oval tract formerly in a light manufacturing district to a one-half acre single-family district. Several plaintiffs, owners of industrial establishments therein, acquired nonconforming uses.

2. Two plaintiffs owned unimproved parcels in the center of the tract. One was larger than the other. The larger parcel was surrounded by factories, as was the smaller parcel. Neither parcel was adapted to single-family dwelling use, to municipal building use, to school, college or university use or church use. While they could be used for a greenhouse or nursery, or a scientific or research laboratory, they could not be so used economically or profitably. The elimination of a normal market [***2] for the land so depressed its value as to eliminate profitability. Under their present residential zoning, surrounded by industrial use, neither parcel was worth a fraction of its assessed valuation. The ordinance was confiscatory as to such parcels.

3. With respect to the improved parcels, plaintiffs failed to meet the burden of showing that the ordinance presently invaded their property rights. Plaintiffs failed to prove that the existing buildings could not be converted reasonably and economically to scientific or research laboratory use. Moreover, the proof was insufficient to show that the value of the industrial buildings as nonconforming uses was so disproportionate to their value as permitted uses as to amount to confiscation.

 

2346 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Stover


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Criminal Court of the City of New York, New York County


38 Misc. 2d 668; 240 N.Y.S.2d 94; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2294



February 1, 1963 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, defendant's motion to strike the evidence claimed to be the product of the illegal search, upon which decision was reserved, is denied. Defendant's motion to dismiss, made at the close of the entire case, is granted upon the ground that the People have failed to establish defendant's guilt beyond a reasonable doubt.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A police officer found defendant in the area of a car accident. A button from the car matched defendant's buttons. Defendant was charged with driving while intoxicated, leaving the scene of an accident, and driving without a license. Defendant brought an unsuccessful motion to strike the evidence claimed to be the product of the illegal search, but the decision was reserved. At the close of the case, defendant brought a motion to dismiss.


OVERVIEW: The officer found the wrecked car with no occupants, but located defendant less than a block from the accident. The officer also found defendant's learner's permit and fountain pen on the passenger's side of the front seat. Defendant admitted that the permit and the fountain pen belonged to him. A search of defendant revealed the car's registration, key, and insurance policy. The court denied the motion to strike, but granted the motion to dismiss. The court held that an essential link in the chain of the state's evidence was missing. The officer's testimony did not exclude the possibility that there had been another occupant of the car. For the court to have concluded that defendant was the sole occupant of the car would have required the heaping of inference upon inference. Thus, the court held that the state failed to establish defendant's guilt beyond a reasonable doubt under any construction of the evidence.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion to strike, but granted his motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- reasonable doubt that defendant was driving -- defendant's button and pen on front seat of wrecked car, and other circumstances, did not prove beyond reasonable doubt that he drove car.

At about 2:30 a.m. a police officer in a patrol car found a lamppost knocked over, and, across the street, on the sidewalk, an automobile with its front smashed and its back against a building. The car was smoldering and smoking, the motor was running, and there was no key in the ignition. To an experienced policeman, all this suggested a car theft and joy ride. On the front seat, there was a button on the driver's side and a fountain pen on the passenger's side. No one was in sight. But less than a block away he found the defendant seated on a step at the entrance to the subway. A button was missing from defendant's left coat sleeve. The [***2] other buttons on the sleeve exactly matched the one found in the car. The policeman took the defendant to the station house and there arrested him on charges of driving while intoxicated, driving without a license, and leaving the scene of an accident. Defendant was then searched. On his person were found the keys to said car, the car registration in a woman's name, and a Motor Vehicle Bureau learner's permit issued to the defendant. He admitted that the fountain pen was his; but he denied any connection with the accident. At the trial, the owner of the car testified that she had given the defendant permission to drive her car and to use the key and registration, and that he had paid her in full for the car when it was found to be a total loss; but she quickly withdrew and changed her testimony that the defendant had admitted driving the car. Defendant offered no testimony. The trial court, without a jury, found that the defendant was in the front seat of the car at the time of the accident, but that the circumstantial evidence did not prove beyond a reasonable doubt that the defendant was behind the wheel or that there was no other person in the car. Defendant's motion, made [***3] at the close of the entire case, to dismiss the information, was granted.

 

2347 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Port Authority Trans-Hudson Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


38 Misc. 2d 412; 239 N.Y.S.2d 782; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2350



January 15, 1963 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a condemnation action, condemnees, a New York corporation and a New Jersey corporation, filed a motion for an order directing condemnor, a port authority, to make payments in advance of the final determination of their damages pursuant to 1947 N.Y. Laws ch. 819, § 1(x). The court issued its findings and ruling on the motions.


OVERVIEW: The corporations owned large office buildings that were condemned. The port authority took possession of the properties. The corporations sought advance payment of 60 percent of the assessed valuations of their respective properties pursuant to § 1(x). The port authority argued that the corporations were not entitled to the advance payment because it did not have a certificate from its corporation counsel that showed that the person, to whom payment was to be made, was the person legally entitled to receive the payment, as required by the last sentence of § 1(x). The court granted the New York corporation's motion for advance payment but denied the New Jersey corporation's motion without prejudice. The court held that if the failure of the port authority's general counsel to furnish the statutory certificate was arbitrary and capricious, and without substantial basis, the absence of the certificate could be disregarded. The court found that the failure to furnish the certificate for the New York property was arbitrary and capricious, but that it was justified for the New Jersey property because there were several open issues regarding title to the property.


OUTCOME: The New York corporation's motion for an advance partial payment of its condemnation award was granted. The New Jersey corporation's motion for an advance partial payment of its condemnation award was denied without prejudice.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Eminent domain -- Port of New York Authority -- advance payment of 60% of assessed value -- despite pending appeals, advance payment to mortgagee is ordered; general counsel cannot arbitrarily withhold his certificate of title (L. 1947, ch. 819, § 1, subd. [x]).

Port Authority Trans-Hudson Corporation is a corporation wholly owned by the Port of New York Authority (L. 1962, ch. 209, § 12). By court order it has acquired title and has taken possession of certain realty in New York belonging to Hudson & Manhattan Corporation and Hudson Rapid Tubes Corporation. Despite the pendency of appeals attacking the validity of the condemnation, the latter corporations are entitled to advance payment of 60% "of the assessed value of the real property taken"; and the Port Authority's general counsel cannot arbitrarily withhold his "certificate * * * showing * * * the person legally entitled to receive" such payment (L. 1947, ch. 819, § 1, subd. [x]) -- in this case, the mortgagee of the properties of Hudson & Manhattan Corporation. However, the application of Hudson Rapid Tubes Corporation is denied, without prejudice to a renewal on papers showing either that there is no merit [***2] to the title questions raised by general counsel, or that the alleged defects have been cleared up, or showing the maximum amounts of the possible interests of others in the properties, or on obtaining the certificate of the general counsel.

 

2348 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Fritch


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Onondaga County


38 Misc. 2d 333; 236 N.Y.S.2d 706; 1963 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2372



January 11, 1963 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The judgments of conviction appealed from are reversed on the law and on the facts, the information is dismissed, and the fines imposed upon the defendants are remitted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed from judgments of the Court of Special Sessions of the City of Syracuse (New York, which convicted them of violations of N.Y. Penal Law § 1141, subd. 1 following a joint trial by jury. Defendants claimed that the book at issue was not obscene as a matter of law and was entitled to the protection of the First Amendment.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged with having in their possession with intent to sell or show a lewd book, and that they did sell one book to a police officer in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 1141 subd. 1. A jury convicted defendants in a joint trial. The court reversed the convictions, determining that the book was not obscene as a matter of law. Although the jury was valuable to aid the court in determining community standards, the court had the ultimate responsibility in deciding the question of whether the book was obscene. The court concurred with decisions of courts in other jurisdictions that held that the same book was not obscene. The prohibitions of § 1141 were strictly limited to works of "hard-core pornography." Although the book was erotic, it did not tend to be an aphrodisiac and the main purpose of the book was not to stimulate sex feelings. The First Amendment protected the book because the appeal of the material, taken as a whole, was not predominantly prurient.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendants' convictions, dismissed the information, and remitted the fines imposed on defendants.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- obscenity -- book of literary value is not obscene (Penal Law, § 1141, subd. 1; N. Y. Const., art. I, § 8; U. S. Const., 1st and 14th Amdts.).

Henry Miller's "Tropic of Cancer" is a book of literary value; it is not hard-core pornography; and its sale does not violate the Penal Law ( § 1141, subd. 1; cf. N. Y. Const., art. I, § 8; U. S. Const., 1st and 14th Amdts.). Judgments of conviction are reversed, information is dismissed, and fines are remitted.

 

2349 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Pupillo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Broome County


37 Misc. 2d 455; 235 N.Y.S.2d 522; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2109



December 18, 1962 



DISPOSITION: The judgment is in all respects affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Justice Court of the Town of Fenton (New York), which convicted defendant of violating N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1180(1), which made it unlawful to operate a motor vehicle upon a public highway at a speed greater than was reasonable and prudent.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was driving an automobile on a road at night as a speed over 45 miles per hour. The road had inclines, declines, and curves. Defendant's car left the road and hit a guardrail. Section 1180(1) provided that no person should operate a motor vehicle or a motorcycle upon a public highway at a speed greater than was reasonable and prudent under the conditions with regard to the actual and potential hazards then existing. Defendant was convicted of violating § 1180(1). Defendant claimed on appeal that § 1180(1) was unconstitutionally vague and indefinite and that the prosecution failed to prove defendant guilty. The court rejected defendant's claims. The court ruled that § 1180(1) was sufficiently definite so as to advise defendant that his conduct was a violation of the law under certain circumstances because every person knew that the speed of a car could be dangerous and hazardous to the occupant and others using the public highway under certain circumstances and that the trial court's factual finding that defendant violated § 1180(1) was not clearly erroneous.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for operating a motor vehicle on a public highway at a speed that was greater than reasonable and prudent under the conditions that were present.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- speeding -- subdivision 1 of section 1180 of Vehicle and Traffic Law defining speeding was constitutional -- information based on information and belief and stating source of information as defendant's admissions was sufficient -- information sufficiently stated violation of such subdivision -- defendant was proven guilty of violating subdivision beyond reasonable doubt.

1. Subdivision 1 of section 1180 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law which directed that no person operate a motor vehicle upon a public highway at a speed greater than was reasonable and prudent under the conditions and having regard to the actual and potential hazards then existing, provided an adequate standard by which a driver's conduct could be tested and was constitutional.

2. An information charging defendant with violating subdivision 1 of section 1180 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law was not defective because made on [***2] information and belief, where the source of the information and belief, admissions made by defendant to an officer, were stated in the information.

3. The information sufficiently stated a violation of the subdivision. It stated the time and place of the violation, the speed at which defendant was traveling and that such speed was greater than was reasonable and prudent. The information was clear without a statement of the conditions and without a statement that defendant failed to have regard for the actual and potential hazards then existing.

4. Defendant was proven guilty of violating subdivision 1 of section 1180 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law beyond a reasonable doubt. Evidence as to conditions in the area was adequate as a matter of law to sustain the determination, and such determination was not against the weight of the evidence.

In re Department of Bldgs. of New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Bronx County


38 Misc. 2d 589; 236 N.Y.S.2d 833; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2131



December 12, 1962 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Therefore, the application is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, the Department of Buildings of the City of New York, filed an application seeking appointment of the Commissioner of Real Estate of the City of New York as receiver of a certain multiple dwelling. The application was made pursuant to N.Y. Mult. Dwell. Law § 309, as amended by 1962 N.Y. Laws ch. 492. The mortgagee of the property objected.


OVERVIEW: The city filed an application for appointment of a receiver for a certain multiple dwelling building. The application for a receiver was made pursuant to N.Y. Mult. Dwell. Law § 309, as amended by 1962 N.Y. Laws ch. 492, which allowed for appointment of a receiver over buildings that were in or in danger of becoming in a dilapidated condition. The owner of the building could not be located, and the only objection was filed by the mortgagee of the property. The mortgagee contended that the provision permitting appointment of a receiver was unconstitutional in that it improperly allowed the receiver to incur expenses to alter and improve the property, which in turn would effect the priority of the mortgage. However, the court noted that unlike previous "receivership" provisions, mortgagees were given notice and could apply to perform the work themselves along with other rights in protection of a lien. As such, the court granted the city's application.


OUTCOME: The court granted the city's application for appointment of the receiver.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- repair of slum dwellings -- chapter 492 of Laws of 1962, amending section 309 of Multiple Dwelling Law, dealing with repair of slum multiple dwellings, was constitutional.

Chapter 492 of the Laws of 1962, amending section 309 of the Multiple Dwelling Law so as to provide for the appointment of the Commissioner of Real Estate of the City of New York as receiver of multiple dwellings, declared to be nuisances because they had deteriorated, so that the receiver could remove conditions found to be contrary to the public health, safety or welfare was constitutional, was a valid use of the police power, complied with the requirements of due process of law, and did not impair the obligation of the mortgagee's contract. It provided for notice of a hearing and for the protection of the mortgagee's lien by giving him the right to perform the work himself.

 

2351 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Lynbrook v. Simon


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


38 Misc. 2d 547; 236 N.Y.S.2d 823; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2161



December 6, 1962 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, as I find that the petition does not allege facts to show the failure of the respondent Secretary of State to perform a duty imposed by law, the petition is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, Secretary of State of New York and billiard room operator, filed a motion to dismiss petitioner village's application for an order pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 requiring a public hearing to be held before the Secretary of State's issuance of a license for operation of the billiard room.


OVERVIEW: The billiard room operator requested the Secretary of State for a license to operate a billiard room in the village. The village filed an action seeking to require a public hearing before such a license was issued. The court dismissed the action, holding that the village did not allege facts to show the failure of the Secretary of State to perform a duty imposed by law. N.Y. Penal Law § 345 did not require a hearing before issuance of a license by the Secretary of State. The court also held that the village could not attack the constitutionality of the statute in this proceeding.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petition.


HEADNOTES

Licenses -- billiard license -- hearing -- Secretary of State not required to conduct public hearing before issuing license to operate billiard room under Penal Law ( § 345).

In a proceeding under article 78 of the Civil Practice Act for an order directing the Secretary of State to conduct a public hearing before issuing a license to operate a billiard room, the petition is dismissed for failure to allege facts showing that respondent did not perform a duty imposed by law. Section 345 of the Penal Law provides for the approval or refusal "for cause" of a license, but it does not require directly or impliedly that a public hearing be held before a license is issued.

 

2352 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Pelham v. North Pelham


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


38 Misc. 2d 234; 237 N.Y.S.2d 253; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2228



November 28, 1962 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff town brought an action against defendant village for a declaration that the village was required to compensate the town tax receiver at rates approved by the town and for recovery of a sum for services in collecting the village taxes.


OVERVIEW: The village adopted a resolution whereby the town tax receiver was to act as village tax receiver at a compensation not to exceed one percent of the village assessment roll. The village later adopted a resolution designating the town tax receiver as its tax receiver and providing that the compensation be paid to the town, but set the compensation at one-half of one percent. The town did not approve the reduction in compensation and the village clerk collected the taxes. The following year the town tax receiver did collect the village taxes. The village resolution provided for compensation of one-half of one percent; the town resolution provided for one percent. The town sought a declaratory judgment that the compensation had to be such amount up to one percent of the tax roll as was approved by the town. It also sought to recover compensation for collecting the taxes. The concluded that both the N.Y. Village Law and the county code could be harmonized. The village was permitted to vary the compensation from year to year, but that power was subject to the town's approval.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that the town was entitled to judgment declaring that until the village's electors revoked their direction to designate the town tax receiver as village tax receiver, the village board was required to make such designation at a compensation up to one percent of the tax roll as was approved by the town. The court also found that the town was entitled to recover compensation for collecting the village's taxes.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- collection of village taxes -- Village Law ( § 121) provided that village electors might direct Village Board to adopt resolution appointing Town Tax Receiver as Village Tax Receiver at compensation not to exceed 1% of village assessment roll; subsequently enacted Westchester County Administrative Code (§ 544) provided that such designation and compensation was subject to approval of Town Board; defendant village's electors at election directed village to use Town Tax Receiver for collection of taxes; such vote never rescinded; in 1958 village sought to reduce compensation of Town Tax Receiver to 1/2 of 1% of assessment roll; plaintiff was entitled to declaration that defendant village was required to fix compensation acceptable to plaintiff -- because of section 544 of Westchester County Administrative Code, since compensation was subject to approval of Town Board, village was required to fix compensation acceptable to town.

1. Section 121 of the Village Law (originally enacted in 1916 as § 138-a) provided that village electors might, at a special village election, direct the Village Board of Trustees to adopt a resolution whereby the Town Tax Receiver [***2] would also act as Village Tax Receiver at a compensation not to exceed 1% of the village assessment roll. Section 544 of the Westchester County Administrative Code, enacted in 1948, provided that designation and compensation were subject to approval by the Town Board. The village electors of defendant village did direct the Board of Trustees to adopt such resolution, and such resolution was never rescinded. It was thus mandatory for the Village Board to designate the Town Tax Receiver to collect village taxes. In 1958, the Village Board sought to reduce the compensation of the Town Tax Receiver to 1/2 of 1% of the assessment roll. The Town Board refused to consent to the reduced compensation. In this action, plaintiff was granted judgment declaring that until defendant village's electors revoked their direction to the Village Board of Trustees to designate the Town Tax Receiver as Village Tax Receiver, the Village Board was required to make such designation at a compensation up to 1% of the assessment roll as was approved by the Town Board of plaintiff and, in addition, plaintiff was entitled to judgment against defendant village for $ 2,999 as compensation for collection of [***3] defendant village's 1961 taxes.

2. The Village Board, under section 121 of the Village Law, was authorized to appoint the Town Tax Receiver as Village Tax Receiver, and the maximum compensation for the Receiver's services was stated. The Westchester County Administrative Code empowered the town to approve the compensation. While defendant village might reduce the compensation from year to year, that power was subject to Town Board approval, and defendant was therefore required to fix a compensation acceptable to the town.

 

2353 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Brody v. Save Way Northern Boulevard, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Queens County


37 Misc. 2d 240; 234 N.Y.S.2d 982; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2334



November 7, 1962 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Defendant's motion dismissing the complaint and for summary judgment in its favor and against the plaintiff is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant company moved for summary judgment to dismiss plaintiff competitor's unfair competition suit under an ordinance regulating signs at gasoline stations, which also claimed that the company did not have the right to sell gasoline under a brand name.


OVERVIEW: The company sold gasoline under a different brand than the competitor, who sought to stifle price competition. The ordinance protected the public against misleading price advertising. New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § B36-103.0 regulated signs at gasoline stations. Section B36-104.0 prohibited the deception of purchasers about the price nature, quality, or identity of products. Granting the motion, the court said that the ordinance was enacted for the benefit of the public and disclosed no intention, express or implied, to create a new cause of action for unfair competition. Accordingly, the competitor's claim in that connection was insufficient as a matter of law and had to be dismissed. The ordinance was not designed to fix prices or protect competitors, but rather to protect the gasoline-purchasing public against fraud. A violation of the sign ordinance was not designed to give a right of action to the competitor, but to protect the public against misleading signs. The ordinance was not intended to create any civil right or remedy. Assuming that an intent could have been implied, it would have availed the competitor nothing. The competitor was not within the protected class.


OUTCOME: The court granted the company's motion for summary judgment against the competitor.


HEADNOTES

Trade-marks, trade names and unfair competition -- criminal but not civil statute -- statute making it offense for gasoline station to display oversized price sign does not give competitor civil cause of action -- statute making it offense to sell, from pump bearing gasoline brand name, gasoline of different brand, does not give retailer civil cause of action against competing retailer.

1. The Administrative Code of the City of New York prohibits gasoline stations from displaying signs larger than 12 inches by 12 inches "referring directly or indirectly to the price or prices of gasoline" (§ B36-103.0), and prohibits practices which "deceive or tend to deceive the purchaser as to the price, nature, quality or identity [of the gasoline] or to sell or offer for sale from any pump, dispensing device, or container any gasoline * * * other than that * * * manufactured or distributed by the manufacturer or distributor * * * under the name, trade name, brand, symbol or mark affixed to or contained on such pump, dispensing device or container, or to substitute,  [***2] mix or adulterate gasoline" (§ B36-104.0), and imposes a fine of not more than $ 250 and/or imprisonment of not more than 60 days for a violation of either of the above sections (§ B36-105.0). Defendant Save Way Northern Boulevard, Inc., displayed an oversize sign reading "SAVE WAY", and was convicted of violating the above section B36-103.0, and the Court of Appeals upheld the constitutionality of that section and affirmed the conviction (10 N Y 2d 727). But this subsequent civil action against said defendant by an undersold competitor, for damages, is dismissed. The statute does not, and cannot constitutionally be construed to, give to a competitor a civil cause of action.

2. Defendant is one of 17 gasoline stations in 6 States which sell "Save Way" gasoline at about two cents below the nationally advertised brands of the major oil refiners. Defendant's pumps and signs label its gasoline as "Save Way". It has not been proved that defendant buys its gasoline from any one of the major oil refiners; but even if defendant does, defendant may sell the gasoline under its own name "Save Way", and it does not thereby violate the above quoted section B36-104.0 of the Administrative [***3] Code of the City of New York; and in any event, even if said section were to be construed to authorize a civil action by oil refiner against a retailer, it could not constitutionally be construed to authorize a civil action by a retailer against a retailer.

 

2354 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cummings


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Binghamton County


36 Misc. 2d 800; 233 N.Y.S.2d 724; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2337



November 7, 1962 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was tried without a jury for the offense of N.Y. Nav. Law § 45(1). The decision was reserved and briefs from the parties were requested. Defendant's motions to dismiss were rejected.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged for improperly operating her boat. Defendant was the operator of the vessel, which was not competing in a regatta. Therefore, the major issue for the court was whether defendant operated her vessel "in a careful and prudent manner." The court held that ordinary care required for safe navigation meant adherence to the established rules of navigation governing vessels generally. Mere proof of an accident was not sufficient to sustain a conviction. The State had to prove a violation of § 45(1) beyond a reasonable doubt. Determining that the State met its burden of proof, the court found that defendant did not have the right of way, and did not follow the proper rules for passing the vessel which did have the right of way. N.Y. Nav. Law § 41(2) dictated the proper procedure for passing. Defendant did not follow the rules and did not operate her vessel in a careful and prudent manner. A mere violation of the rule might not have been enough for a conviction, but when coupled with other circumstances in that defendant did not slow the speed of the vessel when changing course, and did injure persons in the boat, which defendant's vessel collided with.


OUTCOME: The court held that the State proved defendant guilty beyond a reasonable doubt.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- reckless operation of motorboat -- defendant guilty (Navigation Law, § 45, subd. 1) -- no right to jury trial -- Susquehanna River navigable.

1. Defendant was towing a water skier behind her outboard motorboat, and, without slackening her speed of about 25 miles an hour, she made a wide 180 degrees turn, and, while looking behind her at her skier, crashed into the port side (left side) of another motorboat. The latter boat had the right of way, even though it, too, was traveling at about 25 miles an hour and was towing a water skier; whereas defendant violated the "pilot rules" and gave the latter boat no time even to whistle (Navigation Law, § 41, subd. 1, par. [e]; subd. 2, pars. [c], [d]). The crash damaged both boats and injured a passenger in defendant's boat. Defendant is guilty of failing to "navigate * * * in a careful and prudent manner" (Navigation Law, § 45, subd. 1).

2. There is no right to a jury trial of such offense.

3. The Susquehanna River within the City of Binghamton where this collision occurred is 500 feet wide and is navigable (Navigation Law, § 2, subd. 4).

National Advertising Co. v. New York State Thruway Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


36 Misc. 2d 987; 233 N.Y.S.2d 897; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2339



November 7, 1962 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Therefore, since this court has no jurisdiction of the subject matter the defendant's motion is granted, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant New York State Thruway Authority filed a motion for an order dismissing plaintiff company's complaint challenging the constitutionality of N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 261-a.


OVERVIEW: The company filed a complaint challenging the constitutionality of N. Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 361-a, which conferred exclusive jurisdiction on the Court of Claims to hear and determine all claims against the authority for alleged torts or breaches of contract. The authority filed a motion to dismiss the complaint on the grounds that: the court lacked jurisdiction of the subject matter; the court lacked jurisdiction of the authority; and that the complaint failed to state a cause of action. The court stated that it could not have entertained jurisdiction on general equitable principles. The court held that it had no jurisdiction of the subject matter and dismissed the complaint.


OUTCOME: The court granted the authority's to dismiss the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Courts -- jurisdiction over State Thruway Authority -- Supreme Court has no jurisdiction of action against Thruway Authority to declare statute unconstitutional.

Absent the State's consent (cf. Public Authorities Law, § 361-b), the Supreme Court does not have jurisdiction of an action against the State Thruway Authority in equity or for a declaratory judgment as to the constitutionality of a statute (Public Authorities Law, § 361-a).

Homer Engineering Co. v. State


Claim No. 39261


Court of Claims of New York


38 Misc. 2d 262; 235 N.Y.S.2d 222; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2340



November 6, 1962 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Claimant contractor brought an action against defendant State of New York pursuant to an enabling act, 1961 N.Y. Laws 537, which was a special act of legislature conferring jurisdiction on the courts to hear and determine the claim of the contractor to recover sums owed to it by the State.


OVERVIEW: The contractor performed heating work for the State. Upon completion of the job, the State paid the contractor, who failed to deposit the check and realized the mistake over six years later. The court allowed judgment in favor of the contractor. The court ruled that after three years from the date of the issuance of the check, if not presented for payment, the amount was to have been be paid into the Treasury of the State to the credit of the General Fund, and the Comptroller was required to keep a record of said check for a period of six years from the date thereof. The court found that the legislature passed the enabling act for the very reason that the contractor had failed to present the check within the six-year period. The court held that the legislature intended to prevent the State from becoming unjustly enriched after the contractor made a mistake. The court ruled that the enabling act was not unconstitutional because it was in the exercise of the legislature's wide discretion and founded in equity and justice.


OUTCOME: The court gave judgment in favor of the contractor.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] State -- Court of Claims -- statute authorizing Court of Claims to hear and determine claim for amount of check issued by State and which payee failed to deposit for over six years, not unconstitutional (N. Y. Const., art. III, § 19) -- judgment granted for amount of check, without interest.

1. A statute authorizing the Court of Claims to hear and determine a claim for the amount of a check issued by the State for work done on a State hospital, which check the payee failed to deposit for over six years, is not unconstitutional, despite a provision barring payment of claims against the State which, as between citizens of the State, would be barred by lapse of time (N. Y. Const., art. III, § 19).

2. The intent of the Constitution was not to unjustly enrich the State and defeat a claim for which the State's liability already has been established.

3. Judgment is granted for the amount of the check, without interest.
