Greater Bayside Citizens Ass'n v. Birns


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Queens County


37 Misc. 2d 190; 234 N.Y.S.2d 505; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2354



November 2, 1962 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the motion and cross motion are granted and the petition dismissed upon the grounds that petitioner is not a person aggrieved and that even if it were it has not exhausted its administrative remedies.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a proceeding under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 38, petitioner citizens' association sought an order directing respondent Commissioner of the Department of Buildings of the City of New York to rescind and revoke the certificate permitting the construction and maintenance of a certain swim club, and further sought an order prohibiting respondent swim club from any further construction of the planned facility.


OVERVIEW: The Commissioner filed a motion to dismiss the association's complaint pursuant to N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act § 1293, on the ground that it did not state facts sufficient to constitute grounds for the relied sought, it did not allege that the association had exhausted all its administrative remedies, and that the association was not a proper aggrieved party in interest. The swim club filed a cross-motion, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 112 and 113, for a judgment on the pleadings and summary judgment. The court granted the motions of the Commissioner and the swim club to dismiss the association's complaint. In so holding, the court found that: (1) the association was not a property owner and had no standing to bring the action; (2) the association had not exhausted all of its administrative remedies; (3) it was for the Commissioner to decide whether the swim club's application as filed was within the provisions of New York City's zoning regulations; and (4) because there was no issue of constitutionality or validity involved, the association was compelled to review the Commissioner's determination by an appeal to the New York City Board of Standards and Appeals.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motions of the Commissioner and the swim club to dismiss the association's petition.


HEADNOTES

Administrative law -- failure to exhaust remedies -- nonproperty owner lacked standing to bring proceeding to revoke certificate permitting construction of swim club -- moreover, petitioner failed to exhaust administrative remedies by appeal from determination granting certificate.

1. Petitioner, which was not a property owner, had no standing to bring a proceeding to revoke a certificate permitting the construction of a swim club.

2. Moreover, petitioner failed to exhaust its administrative remedies by an appeal to the Board of Standards and Appeals from the determination of the Commissioner of Buildings.

 

2358 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Estrialgo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Kings County


37 Misc. 2d 264; 233 N.Y.S.2d 558; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2392



October 29, 1962 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to suppress evidence that he asserted was the product of an unreasonable search. The court conducted a hearing on the motion to suppress.


OVERVIEW: A police officer observed defendant carrying luggage at 4:30 p.m. To the officer, this seemed suspicious but only in the sense that Puerto Rican youngsters carrying expensive looking luggage in that neighborhood presented an incongruous situation. The officer detained defendant and questioned him. Defendant informed him that the luggage belonged to a friend whom he inadequately identified. The officer took defendant and the luggage with him into the police car to locate the "friend." There was no such person. The officer delivered defendant and the luggage to a detective in the squad office. The detective soon elicited a confession. The issue presented was whether at the time of first detention, the police had probable cause to believe that a felony had in fact been committed and had statutory authority to arrest. The court held that nothing which the officer observed justified suspicion of felony. No right of detention existed. The search occurred at the time of the detention and arrest when the luggage was placed in the police car. The indispensable absolute of probable cause to search was not then established. Thus, the evidence and the incriminating statement were suppressed.


OUTCOME: The court suppressed the evidence and defendant's incriminating statement.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- unlawful search and seizure -- on mere suspicion, police put man and luggage into police car and took him to police station, where he admitted felony of grand larceny of said luggage; luggage and confession are both inadmissible and suppressed (U. S. Const., 4th Amdt.; Code Crim. Pro., § 177, subds. 3, 4; § 813-c).

1. While cruising in a police car in a well-to-do neighborhood, a police officer saw a young Puerto Rican carrying expensive-looking luggage. The officer stopped and questioned him. Told that the luggage belonged to "a friend", the officer took him and the luggage into the police car to locate the "friend" but found no such person. The officer then delivered the young man and the luggage to a detective in the police station; and there the detective soon elicted from the young man a confession that he had broken into an automobile and had stolen the luggage. All this excellent police work transpired in about half an hour. The young man is now charged with the felony of grand larceny. On the above facts: (1) an arrest occurred when the young man and the luggage were placed in the police car; and at that time no probable cause had been found for an [***2] arrest (Code Crim. Pro., § 177, subds. 3, 4); and (2) a search occurred at the same time, as an incident of the arrest, and it likewise was without probable cause (U. S. Const., 4th Amdt.; N. Y. Const., art. I, § 12). After a hearing, the court ordered that the seized evidence and defendant's confession both be suppressed (Code Crim. Pro., § 813-c).

2. Police have duties of crime prevention and detection, as well as apprehension. They can approach and detain a suspect, and meanwhile observe him and his possessions at close range and briefly and perfunctorily question him, and they can meanwhile investigate at the scene or reasonably close to the scene. But when they detain him in the police station or elsewhere for questioning or while they investigate elsewhere, abuses of constitutional rights may occur. The founders of our constitutional government believed that the citizen needs protection against law-breaking by law enforcers. If the detention is not brief and perfunctory, the law will deem that an arrest occurred at the earliest beginning of the detention. Hence, if, at that earliest time, insufficient probable cause existed for an arrest and for a search as an incident [***3] of the arrest, any evidence thus unconstitutionally obtained is inadmissible and must be suppressed.

3. Factually, three different types of situations occur in which the problem arises as to whether the police have probable cause to search and seize. First, a felony has in fact been committed, and the police learn of it from the victim or their own observation or from fellow officers or reliable informants; in such case, a description of the person, or a suspect's furtive conduct near the scene of the felony, may give the police probable cause to arrest and search him (Code Crim. Pro., § 177, subds. 2, 3). Second, police hear the sound of breaking glass or screams in the night or they receive a report that a trespasser is on a roof at a late hour, and they find a suspect emerging from the building or in the back yard or running away from the scene; in such situations, they may have probable cause to believe (a) that a felony has been committed and (b) that they are arresting and searching the person who committed the felony (Code Crim. Pro., § 177, subd. 4). Where they have substantial information as to (a), less substantial information is required as to (b). On the other hand,  [***4] where (a) they hear from a reliable informer that narcotics are being sold at a certain place, this is not very substantial information that a felony as defined in our narcotics statutes has occurred; hence, (b) there must be evidence which approximates the actual commission of a felony within the officer's observation or presence (cf. Code Crim. Pro., § 177, subd. 1). Third, the police have no information that a felony has been committed, but they observe a person in the late hours carrying a quantity of furs or of clothing or of merchandise, with labels still affixed. In such situation, they can briefly detain and question and observe the suspect, but they cannot search him. Only if such brief questioning and observation produce probable cause to arrest and search him, can they then lawfully do so. The situation in the instant case is in this third category. The brief questioning of this defendant did not produce any probable cause to put him and the luggage into the police car and take him to the police station.

 

2359 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Gorman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Oneida County


36 Misc. 2d 568; 232 N.Y.S.2d 889; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2423



October 24, 1962 



DISPOSITION: Judgment of conviction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the City Court of Rome, Oneida County (New York), which convicted him of violating N.Y. Nav. Law § 45(1).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found guilty of violating § 45(1) for operating his boat too close to swimmers. He appealed, arguing that the oral decision of the trial court at the time of judgment indicated doubt in the mind of the judge that the statute was violated. The court found that the trial court was speaking of intent not being an element of the offense, so defendant was taking the word "probably" out of context to assign doubt in the judge's mind as to the fact of a violation. Defendant also argued that there was no showing of danger to any identified persons and no complaints of dangerous operation of the boat from any swimmers or watchers on the shore. The court held that the fact the swimmers were not identified and did not make a complaint was unimportant because the trier of the fact had a right to give credibility to the testimony of a deputy who witnessed defendant's actions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- reckless operation of motorboat on lake -- conviction affirmed (Navigation Law, § 45, subd. 1).

Judgment convicting the operator of a motorboat who, in towing a water skier on a lake, passed at about 20 miles an hour between two groups of swimmers and then turned and caused a considerable wash, of "unreasonably" interfering with and endangering the swimmers (Navigation Law, § 45, subd. 1), is affirmed. The Deputy Sheriff's information on his own knowledge is sufficient. It is immaterial that no swimmer has made a complaint.

 

2360 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Rettaliata v. Huntington


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special and Trial Term, Nassau County


36 Misc. 2d 781; 233 N.Y.S.2d 593; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2514



October 5, 1962 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the complaint is dismissed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owners brought a declaratory judgment action against defendant town, contending, among other things, that the town's zoning ordinance was unconstitutional as applied to their property and that there were procedural defects in the adoption of various amendments to the ordinance. The court dismissed the claim regarding the constitutionality of the ordinance, but the claims regarding procedural defects remained.


OVERVIEW: The town adopted a resolution to amend the ordinance, stating that the ordinance was amended by substituting a map that was attached to the minutes of the town meeting. The town then published a copy of the notice and the zoning map in a local paper. In dismissing the property owners' complaint for declaratory judgment, the court held that the enactment of the amended map was properly published and posted in the local newspaper because the published materials were the same as those contained in the adopted resolution. The court rejected the property owners' argument that the ordinance could only be amended in words and figures and that the notice that referred to the map was legally insufficient. Instead, the court found that the resolution of adoption of the amended map was an "ordinance" within the meaning of N.Y. Town Law §§ 264, 265 because the New York Town Law did not limit the method of changing a district boundary to an ordinance by stating the boundary in words and figures. Instead, the Town Law authorized the town's board to fix the appropriate method.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the property owners' cause of action.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- property owner failed to prove confiscation, or that amendments were not properly posted and published; action dismissed -- zone boundaries may be changed by merely adopting amended map, if such map clearly shows boundaries.

1. In this action by the owners of vacant land (1) to adjudge the zoning ordinance confiscatory and unconstitutional as applied to plaintiffs' property and (2) to adjudge that amendments to the Zoning Ordinance were not properly adopted, the court dismissed cause (1) at the close of plaintiff's case, because plaintiffs failed to prove (a) their cost for the property or any other monetary proof, and (b) beyond a reasonable doubt that their property cannot be adapted to one-acre zoning or that the zoning is discriminatory or does not conform to a comprehensive plan. After trial, the court dismissed cause (2), since it found that the latest amendment was properly published and posted and enacted, and hence there was no need to consider the earlier amendments.

2. A Zoning Ordinance may be validly amended by a few words, such as: "the same hereby is amended by [***2] substituting and adopting the annexed map as the Amended Building Zone Map", if the map designates the zones by lines, roads, or other landmarks, drawn to scale. Similarly, with such an annexed map, a notice of public hearing may simply state, for example: "for the purpose of considering the adoption of an Amended Building Zone Map annexed hereto". The statutes do not require a lot of words (Town Law, §§ 130, 264, 265).

 

2361 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Doe


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Family Court of New York, City of New York, Manhattan Juvenile Term


36 Misc. 2d 611; 232 N.Y.S.2d 715; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2557



October 1, 1962 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The child, who had been found to be habitually disobedient and beyond parental control, ran away from home and was returned to the court by the police. Three voluntary institutions informed the court that they were not in a position to accept her.


OVERVIEW: The child was brought to court on a parental petition. She was remanded to a facility for temporary detention. The mother and the child acquiesced to the recommendations that the child was in need of institutional care. The child did not profit from the program and she was permitted to return home. The issue was whether, under the Family Court Act, the court had been divested of all power to "place" in the State Training School for Girls a girl coming within the definition of a person in need of supervision. The court determined that a person in need of supervision was a category of delinquency as to which the legislature directed the substitution of the softer word "place" for "commit" and the review of those placements at the end of 18 months. The court explained that N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 756 provided that a person in need of supervision could be placed in an authorized agency, and a state institution was an authorized agency. The court placed the child in the State Training School for Girls pursuant to N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act §§ 119, 756. The court held it to be the legal duty of the State Department of Social Welfare to receive and accept the child for appropriate care and treatment.


OUTCOME: The court placed the child in the State Training School for Girls and her remand to the Youth House for Girls was continued pending her transportation to the training school.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Infants -- "Person in need of supervision" -- Family Court Act defined "authorized agency" to include State supported or controlled institution (§ 119) and Family Court could, therefore, (under § 756) order "placing" in State Training School of habitually disobedient, chronically truant, parentally uncontrollable and sexually promiscuous girl who was within definition of "Person in need of supervision" although not "Juvenile delinquent" (§ 712) despite limitations of Social Welfare Law (§ 425 et seq.) to "Juvenile delinquents" and "delinquents" and to "commitment" rather than "placement"; such interpretation avoids construction raising doubts of unconstitutionality (N. Y. Const., art. VI, § 13) and leading to unreasonable or harmful result.

1. The Family Court had power under the Family Court Act (eff. Sept. 1, 1962) to order the "placing" in a State Training School, as a "Person in need of supervision" (§ 712), of a girl under 18 years of age who was previously found to be habitually disobedient, parentally uncontrollable, chronically truant and sexually promiscuous, who had not profited from an experimental three-month period in a voluntary institution, and whose conduct [***2] continued to deteriorate following her return home on probation.

2. Section 712 defined a "Juvenile delinquent" as one under 16 "who does any act which, if done by an adult, would constitute a crime", but further defined as a "Person in need of supervision" a boy under 16 or a girl under 18 "who is an habitual truant or who is incorrigible, ungovernable or habitually disobedient and beyond the lawful control of parent or other lawful authority". With respect to an adjudicated juvenile delinquent, section 753 authorized the court to order "placing" the child in an "authorized agency" (pursuant to § 756) for 18 months or "committing" him to a State or other "authorized agency" for up to three years (pursuant to § 758), but as to an adjudicated "Person in need of supervision", section 754 provided only for "placing" (under § 756) with no power given to "commit" (under § 758). However, during the course of legislative consideration of the Family Court Act a new section 119 was added which defined an "authorized agency" to include not only voluntary institutions but also those supported or controlled by the State or its subdivisions. Accordingly, a "Person in need of supervision"  [***3] may be "placed" in a State institution -- an "authorized agency" (§ 756).

3. Although provisions of the Social Welfare Law (§ 425 et seq.) enacted before 1962 and dealing with State training schools, refer only to "Juvenile delinquents" and "delinquents" and not to "Person in need of supervision", and to "commitments" rather than "placements", the failure to conform the language of some sections of the Social Welfare Law was less indicative of legislative intent than the express inclusion of State training schools within the definition of "authorized agencies". If two or more interpretations of an ambiguous statute are permissible, the construction will be avoided which leads to an unreasonable, absurd or harmful result. Inasmuch as voluntary institutions can accommodate only 25% or less of children requiring institutional care, so that the others must of necessity be accommodated in State training schools, although, as the State Department of Social Welfare has pointed out, 80% to 90% of the girls in the State institutions could not be defined as "Juvenile delinquents" because they have not committed acts which would constitute crimes, it is unreasonable to suppose that the [***4] statute was intended to give the court no alternative but to abandon these children in the open community in accordance with the interpretation by the Department which would limit admission to the State training schools to children adjudicated "delinquent".

4. Moreover, a court, especially one of original jurisdiction, should seek to avoid a conclusion of unconstitutionality and any grave doubts thereupon, and provisions of the Constitution (N. Y. Const., art. VI, § 13, as amd. in 1961) limit the Family Court's jurisdiction to actions and proceedings for the "protection, treatment, correction and treatment of those minors who are in need of the exercise of the authority of the court because of circumstances of neglect, delinquency or dependency as the legislature may determine", whereas, if section 712 be interpreted to exclude a "Person in need of supervision" from the term "delinquency", the court would be excluded from all jurisdiction over such cases. To avoid such constitutional doubts, the term "Person in need of supervision" must be interpreted as a category of delinquency for which the Legislature directed a different label, a substitution of the softer word "place" for [***5] "commit", and review of these placements after 18 months.

 

2362 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Hudson Land Corp. v. Temporary State Housing Rent Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


36 Misc. 2d 844; 236 N.Y.S.2d 544; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2586



September 26, 1962 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Therefore, the amended complaint is dismissed. The cross motion is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landlord brought a proceeding against defendant state rent commission seeking a judgment declaring that 1962 N.Y. Laws 21, § 5 did not rescind previously granted rent increases or a declaration that the act was unconstitutional. The commission moved to dismiss the complaint for legal insufficiency and the landlord filed a motion for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: A landlord applied for a rent increase alleging that the property was not yielding the return allowed by law. While the application was pending, the rent control law was amended to provide that the current equalization rate was to be used to adjust the property's assessed valuation. The rent administrator issued orders for increases but before they took effect 1962 N.Y. Laws 21 was enacted nullifying increases based on the current equalization rate. The landlord complained that the rent administrator should have used the equalization rate in effect when the application for an increase was originally filed. The landlord argued that if the current equalization rate was correctly applied, then the 1962 act was unconstitutional because it arbitrarily and unreasonably destroyed its vested rights in the increases. The court dismissed the landlord's complaint because the rent administrator had no discretion to apply the former equalization rate and rollbacks of rent increases were constitutional. A landlord had no vested right to application of the rules in effect when an application for an increase was filed even where the application had proceeded to the point of favorable action.


OUTCOME: The court granted the commission's motion and dismissed the landlord's complaint.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- unpaid rent increases were validly and constitutionally nullified (L. 1962, ch. 21, § 5).

An apartment house landlord who applied in 1960 for rent increases based on 1954 equalization rates, and who in 1961 was granted rent increases based on 1961 equalization rates, and which increases were protested and unpaid by the tenants, was validly and constitutionally deprived in 1962 of such increases, and his proceeding was validly and constitutionally transferred by the State Rent Administrator to the newly established New York City Rent and Rehabilitation Administrator (Emergency Housing Rent Control Law, § 13; § 4, subd. 4, par. [a], as amd. by L. 1957, ch. 755 and L. 1961, ch. 337; L. 1962, ch. 21, § 1, subds. 4, 5, 6, 11, 12, and §§ 4, 5, 6).

 

2363 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Schildhaus v. Warden of City Prison


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


37 Misc. 2d 660; 235 N.Y.S.2d 531; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2619



September 20, 1962 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator, on behalf of the criminal defendant, filed a writ of habeas corpus seeking defendant's release from hospital commitment. A city magistrate had committed defendant to the hospital for psychiatric study.


OVERVIEW: Defendant had been charged with violating the city health code by failing to supply sufficient heat to his residential buildings, to which he pled guilty. A city magistrate determined that defendant was in a state of idiocy, imbecility, or insanity so that he was incapable of understanding the proceedings against him, and therefore, ordered that defendant be committed to the hospital for psychiatric examination pursuant to N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 870. Defendant filed a petition for writ of habeas corpus seeking his release from commitment. The court sustained the writ and ordered that defendant be remanded to the prison warden so that he could be produced for sentencing. The court held that a magistrate had authority to order a defendant to commitment, but that such an order had to be based on reasonable grounds. The court found that the record did not support a reasonable belief that defendant was subject to commitment. The court found that defendant's failure to provide heat could be considered cruel, callous, and selfish, but his actions did not rise to the level of idiocy, imbecility, or insanity.


OUTCOME: The court sustained the writ and ordered defendant's release from commitment.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- insanity -- court having appropriate jurisdiction may order committed for preliminary mental examination defendant who has pleaded guilty to offenses which were not crimes -- no right to jury trial on issue of such defendant's mental competence, but defendant has constitutional right to be heard prior to final commitment or adjudication of incompetence -- such commitment does not violate defendant's constitutional rights -- however, as Magistrate who ordered commitment did not have reasonable ground for belief that defendant was subject to commitment for psychiatric study, he acted without jurisdiction -- accordingly, writ of habeas corpus sustained and relator remanded for sentence.

1. When there is reasonable ground to believe that a defendant charged with offenses which are not crimes (N. Y. City Health Code, § 131.03; N. Y. City Crim. Cts. Act, § 102-c [now § 95]) is in such a mental state that he is incapable of understanding the charge or proceeding or of making his defense, the court or Magistrate may order such defendant to be examined to determine the question of his sanity (Code Crim. Pro., § 870).

2. Even aside from statutory authority, such an order [***2] of commitment is within the inherent juridical competence of a duly constituted criminal court having appropriate jurisdiction, since one held on a criminal charge should not be tried, sentenced or punished while he is incapable of making a defense or of understanding the proceeding (Penal Law, § 1120).

3. There is no right to a jury trial on the issue of such defendant's mental competence. However, a defendant has a constitutional right to be heard prior to final commitment or adjudication of incompetence (U. S. Const., art. III, § 2; N. Y. Const., art. I, § 2).

4. Whether the maximum period of 60 days provided for in the commitment order herein would be unreasonable need not be decided as the instant writ of habeas corpus was obtained within one day after the directed hospitalization.

5. Commitment of a defendant for mental observation does not constitute cruel and unusual punishment (U. S. Const., 8th, 14th Amdts.; N. Y. Const., art. I, § 5).

6. An exercise of discretion by one having jurisdiction will not be reviewed by way of a writ of habeas corpus. However, in acting under the bare assumption that defendant's record of prior similar violations warranted a reasonable [***3] belief that he was subject to commitment for psychiatric study, the Magistrate proceeded without jurisdiction. Accordingly, the writ is sustained and defendant is released from the hospital commitment and remanded for sentence in pursuance of his pleas of guilty to the offenses charged.

 

2364 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Kerber Motors Corp. v. Dolphin Motors, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Queens County


36 Misc. 2d 849; 234 N.Y.S.2d 125; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2633



September 19, 1962 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the complaint is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff car dealer sought recovery from defendants, car distributor and car importer, for their refusal to deliver motor vehicles and parts on the same terms and in the same quantities as they had in the past, in violation of N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 197. The distributor and the importer sought dismissal of the complaint, alleging that § 197 was unconstitutional, and in any event, that they terminated the dealer's distributorship for cause.


OVERVIEW: The court rejected the claim of the distributor and the importer that § 197 was unconstitutional. The court held that, if at all possible, statutes were construed to avoid the conclusion that they conflicted with any fundamental constitutional law. However, the court found that the distributorship was terminated for cause, within the meaning of § 197. Thus, the court granted the motion to dismiss the complaint. The court found that the car dealer submitted claims for repair work that was never done, that the car dealer affixed to these claims signatures of customers that were not genuine, and that the car dealer received credit from the car distributor and the car importer on these claims. The court further found that the car dealer's franchise agreement provided that the submission of a false or fraudulent report, statement, declaration, or claim provided cause for termination of the agreement. Accordingly, the court held that the car dealer's course of conduct was a bar to the relief it sought, and the court dismissed the car dealer's complaint.


OUTCOME: In an action where a car dealer sought recovery for the termination of its franchise agreement, the court granted the motion for dismissal brought by the car distributor and the car importer, and dismissed the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- dealer's franchise -- manufacturer properly refused to make further deliveries to dealer who had submitted false repair claims to manufacturer.

An automobile dealer who, contrary to his franchise from the manufacturer, submitted false repair claims to the manufacturer, was deprived by the manufacturer of his franchise "for cause" ( General Business Law, § 197).

People v. Harrison


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special and Trial Term, New York County


36 Misc. 2d 62; 232 N.Y.S.2d 389; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2644



September 17, 1962 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion is, therefore, denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a coram nobis motion seeking to be relieved of his conviction upon his plea of guilty to the crime of felonious possession of a narcotic drug.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's motion sought relief from his conviction on the ground that the evidence against him was obtained by unlawful search and seizure. In denying defendant's motion, the court held that since defendant pled guilty to the charge against him while he was represented by an attorney, he waived any contention as to the unlawfulness of the search and seizure. Further, the court held that defendant's failure to move for a suppression of the allegedly tainted evidence prior to trial estopped defendant's right to raise the question as an issue after conviction.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's motion.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- coram nobis application -- defendant, who had attorney, pleaded guilty to one count of indictment; even if search and seizure unlawful, coram nobis is not now available.

Coram nobis is not available to a defendant who (1) failed to move to suppress evidence which he now claims was produced by an unlawful search and seizure, and (2) while represented by an attorney, pleaded guilty to one count of the indictment.

 

2366 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Schwartz v. Compania Azucarera Vertientes-Camaguey


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Kings County


39 Misc. 2d 63; 240 N.Y.S.2d 247; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2646



September 17, 1962 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff shareholder brought this action pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 977(b) to obtain the appointment of a permanent receiver of the New York assets of defendant, a Cuban corporation.


OVERVIEW: The shareholder brought this action to appoint a permanent receiver over the corporation's New York assets contending that the Cuban government nationalized the corporation, causing it to liquidate assets and cease doing business. The corporation contended that the statute, if enforced, would unjustly and arbitrarily deprive it of its property in violation of the due process clauses of U.S. Const. amend. XXIV and N.Y. Const., art. I, § 6. The court dismissed the shareholder's complaint, vacated the temporary receivership, and held that: (1) all the activities of the corporation in Cuba and its right and capacity to control and continue its enterprises there terminated following the promulgation of a Cuban resolution; (2) the corporation had not been dissolved, liquidated, or nationalized, and its charter, or organic law, had not been suspended, repealed, revoked or annulled, within the meaning of § 977(b); and (3) the various corporate activities of the corporation constituted doing business within the meaning of § 977(b).


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the shareholder's complaint and vacated the temporary receivership.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Receivers -- local assets of foreign corporation -- in action by stockholder for appointment of receiver of Cuban corporation pursuant to section 977-b of Civil Practice Act, while corporate assets in Cuba were expropriated, under Cuban law, its corporate existence was not nationalized within said section and its charter had not been suspended or revoked -- evidence showed that defendant had not ceased doing business -- judgment was granted dismissing complaint, vacating temporary receivership and directing receiver to account.

1. Plaintiff, a stockholder of defendant, commenced this action pursuant to section 977-b of the Civil Practice Act to obtain the appointment of a permanent receiver of the New York assets of defendant, a corporation organized under the laws of Cuba. Section 977-b authorized a stockholder to commence such action when the corporation had assets within the State and (a) it had been nationalized, or (b) its charter had been suspended or revoked, or (c) it ceased to do business. Defendant conducted extensive business enterprises in Cuba prior to June 3, 1959. On that date and thereafter various properties of defendant located in Cuba were seized. On [***2] July 6, 1960, Cuba enacted Law No. 851 which conferred authority upon its president "to nationalize, through forced expropriations, the properties or 'Empresas' owned by physical and corporate persons who are nationals of the United States * * * or of the 'Empresas' in which such physical and corporate persons have an interest, even though they be organized under the Cuban laws." Provision was made for payment through bonds. Resolution No. 1 enacted on August 6, 1960 nationalized the "property and empresas" of defendant. As a result thereof, all activities of defendant in Cuba, and its right and capacity to control its enterprises there terminated. Despite plaintiff's claim that the word "empresas" meant "corporate or juridical entity", the effect of the resolution was to vest in the Cuban State the corporate properties and control of the enterprises in Cuba. The evidence supported the view that the corporations continued to exist and that payment for the properties expropriated would ultimately be made, not to the stockholders, but to the corporation itself. The evidence supported the view that the termination of defendant's right to pursue its enterprises in Cuba would not,  [***3] under Cuban law, deprive defendant of the right to do business elsewhere. Accordingly defendant corporation had not been dissolved, liquidated or nationalized, and its charter had not been suspended or revoked within the meaning of section 977-b of the Civil Practice Act.

2. The cessation of defendant's business caused by the intervention of a temporary receiver did not mean that defendant had ceased doing business. Defendant maintained its New York office; its officers resided in the United States; it entered into new employment contracts; it conserved its assets; its board of directors met regularly; it paid creditors; it sought out new business opportunities; it maintained its memberships in trade organizations and it made claim against the Cuban Government through the United States Department of State for in excess of $ 93,000,000. All these activities established that defendant had not ceased to do business.

3. Judgment was granted defendant dismissing the complaint, vacating the temporary receivership and directing the receiver to account.

Trozze v. Drooney


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


City Court of New York, Binghamton


35 Misc. 2d 1060; 232 N.Y.S.2d 139; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2655



September 17, 1962 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Motion denied, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After petitioner landlord brought a summary proceeding and obtained a warrant to evict and a judgment for rent due upon default by respondent tenant, respondent welfare department brought a motion to vacate the warrant and judgment and interpose on behalf of the tenant, a welfare recipient, the defense of violation, under § 143-b of the New York Social Welfare Law, 1962 N.Y. Laws ch. 997.


OVERVIEW: The department asserted that it had not received notice of the pending eviction proceeding from the tenant, and asserted that the tenant had a valid defense to eviction for non-payment under the newly-enacted § 143-b. The court held that § 143-b violated the due process rights of the landlord under the Fifth and Fourteenth Amendments. The court noted that § 143-b established building violations which were dangerous or detrimental to life or health as a defense to non-payment by welfare recipients. The court found that the statute was motivated by a worthy goal, and noted that the state had authority to prevent such violations under its police power. The court held that the landlord's contract to rent the premises was a property right within the scope of the Fifth Amendment, and the statute would prohibit the landlord from recovering a money judgment for back rent during a time when the tenant enjoyed possession, thus violating the landlord's contract rights.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motions by the department and the tenant to vacate the warrant of eviction and judgment for rent due in favor of the landlord against the tenant.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- tenants who receive public welfare -- tenant who is welfare recipient cannot refuse to pay rent on ground of building violations; such defense (Social Welfare Law, § 143-b, subd. 5) unconstitutional.

Subdivision 5 of section 143-b (added by L. 1962, ch. 997) of the Social Welfare Law, which provides that "It shall be a valid defense in any action or summary proceeding against a welfare recipient for non-payment of rent to show existing violations in the building wherein such welfare recipient resides which relate to conditions which are dangerous, hazardous, or detrimental to life or health as the basis for non-payment", is unconstitutional and void. (U. S. Const., 5th & 14th Amdts.)

Application of De-Jo Constr. Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


36 Misc. 2d 288; 232 N.Y.S.2d 908; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2657



September 14, 1962 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The court concludes that the papers before it fail to establish that the petitioner is entitled to the relief it seeks, and the petition is accordingly dismissed on the merits, but without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner contractor sought an order directing respondent building inspector to issue it certain building permits.


OVERVIEW: The contractor sought to erect houses in a town on plots of land that were of a peculiar shape. The town adopted an amendment to is zoning ordinance, Town of Hempstead, N.Y., Zoning Ordinance § B-10.1, which intended to prohibit similar layouts of land. The contractor filed applications for building permits, but they were refused as not being in compliance with § B-10.1. The contractor claimed that § B-10.1 was unconstitutional and void because it illegally delegated to the planning commission power to enact zoning legislation for the town. He also claimed that it was invalid because it imposed restrictions on the frontage of plots beyond those fixed by N.Y. Town Law § 280-a(5). The court held that there was no unlawful delegation. Under N.Y. Town Law § 280-a(5), the commission could not effectively approve a subdivision plat unless the access standards were met. Nothing in N.Y. Town Law § 280-a indicated any legislative intention to prohibit the town from enacting higher standards for street frontage of building plots than those prescribed by § 280-a.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the contractor's petition.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- when builder applied for permits to erect buildings on peculiarly shaped lots, town properly amended zoning ordinance and then properly rejected application -- zoning amendment may constitutionally have effect of delegating power to County Planning Commission to approve plats whose lots have less than 40 feet of street frontage (Nassau County Govt. Law, § 1610; Town Law, §§ 265-a, 269, 280-a, subd. 5).

1. Petitioner applied for permits to erect on a tract the greatest possible number of dwellings consistent with the town's zoning ordinance, by laying out the lots in a so-called "pot-handle" or "flag" shape; whereupon the town properly amended the zoning ordinance and then properly rejected petitioner's application.

2. The zoning amendment is not unconstitutional merely because its effect is to delegate to the County Planning Commission the power to approve subdivision plats theretofore or thereafter filed on which the lots have less than 40 [***2] feet of street frontage (Nassau County Government Law [L. 1936, ch. 879, as amd.], § 1610; Town Law, §§ 265-a, 269, 280-a, subd. 5).

People v. Feinstein


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


District Court of New York, Second District, Nassau County


36 Misc. 2d 261; 231 N.Y.S.2d 833; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2663



September 13, 1962 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the motion is granted and the information is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant machine cabinet-making shop operator challenged the information that charged him with a violation of the building zone ordinance of the Town of Hempstead, New York. The operator filed a motion to dismiss the information for insufficiency.


OVERVIEW: On review, the court agreed with the operator that the town's information was insufficient and therefore the court dismissed the information. The court examined whether it was constitutional to charge the operator with a violation of the building zone. The court noted that the building ordinance could not be given such a strained construction that craftsmen would be prevented from making use of modern tools where the use of such tools had no adverse effect on the public welfare, health, morals or safety of the community. On the issue of the sufficiency of the information, the court noted that there was no description of the type of machines used, nor was their number or their nature set forth. Further, there were no facts as to the scope, volume, or other characteristics of the business conducted on the premises. Because of this, the court found that the information was insufficient on its face. The court granted the operator's motion and dismissed the information.


OUTCOME: The court granted the operator's motion to dismiss the town's information for lack of sufficiency.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- ordinance of town permitted, in business district, use of premises for hand cabinet making; information charging defendant with violating ordinance by operating in business district "machine cabinet making" shop without describing machines, failed to state sufficient facts to constitute crime -- ordinance did not prohibit use of electrically operated hand tools.

1. A zoning ordinance of a town permitted, in a business district, use of the premises for carpenter shops or hand cabinet making shops. An information charging defendant with violation of the ordinance in that defendant operated, in a business district, a "machine cabinet-making" shop on the premises, without describing the machines used, their number or their nature, or the scope, volume and characteristics of the business conducted on the premises, failed to allege facts constituting a crime. The information was dismissed.

2. The ordinance did not prohibit the use of portable, electrically operated hand tools.

Bernkrant v. Temporary State Housing Rent Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


36 Misc. 2d 507; 233 N.Y.S.2d 70; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2674



September 12, 1962 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion to dismiss the complaint is accordingly granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant temporary state housing rent commission sought to dismiss the action by plaintiff landlord pursuant to N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act § 473 to declare N.Y. Emergency Housing Rent Control Law § 9(1) (1946) unconstitutional. The landlord by cross-motion sought to expunge an affidavit and filed for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The landlord alleged that the commission was allowed unlimited review of its orders. If an administrative agency had the power to reconsider and review without limit, there never would be an end to administrative procedure or litigation. The court did not find such unlimited reviewing power lodged in the commission either by statute or through any other source. The commission acted inappropriately while the action was under review on appeal by issuing another order denying de novo the certificate of eviction. Over the landlord's protest, an order of the court directed a remission for further consideration by the commission. The city rent and rehabilitation administrator succeeded to the commission's jurisdiction and had not issued her determination. The court held that the proceedings before the commission should be brought to an end. As the landlord's application for a certificate was before the commission following a court order, such power residing in the court, the action for declaratory judgment was not entertained.


OUTCOME: The court granted the commission's motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent control -- State Rent Commission's power to recall its own decisions is not unconstitutionally free from all judicial review.

The statutory power of the State Rent Commission to recall its own decisions and to review them de novo even during the pendency of a proceeding for a judicial review (Emergency Housing Rent Control Law [L. 1946, ch. 274, as amd.], § 9, subd. 1) has not, in this instance or generally, been exercised in such a manner as to escape ultimate judicial review; hence this action for a judgment declaring that the statute confers such unlimited power unconstitutionally is dismissed.

Paterson v. Univ. of N.Y.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, SPECIAL TERM, NASSAU COUNTY


237 N.Y.S.2d 845; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4264

2372 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Tesoriero v. Examining Board of Plumbers


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Oswego County


36 Misc. 2d 412; 232 N.Y.S.2d 706; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2751



August 29, 1962 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The application is granted, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner plumber filed an application for an order directing respondent, the Examining Board of Plumbers of the City of Oswego, New York, to grant his application to take an examination for a master plumber's license and directing the Board to delete from the plumbing rules a requirement that an applicant must have served five years in the employ of a certified master plumber in the city.


OVERVIEW: The plumber had been doing business in the city for many years and had five years' experience as a journeyman plumber in the trade of plumbing. He filed an application to take an examination for a master plumbers' license. The board denied his application on the ground that he had not met one of the city's plumbing regulations, which required an applicant to have been employed by a master plumber holding a certificate of competency. The plumber challenged the constitutionality of the regulation. The Board argued that the regulation was a valid exercise of its police power. The court granted the plumber's application, holding that the regulation was an unreasonable, discriminatory, and unwarranted restriction upon the plumber's right to engage in his lawful occupation. The court found that the regulation was not a valid exercise of the city's police power because its real purpose was not to protect the public health, morals, or general welfare.


OUTCOME: The court granted the plumber's application.


HEADNOTES

Licenses -- licensing of plumbers -- city's Plumbing Rules and Regulations insofar as they required, as condition for applicant to take examination for certificate of competency to entitle him to master plumber's license, that he have five years' experience as plumber but only with master plumber holding certificate of competency from city's Examining Board of Plumbers were unconstitutional.

Section 1-a of the Plumbing Rules and Regulations of the City of Oswego, insofar as it required petitioner, a journeyman plumber with five years of experience, to have said experience with an employing or master plumber holding a certificate of competency issued by the Examining Board of Plumbers of the City of Oswego, or some other municipality, before being eligible to take the examination for a certificate of competency to entitle him to a master plumber's license, was unconstitutional as an unwarranted restriction upon petitioner's right to engage in his lawful occupation.

 

2373 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Application of Wilson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Ulster County


36 Misc. 2d 734; 233 N.Y.S.2d 955; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2768



August 27, 1962 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Petitions dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner political party chairmen brought a proceeding under N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act art. 78 seeking an order to direct respondents, New York Secretary of State and other government officials, to issue a certificate for the election of a family court judge for a particular county.


OVERVIEW: The chairmen of the county political committees contended that the voters of the county were entitled to elect a family court judge pursuant to a resolution that the county filed with the Secretary of State in July 1962, creating a judicial vacancy under the N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 131. The court, however, concluded that § 131(a) excluded the normal operation of the statute where a certificate was filed subsequent to March 1, 1962. The court stated that the legislature had established a cut-off date of March 1, 1962 for the creation of new children's court judgeships under § 131 due to the rapid increase in the number of new judgeships being created. The court also concluded that N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 131(a) was not unconstitutional because N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 13 did not require a separate family court judge in each county. The court recognized the strong presumption in favor of the constitutionality of a statute and refused to substitute its judgment for that of the legislature. While the petition under N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act art. 78 was a proper method to challenge the constitutionality of the statute due to the exceptional public nature of the case, it was without merit.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petition.


HEADNOTES

Family Court -- Judges -- after March 1, 1962, County Board of Supervisors and County Judge cannot create Children's Court Judgeship (Family Ct. Act, art. 1, part 1; N. Y. Const., new art. VI, eff. Sept. 1, 1962).

The Children's Court Act (§ 4, subd. 2) empowered a County's Board of Supervisors and County Judge to file a certificate requiring that a Children's Court Judge be elected; but the Children's Court Act is repealed (L. 1962, ch. 689, § 41, eff. Sept. 1, 1962), and no such certificate can validly be filed after March 1, 1962 (Family Ct. Act, § 131, subd. [a]; N. Y. Const., new art. VI, eff. Sept. 1, 1962). In Ulster County, as in many other counties, the County Judge who formerly also acted as Children's Court Judge now acts as the Family Court Judge (Family Ct. Act, art. 1, part 1; N. Y. Const., new art. VI, §§ 14, 13, subd. a).

Jaquith v. Simon


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Albany County


35 Misc. 2d 508; 231 N.Y.S.2d 735; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2804



August 17, 1962 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner voters filed a proceeding under N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act. art. 78 seeking a mandamus order to require respondent New York Secretary of State to accept the voters' independent nominating petitions for candidates to be voted for by all state voters without regard to the requirement in N.Y. Elec. Law § 138(5)(a).


OVERVIEW: The voters challenged N.Y. Elec. Law § 138(5)(a), which required that an independent nominating petition for candidates to be voted for by all the voters in the State had to be signed by at least 12,000 voters, of whom at least 50 had to reside in each county of the State. The voters argued that the minimum requirement for each county was unconstitutional under the Equal Protection Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution and other state and federal constitutional provisions. The court concluded that although mandamus was only an appropriate remedy for a wrong already suffered and not to prevent an anticipated wrong, that in exceptional and extraordinary cases involving duties of public officers in matters of a public nature the proceeding would be allowed. The court, however, upheld the validity of § 138, holding that the State had the power to assure a proper diffusion of political initiative between thinly populated counties and those having concentrated masses. There was no invidious discrimination involved. The statutory scheme was rational and was a reasonable attempt to achieve the State's objective.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the voters' petition.


HEADNOTES

Elections -- independent nominating petitions -- Election Law ( § 138, subd. 5, par. [a]) requiring independent nominating petition for State-wide office to be signed by 12,000 voters of whom 50 reside in each county of State was constitutional.

1. A proceeding in the nature of mandamus was entertained for the purpose of passing on the constitutionality of a provision of the Election Law. The court disregarded technical objections so as to resolve the basic questions raised by the petition.

2. Paragraph (a) of subdivision 5 of section 138 of the Election Law, which provided that "An independent nominating petition for candidates to be voted for by all the voters of the state must be signed by at least twelve thousand voters, of whom at least fifty shall reside in each county of the state, the counties of Fulton and Hamilton to be considered as one county", was constitutional.
