Yorkshire Associates, v. Halpern


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Bronx County


39 Misc. 2d 367; 240 N.Y.S.2d 646; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2840



August 7, 1962 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, plaintiff's motion for an injunction pendente lite is denied and the temporary stay is vacated; the cross motion by defendant to dismiss the complaint is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landlord filed an action for an injunction pendente lite, seeking to prevent defendant rent director from decreasing the maximum rents for the individual housing accommodations provided by the landlord. The landlord obtained a temporary stay enjoining the rent director from taking action against him. The rent director filed a motion to dismiss the complaint.


OVERVIEW: The landlord's apartment building contained 35 housing accommodations. The rent director received a list of violations posted against the building, and commenced an administrative proceeding to decrease the maximum rents for the housing accommodations. The landlord claimed that the violations had been removed and filed suit to permanently enjoin the administrative proceeding. The court vacated the temporary stay enjoining the rent director from taking action against the landlord, denied the landlord's request for an injunction pendente lite, and dismissed the landlord's complaint. The court could not restrain the administrative proceeding or prevent the rent director from executing his official, statutory duties, especially because conditions detrimental to life and health were involved. There was no proof that the proposed administrative proceeding would cause the landlord irreparable injury; the results of such a proceeding would be reviewable.


OUTCOME: The landlord's motion for an injunction pendente lite was denied and the temporary stay was vacated. The rent director's motion to dismiss the complaint was granted.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- enjoining proceedings by Rent Director -- motion to enjoin pendente lite Rent Director from reducing maximum rents because of violations placed against apartment building was denied; defendant's actions were authorized by law -- unfinished administrative proceeding not subject to review until all administrative remedies were exhausted; complaint was dismissed.

1. A motion for an injunction pendente lite to enjoin The Bronx Rent Director from conducting proceedings for the reduction of the maximum rents of plaintiff's apartment building because of violations posted against the building which violations created a fire hazard, were dangerous and were detrimental to health, was denied. Defendant was charged by law with conducting such proceedings and the court would not interfere with the conduct of defendant's official duties. Plaintiff failed to establish that defendant's actions would cause irreparable injury.

2. The conduct and reasonableness of [***2] the unfinished administrative proceeding was not at issue, could not be anticipated and could not be placed before the court until plaintiff exhausted its administrative remedies. The complaint was dismissed.

 

2376 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Smith


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


County Court of New York, Kings County


35 Misc. 2d 533; 230 N.Y.S.2d 894; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2862



August 1, 1962 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion to suppress evidence seized by the state.


OVERVIEW: Defendant filed a motion to suppress evidence seized from him. The court found that the search was made without a search warrant and although probable cause to arrest existed, the search was not incidental to and contemporaneous with an arrest. The court found that defendant was not arrested until many days later and that such a search without a search warrant and not incidental to an arrest was a general search and therefore unreasonable. The court held that defendant had standing to seek suppression of the evidence. The court found that at the hearing the police officer testified that the fixed premises, a secondhand automobile lot, was registered in the name of defendant's brother. The investigation that led to the search sought to establish that in fact the licensed premises were being operated by defendant in his brother's name, that in fact, defendant was the true owner and operator and in this connection there was evidence that he was "employed" on the lot for long periods. The court held that the search was unreasonable and that the defendant had standing and granted the motion to suppress.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's motion to suppress evidence.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Crimes -- unlawful search and seizure -- general search without warrant was unreasonable -- Code of Criminal Procedure (§ 813-c) provided liberal rule with respect to defining person having "standing" to make motion to suppress -- property found in search would not be admitted in evidence on defendant's trial.

1. A search without a search warrant and not incidental to an arrest -- defendant was arrested many days later -- was a general search and unreasonable.

2. Section 813-c of the Code of Criminal Procedure provided that "A person claiming to be aggrieved * * * and having reasonable grounds to believe that the property * * * claimed to have been unlawfully obtained may be used as evidence against him" had standing to make a motion to suppress such evidence. Such statutory rule was a liberal rule applicable to all property whether "contraband" (mainly possession of gambling paraphernalia or narcotics) or "fruits" (stolen property) or "instrumentalities" (forged instruments, marked money, blood stained weapons).

3. During the search of the premises, a secondhand automobile sales lot registered in the name of defendant's brother, the police found identifiable parts of a [***2] stolen automobile (a "fruits" case). Since the search was unreasonable, defendant had "standing" to make the motion to suppress and such motion was granted. However, since there was other evidence, no order of suppression was necessary. The product of the search will not be admitted on the trial.

 

2377 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Brown v. McNeill


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Dutchess County


35 Misc. 2d 53; 230 N.Y.S.2d 826; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2897



July 23, 1962 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a proceeding against respondent state mental hospital superintendent, relator state mental hospital inmate was granted a writ of habeas corpus on which he sought his release from the Matteawan State Hospital on the grounds that he is sane and that the statute under which he was transferred to Matteawan, N.Y. Correct. Law § 412 (1929, amended 1959), was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The inmate claimed that his continued was illegal because there were no criminal charges pending. In considering his petition, the court ruled that the lack of pending criminal charges did not invalidate his detention. Under N.Y. Correct. Law § 412, someone who was validly committed to a mental hospital, who was previously sentenced to imprisonment, and who still manifested criminal tendencies, was property transferred on an administrative order from the mental hygiene commissioner. The court concluded that the inmate was validly confined, was previously committed to a corrections department institution, and remained insane. The court also reaffirmed that § 412 was constitutional. In addition, the court ruled that with an administrative transfer, there had to be some guarantee or assurance that it was not made capriciously or arbitrarily. The determination was also subject to review through the remedy of habeas corpus. Any administrative inconvenience in the availability of the habeas procedure was not a justification for its denial. The court determined that remand was necessary because the record did not delineate what conduct by the inmate formed the basis for the transfer.


OUTCOME: The court remanded the matter for a further hearing, and it ordered the state mental hospital superintendent to file an amended return setting forth in detail the acts and conduct of the inmate that allegedly constituted the manifestation of continued criminal tendencies.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Insane and other incompetent persons -- constitutionality of statute -- habeas corpus -- section 412 of Correction Law, providing for transfer to Matteawan State Hospital of mental hospital inmate who has been previously sentenced to imprisonment in a correctional institution and who still manifests criminal tendencies, not unconstitutional -- remedy of habeas corpus available to inmate who claims his transfer pursuant to that section to be wrongful.

1. Section 412 of the Correction Law, providing for the transfer to Matteawan State Hospital of a person committed to a State mental hospital who has been previously sentenced to a term of imprisonment in correctional institution and who still manifests criminal tendencies, is not unconstitutional. Relator is not deprived of his liberty by reason of his transfer, pursuant to this section, from a hospital under the jurisdiction of the Department of Mental Hygiene to one under the control of the Department of Correction. In each instance his detention depends on the continuance of his insanity, an issue which he may question at any time by habeas corpus. The fact that relator may be more restricted, may enjoy fewer privileges [***2] and would be confined with dangerous persons at Matteawan is not sufficient to warrant a declaration that section 412 is unconstitutional.

2. The transfer provided for by section 412 is, by the provisions of that section, effected by administrative order of the Commissioner of Mental Hygiene. However, the remedy of habeas corpus is available to an inmate who claims his transfer to be wrongful.

3. It is established by the testimony of a licensed psychiatrist that relator is still insane. However, since the conduct on his part which was the basis for the transfer is not disclosed in the present record the matter is remanded for a further hearing on this matter.

 

2378 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Paterson v. University of New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


35 Misc. 2d 608; 231 N.Y.S.2d 557; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2903



July 19, 1962 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants filed a motion to dismiss a declaratory judgment action filed by plaintiffs, who worked in landscaping-related businesses and who were challenging the constitutionality of a statute regulating the profession of landscape architecture.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs brought a declaratory judgment action against defendants, seeking a declaration that N.Y. Educ. Law art. 148 was unconstitutional. Article 148 created the profession of "landscape architecture" and invoked criminal penalties for those who engaged in the proscribed conduct without a license. Defendants filed a motion to dismiss the complaint as insufficient in law. The court concluded that the issue of constitutionality was properly raised. Article 148 contained no guideposts for the relevant professional board to follow in reaching a decision as to the "grade and character" of work acceptable to them, and authorization given to an administrative officer to choose without providing a measure or standard for such classification was beyond the power of the legislature. The article gave no clear guidance to aid the average person in deciding what conduct was criminal and what was not. Assuming that the conduct involved constituted a legitimate area for legislative regulation, it did not warrant the sweeping and unnecessary interference with harmless pursuits that the article accomplished.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendants' motion.


HEADNOTES

Licenses -- landscape architecture -- action will be entertained to declare article 148 of Education Law unconstitutional.

Article 148 of the Education Law makes it a misdemeanor to perform after July 1, 1961 various acts which are rather uncertainly defined as the practice of "landscape architecture" without a license. In such an innocuous field, such uncertainty in a statute as to criminality may deprive persons of due process and may be an improper use of the State's police powers. The statute requires licenses, and requires applicants for licenses to take examinations, and authorizes the examining board to license or to refuse to license persons already in such practice, depending on whether their experience has been "of a grade and character acceptable to the board" and provided that such persons apply to the board before July 1, 1961 (Education Law, § 7323, subd. 3). The statute's standards of what shall be "acceptable to the board" are not definite (N. Y. Const., art. III, § 1). Plaintiffs are persons already in practice, to whom the board has denied licenses. Plaintiffs ask for a declaratory judgment of unconstitutionality. Motion [***2] to dismiss their complaint is denied.

 

2379 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


County of Nassau v. Bennett


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


35 Misc. 2d 586; 231 N.Y.S.2d 766; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2951



July 10, 1962 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The plaintiffs' motion for a temporary injunction is granted as to estates in which plaintiff County Treasurer has been appointed administrator and is presently acting as such, and otherwise denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a county and a county treasurer, filed suit against defendants, a surrogate of the county and others, challenging the constitutionality of N.Y. Surr. Ct. Proc. Act Law § 125-b. The matter was before the court upon plaintiffs' motion for a temporary injunction.


OVERVIEW: The alleged unconstitutionality was based on N.Y. Const. art. III, § 16. In the case at bar, the court was dealing with an area, namely the administration of decedents' estates, the regulation of which was essentially a state function, as witnessed New York Surrogate's Court Act, which was uniformly applicable throughout the state, and yet plaintiffs contended this was exclusively a matter of county government. The court thought it clear that the legislation was not concerned with county "organization" or "government." By the creation of the office of public administrator who, under N.Y. Surr. Ct. Proc. Act Law § 118, had an absolute priority over the county treasurer, the county treasurer could in no sense be said to have been deprived of fees which he had not earned and the very right to which depended upon a future discretionary act by the surrogate. The court was of the opinion that N.Y. Surr. Ct. Proc. Act Law § 125-b(14) was unconstitutional in its entirety. However, the court was further of the opinion that it might be excised from the rest of the law without doing violence to its purpose, even though the act did not contain a separability clause.


OUTCOME: The court granted plaintiffs' motion for a temporary injunction as to estates in which the county treasurer had been appointed administrator and was presently acting as such. The court otherwise denied the motion.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- Public Administrator of county -- Public Administrator of Nassau County is validly created office (Surrogate's Ct. Act, § 125-b); but subdivision 14, requiring County Treasurer to hand over all present estates of decedents, is unconstitutional (N. Y. Const., art. III, § 17); however, said subdivision 14 may be excised.

1. Section 125-b (as at present numbered; L. 1962, ch. 574) of the Surrogate's Court Act validly and constitutionally creates the office of Public Administrator of Nassau County, even though this statute incorporates several other statutes by reference (N. Y. Const., art. III, § 16), and even though it is a "special or local" statute, since it does not "provide for the organization and government" of said county (N. Y. Const., art. IX, § 1, subd. [b]); but subdivision 14 of said section 125-b, purporting to make the Public Administrator the automatic and immediate successor of the County Treasurer as administrator of any and all [***2] estates whereof the Surrogate has heretofore appointed the County Treasurer as administrator (cf. Surrogate's Ct. Act, §§ 118, 123), is void since said subdivision 14 of section 125-b contravenes the constitutional prohibition against any "local bill * * * decreasing fees, percentages or allowances of public officers, during the term for which said officers are * * * appointed (N. Y. Const., art. III, § 17); accordingly, enforcement of said subdivision 14 of section 125-b is restrained pendente lite.

2. Subdivision 14 may be, and is, excised from the rest of the act, even though the act does not contain a separability clause.

 

2380 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Ramapo v. Spring Valley


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Rockland County


40 Misc. 2d 589; 243 N.Y.S.2d 569; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2958



July 9, 1962 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The plaintiff's motion for judgment on the pleadings is therefore denied, and since the court has the power under rule 112 of the Rules of Civil Practice to give judgment in favor of either party, judgment is granted in favor of the defendants dismissing the complaint.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff Town of Ramapo, New York, brought an action against defendant villages for a judgment declaring unconstitutional 1961 N.Y. Laws 88, which amended 1936 N.Y. Laws 526, § 2(A) to allow the Town to enter into an agreement with any village wholly or partly within its limits and that maintained its own police department to provide for apportionment of the cost of the Town's police department. The Town moved for judgment on the pleadings.


OVERVIEW: Both villages maintained police departments. The Town entered into an agreement that provided the villages with apportioned exemptions, expressly stating that if 1961 N.Y. Laws 88 was held to be unconstitutional, the Town was to be reimbursed for the amount of the exemptions. The court held that the Town was not estopped from challenging the constitutionality of 1961 N.Y. Laws 88 by virtue of having voluntarily entered into the agreement. The agreement recited that the Town had been advised that 1961 N.Y. Laws 88 might have been unconstitutional and expressly provided for reimbursement in that event. Rather than waiving its rights, the Town expressly reserved them. The Town was a proper party to bring the declaratory judgment action, as it was a party to the agreement. However, 1961 N.Y. Laws 88 was not unconstitutional. N.Y. Const. art. XVI, § 1 provided that tax exemptions could only be granted by general laws. N.Y. Const. art. III, § 17 barred the passage of a private or local bill granting an exemption for taxation on property. 1961 N.Y. Laws 88 did not grant an exemption. It attempted to equalize assessments to defray the cost of performing a necessary municipal function.


OUTCOME: The court denied the Town's motion for judgment on the pleadings and entered judgment for the villages that dismissed the Town's complaint.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- apportionment of cost of Police Department -- plaintiff town entered into agreement with defendant villages, located wholly or partly within town, granting them apportionment of cost of Police Department of town, pursuant to chapter 88 of Laws of 1961, because villages maintained their own departments; in action by town to declare statute unconstitutional, defense that town is estopped from bringing action or waived right is insufficient -- plaintiff town has right to remedy of declaratory judgment because of continuing controversy with villages and residents of town other than in villages -- statute not unconstitutional since it does not grant tax exemption to villages.

1. Pursuant to chapter 88 of the Laws of 1961, which authorized a town of the first class in Rockland County to enter into an agreement with villages wholly or partly within the town, which maintained their own [***2] Police Departments, to provide for apportionment of the cost of the town Police Department between the village and the remainder of the town, plaintiff town entered into agreements with defendant villages in December, 1961 granting defendant villages exemptions from their normal assessments for town police services, and then commenced this action for judgment declaring chapter 88 unconstitutional. A defense that plaintiff is estopped from questioning the validity of the contract or has waived its right to do so is insufficient. Defendants were not misled. They were warned by a provision in the contract that plaintiff doubted the legality of the statute, and they agreed that the town be reimbursed if the statute should be declared unconstitutional. The implication was that the statute would be judicially tested. The town did not waive its rights, but expressly reserved them.

2. The remedy of an action for declaratory judgment is available to plaintiff. There is an existing controversy in which the villages sought tax exemption. The agreement made favored the villages as against residents of other portions of the town, but with the distinct reservation that the question of legality [***3] be determined. Plaintiff, as one of the parties to a doubtful contract is the proper party to test its legality.

3. The purpose of the statute is to protect the citizens of a village which maintains an adequate police force from the burden of double taxation. The general law pertaining to other counties contained in section 150 of the Town Law did not apply to Rockland County because the town Police Department had been created by special act. Chapter 88 was enacted to grant villages in Rockland County the same rights as villages elsewhere. The statute does not grant tax exemption by special act and does not violate section 1 of article XVI of the Constitution. Defendants are granted judgment dismissing the complaint.

 

2381 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Fuss v. French Nat'l Railroads


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


35 Misc. 2d 680; 231 N.Y.S.2d 57; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2962



July 9, 1962 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, the French National Railroads, filed a motion to dismiss plaintiff New York resident's complaint for personal injuries sustained while a passenger on a train owned by the railroads.


OVERVIEW: The New York resident sustained personal injuries while a passenger on one of the railroads' trains while visiting France. He was reboarding the train when he fell off. As a result, both of his legs were amputated, and he suffered other painful injuries. The New York resident filed a complaint against the railroads in New York for his personal injuries. The railroads filed a motion to dismiss the complaint, claiming that the New York court's jurisdiction over the action would constitute an undue burden on the foreign commerce of a transportation company in violation of U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, and that the New York resident was required to assert his rights in the courts in France. The court dismissed the railroads' motion, holding that the exercise of jurisdiction by a court in New York would not represent an undue burden on the railroads. The court ruled that New York was the appropriate forum because it was financially impossible for the New York resident to bring the suit in France, there was grave risk to his health, and the railroads conducted business in New York.


OUTCOME: The court denied the railroads' motion to dismiss the New York resident's complaint to recover for personal injuries.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Courts -- action for foreign tort -- no unreasonable burden placed on foreign commerce by New York courts' entertaining jurisdiction of action by resident against foreign corporation for tort committed in France -- provision of French law placing venue of action in courts of France not binding on courts of New York.

1. No unreasonable burden was placed on foreign commerce by the entertainment by the courts of New York of an action by a resident of New York to recover damages for personal injuries sustained by plaintiff in France while a passenger on a train of defendant foreign corporation, allegedly caused by defendant's negligence. That New York was the proper forum was shown by the fact that plaintiff's poverty prevented bringing suit in France, that he was physically incapacitated from bringing suit in France without grave risk to his health, that defendant conducted extensive operations in New York, and that defendant solicited plaintiff's business in New York and advised him concerning his travel in France.

2. A provision of French law which required plaintiff to assert his rights against defendant in the courts of France did not prevent plaintiff from asserting his [***2] rights in New York. The remedy available under French law was not different from the remedy available in a negligence action in New York. The convenience of defendant was served by such provision of the French law and no reason to prefer defendant's convenience existed. Moreover, the court was not bound to enforce the foreign provision limiting the venue of an action brought to enforce a foreign right.

Capway Realty Corp. v. Temporary State Housing Rent Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Kings County


36 Misc. 2d 100; 231 N.Y.S.2d 90; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2988



July 3, 1962 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] This motion will therefore be granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landlord brought an action to declare unconstitutional an act of defendant, the New York Temporary State Rent Commission, which revoked an increase in rents that it had granted the landlord. The Commission moved to dismiss the complaint under N.Y. R. Civ. Prac. 106 on the ground that it did not set forth a cause of action.


OVERVIEW: On September 1, 1961, the landlord was granted an increase in rents. On February 27, 1962, the Commission revoked such increase, pursuant to its alleged authority under 1962 N.Y. Laws 21, § 5, which provided for nullification and rescission of any adjustment in maximum rents granted by the Commission after June 30, 1961. The court noted court of appeals decisions that stated that freezes and rollbacks of rent increases were historically and constitutionally acceptable and that the state rent control statute did not contemplate a taking of the property of any landlord and even though it might, on occasion, compel an owner to operate his real property at a loss, the statute was not for that reason to be condemned as an arbitrary use of the police power. The court stated that while under those decisions the court of appeals did not directly consider § 5, yet under those decisions, the court felt that the determination would be the same and it had to be governed by such rulings.


OUTCOME: The motion was granted.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- rollback of maximum rents -- statute revoking increase in maximum rents granted after June 30, 1961 was constitutional.

Plaintiff landlord was granted an increase in rents on September 1, 1961 and, on February 27, 1962, defendant revoked such increase under section 5 of chapter 21 of the Laws of 1962 which provided for rescission of any adjustment in maximum rents granted after June 30, 1961. In this action to declare the rescission and section 5 unconstitutional, the complaint was dismissed.

 

2383 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Buckley v. Meng


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


35 Misc. 2d 467; 230 N.Y.S.2d 924; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3045



June 25, 1962 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner editor challenged the validity and application of respondent college's regulations which denied the editor the right to use the college's auditorium for a series of forums.


OVERVIEW: The college explained its refusal to permit the use of the auditorium by saying that, after reading the editor's opening remarks, the dean formed the opinion that political issues were involved and the college was enjoined from allowing the use of its premises by political groups. The journal had previously been permitted to use the auditorium and the editor argued that, although there was no duty to make the college available for nonacademic uses, once having made it available, the regulations had to meet constitutional standards. The court struck the regulations and ordered that new ones be enacted. The court found that the regulations were either so vague as to invite discriminatory and arbitrary regulations, or else rested upon a classification of uses which stifled the free discussion of vital public issues. The court determined that a decision as to whether a program was permissible depended on a determination outside the scope of the regulation. The court concluded by forbidding the college from denying discussion of public issues from its halls on the ground that it was the voice of a minority or the voice of one not approved by an official acting under the regulations.


OUTCOME: The regulations denying the editor the use of the auditorium were struck down and the court ordered the college's administrative committee to enact a new set of regulations.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Constitutional law -- freedom of speech -- regulation of city sponsored college permitting use of its facilities for programs of outside organizations "compatible with the aims" of such college violated freedom of speech amendment of Constitution.

A regulation of the Administrative Committee of Hunter College -- one of the municipal colleges of the Board of Higher Education of the City of New York -- which permitted use of college facilities for "Other programs offered by outside organizations insofar as these are determined to be compatible with the aims of Hunter College as a public institution of higher learning", was either so vague as to invite discriminatory and arbitrary action or rested upon a classification of uses which stifled freedom of speech in violation of the First Amendment of the United States Constitution. A decision as to whether a program was permissible depended on the discretion of some undetermined official.

Glen Cove Theaters v. City of Glen Cove


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, SPECIAL TERM, NASSAU COUNTY


231 N.Y.S.2d 747; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4226



June 18, 1962, Decided

 

2385 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Falcone


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, New York County


34 Misc. 2d 729; 232 N.Y.S.2d 80; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3175



June 8, 1962 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion to vacate the warrant is denied. This renders academic the application to vacate the order, and it, too, is therefore denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent subpoenaed witness filed a motion to quash a subpoena issued by petitioner investigative commission to require him to appear before it to discuss organized crime.


OVERVIEW: The subpoenaed witness argued that the court did not have jurisdiction to issue an order to show cause or to issue the warrant for him to appear. The court rejected the witness's arguments. The court pointed out that N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act § 406(2) required the issuance of a warrant for the arrest of the subpoenaed witness who had failed to appear for a hearing issued by the investigative commission. The court further found that there was no express requirement that the investigative commission give notice to the subpoenaed witness who failed to appear before it before the court was entitled to issue a warrant. The court noted that the statute required it to issue the warrant.


OUTCOME: The court denied the subpoenaed witness's motion to vacate the arrest warrant and also denied his motion to vacate the order to show cause.


HEADNOTES

Witnesses -- subpoenas -- following personal service of subpoena upon respondent to appear and testify, respondent defaulted and warrant was issued to Sheriff to apprehend respondent and bring him before petitioner Commission of Investigation to be examined -- motion to vacate warrant was denied -- notice to defaulting witness was not condition precedent to issuance of warrant and omission of requirement of notice from section 406 of Civil Practice Act did not render it unconstitutional.

Petitioner, Commission of Investigation of State of New York, served a subpoena upon respondent personally to appear and testify in an investigation. Respondent failed to appear. Thereafter, an order to show cause why a warrant of attachment should not issue to the Sheriff commanding him to apprehend respondent and bring him before the commission was served upon respondent by substituted service (mailing of the papers by registered mail and affixing the papers to the outer door of his residence). Upon respondent's default, a warrant issued commanding [***2] the Sheriff to apprehend respondent and bring him before the commission to be examined. Respondent's motion to vacate the warrant was denied. Under subdivision 2 of section 406 of the Civil Practice Act, there was no requirement that notice be given to the subpoenaed witness who failed to appear, before the court might issue a warrant to compel his attendance. Notice to the defaulting witness was not a condition precedent to issuance and execution of a warrant, and the omission from the statute of a requirement of notice did not render it unconstitutional. Personal service of the order to show cause was not a prerequisite to the issuance of the warrant. The recitals in the warrant as to the granting of the order to show cause and the issuance of the order were surplusage so long as it was issued, as required by subdivision 2 of section 406 of the Civil Practice Act," upon proof by affidavit of the failure [of the respondent] to attend".

 

2386 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Gilmartin v. Lipson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


34 Misc. 2d 998; 229 N.Y.S.2d 611; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3200



June 5, 1962 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition is dismissed and the stay is vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner applied to the court for an order vacating and setting aside a subpoena duces tecum served upon it by respondent commissioner of accounts.


OVERVIEW: The commissioner showed that he was conducting an examination in the activities of the park department. A park department employee gave testimony that petitioner paid money to the employee in order to get business. The commissioner stated that he issued the subpoena because he could not ascertain where the notations regarding the payments would be in petitioner's books. Petitioner contended that the subpoena was invalid and ineffective as to him, because he was not a governmental employee and was solely a private citizen. Therefore, he argued that the commissioner had no power to examine the records or to investigate his affairs. He also alleged that the statute that the commissioner was acting under, 1936 N.Y. Laws § 879 (1936), was unconstitutional in that it attempted to make criminal the failure to appear and be examined by the commissioner. He argued that the subpoena was void in that the required warning of the punishment to be inflicted in the event of failure to appear and produce did not conform to the applicable statutes. The court found the subpoena was not improper in form and contained a sufficient warning. The petition was dismissed.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petition and vacated the stay of proceedings that was entered in order to determine the petition.


HEADNOTES

Witnesses -- subpoena -- vacatur -- motion to vacate subpoena issued by Commissioner of Accounts of Nassau County denied -- Commissioner's power of investigation not limited to government employees -- petitioner could assert constitutional right against self incrimination at hearing -- no standards needed to guide investigative officer -- issuance of subpoena not beyond scope of Commissioner's power -- subpoena not broad -- proper in form.

1. An application to vacate a subpoena duces tecum served upon petitioner by the Commissioner of Accounts of Nassau County for the purpose of investigating certain payments made to an employee of the Park Department was denied.

2. The County Government Law of Nassau County did not limit the Commissioner's power to investigate to government employees only but that power extended to private citizens and their records.

3. The danger of self incrimination would not arise until petitioner appeared before the Commissioner to testify, and petitioner could then assert his constitutional protection with respect to any question.

4. Under section 206 of the County Government Law of Nassau County,  [***2] the Commissioner had power to investigate only and report his findings to the County Executive. No standards to guide him were necessary.

5. The issuance of the subpoena duces tecum was not beyond the scope of the Commissioner's power.

6. The subpoena was not too broad. Petitioner failed to show that it called for documents irrelevant to the inquiry.

7. The subpoena was proper in form and conformed with section 403 of the Civil Practice Act.

 

2387 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Palidoro v. Feuer Transp., Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Municipal Court of the City of New York, Borough of the Bronx


35 Misc. 2d 385; 229 N.Y.S.2d 820; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3244



May 25, 1962 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employees brought an action against defendant employer to enforce a determination reached through arbitration that the employees were, in fact, employees rather than independent contractors, and that they were entitled to certain benefits under the collective bargaining agreement. The employer filed a motion to dismiss the employees' complaint, contending the court lacked jurisdiction and the employees' claims were time barred.


OVERVIEW: The employees contended that they were entitled to additional holiday, vacation, and time off compensation based on the arbitrators' determination that the employees were not independent contractors and were, in fact, entitled to the benefits provided under the collective bargaining agreement negotiated between the employees' union and the employer. The employer sought dismissal of the employees' claim, asserting that the court did not have jurisdiction over the action and that the employees' claims were time-barred under the agreement's provisions. The court granted the employer's motion to dismiss based on its determination that the employees were bound by the arbitration provisions of the collective bargaining agreement. They were required to pursue their claims by way of arbitration as required by the agreement and could not maintain an independent action. The court had no jurisdiction over the subject matter of the action to enforce the arbitration award. The court also determined that the employees' claims were time-barred because more than five days had elapsed after the grievances in the employees' action arose.


OUTCOME: The court granted the employer's motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Arbitration -- enforcement of award -- action against employer by members of union for benefits under collective bargaining agreement between union and employer could not be maintained; plaintiffs were bound by arbitration clause of such agreement for settling disputes -- if union failed to prosecute plaintiffs' grievances, they had remedy against union -- Municipal Court lacked jurisdiction to enforce prior award in arbitration -- if benefits accrued under agreement, plaintiffs were barred by five-day limitation period therein for assertion of claims -- if plaintiffs sought to enforce prior award, one year limitation (Civ. Prac. Act, § 1461) barred such enforcement -- moreover, enforcement was right of union, not of plaintiffs.

1. An action by members of a union with which defendant had a collective bargaining agreement to recover holiday and vacation pay and for time not worked, granted by the collective bargaining agreement, was dismissed. The collective bargaining agreement provided for arbitration of disputes thereunder and plaintiffs' remedy to obtain benefits due under the agreement was arbitration, the method provided by the agreement. Plaintiffs were bound by the [***2] arbitration provisions of the collective bargaining agreement. The duty to demand arbitration rested, not upon defendant, but upon plaintiffs.

2. If plaintiffs' union failed to prosecute their grievances, plaintiffs had a remedy against their union but not defendant.

3. If plaintiffs were seeking to enforce an award in arbitration made with respect to other employees of defendant, then the Municipal Court of the City of New York lacked jurisdiction.

4. If the benefits sought by plaintiffs accrued to them under the collective bargaining agreement, their failure to assert their claims within five days after they arose, as provided by the agreement, barred plaintiffs from enforcing them.

5. Assuming that plaintiffs were seeking to enforce the award in arbitration made to others, plaintiffs were barred from doing so by the one-year limitation provided in section 1461 of the Civil Practice Act for motions to confirm awards in arbitration.

6. Moreover, plaintiffs, as individuals, had no standing to enforce the award. The right to enforce belonged to the union.

 

2388 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Rosenbluth v. Rosenbluth


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Sullivan County


34 Misc. 2d 290; 228 N.Y.S.2d 613; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3296



May 16, 1962 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff wife sued for separation from defendant second husband on the ground of abandonment and nonsupport. The husband, while conceding that he had abandoned her, claimed that such abandonment was justified because it occurred as soon as he discovered that an Alabama divorce procured by the wife from her first husband was void and he counterclaimed for a decree of annulment of the marriage to the wife on that basis.


OVERVIEW: The second husband claimed to have abandoned the wife upon learning that her divorce from her first husband was obtained during a one-week stay in Alabama. Because neither party thereto was domiciled or intended to be domiciled in Alabama, the second husband claimed that the resulting decree was arguably void. The court ruled that the test to be applied to determine whether the second husband, as a "stranger" to the divorce degree, was entitled to collaterally attack it in New York depended on whether he would be permitted to attack the decree in a court in Alabama, the logic being that the Full Faith and Credit Clause of the United States Constitution, U. S. Const., art. IV, § 1, required the court to give the same deference to the Alabama decree as it would be given in the state in which it was issued. Noting that the Alabama court did not obtain jurisdiction over the marital res at any time, the court ruled that the Alabama court would have permitted a collateral attack on the decree for lack of jurisdiction. That being the case, a collateral attack by a "stranger" like the second husband was permissible and the second husband was entitled to have the marriage annulled.


OUTCOME: The court held that the second husband was entitled to collaterally attack the Alabama divorce decree obtained by his wife and her first husband; that the Alabama divorce decree was void for lack of jurisdiction; that the second husband was entitled to a decree annulling his marriage to the wife; and that the wife was not entitled to support.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Husband and wife -- foreign divorce -- collateral attack -- since stranger to Alabama divorce decree could attack it collaterally in that State, defendant, stranger to plaintiff wife's Alabama divorce action against first husband, was permitted to attack it here -- Alabama courts lacked jurisdiction of wife's marital res and assumed it because of wife's fraud -- wife's Alabama divorce decree was invalid and defendant husband was granted judgment annulling marriage to plaintiff.

1. Since a stranger to a divorce decree in the State of Alabama was permitted by the courts of that State to attack its decrees collaterally for fraud, the courts of this State also permitted such collateral attack. ( Nitschke v. Nitschke, 21 Misc 2d 632, not followed.)

2. Plaintiff wife procured a decree of divorce from her first husband in Alabama at a time when neither she nor her husband was a resident of that State. The courts of Alabama lacked jurisdiction of the material res and assumed jurisdiction by reason of the fraud practiced on it by plaintiff as to her domicile. As to defendant, plaintiff's second husband, the decree was void. Accordingly, plaintiff's complaint in her action [***2] for a separation was dismissed and defendant was granted judgment on his counterclaim annulling the marriage between them.

Idelevitz v. City of Glen Cove


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, SPECIAL TERM, NASSAU COUNTY


230 N.Y.S.2d 591; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4240



May 16, 1962, Decided

 

2390 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


American Rescue Workers, Inc. v. Smith


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Niagara County


34 Misc. 2d 677; 228 N.Y.S.2d 85; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3318



May 14, 1962 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner religious corporation sought an order directing respondent Mayor of the City of North Tonawanda, New York to issue it and its agents a permit to solicit funds in the City of North Tonawanda to promote its religious and social projects, and an order restraining the mayor from prohibiting the dissemination of religious literature in the city and from prohibiting certain agents of the corporation from soliciting funds.


OVERVIEW: The agents of the corporation applied to the city for a solicitor's permit under the City of Tonawanda, New York Ordinance § 4. The mayor notified the city attorney that the application was disapproved. No reason was assigned for disapproval. The ordinance in question was mandatory and a permit had to be issued upon the mayor finding that the applicant was qualified under the ordinance. No other standards were set forth in the ordinance. The issue before the court was whether a municipality in the exercise of its inherent police power could have refused a permit. Municipal corporations were allowed, under their police power, to regulate and license solicitation of the type in question where these activities were noncommercial and involved the spreading of religious faith. Such licensing was strictly limited to that required by essential police regulation of the particular activities. Since § 4 contained no standard and no criteria to govern the issuance of permits for solicitation it was denied without valid legal reason, and denial of the permit exceeded the police power which the city was allowed to exercise in the regulation of religious activities and nonprofit enterprises.


OUTCOME: The court granted the corporation the permit sought and the mayor was directed to issue permits to the certain agents of the corporation to solicit funds.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- permit to solicit funds for religious corporation -- ordinance fails to set standards of solicitors' character and nonprofit; hence, denial of permit exceeds police power; permit shall issue.

The Mayor of a city is directed (Civ. Prac. Act, art. 78) to issue permits to two individuals to solicit funds on behalf of petitioner, a religious corporation. An ordinance of the city requires a permit, but the ordinance sets no standards by which to determine whether the solicitors are of good character and whether the solicitation is religious and nonprofit. Hence, as applied, it exceeds the police power.

 

2391 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Chase v. Glen Cove


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


34 Misc. 2d 810; 227 N.Y.S.2d 131; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3588



April 2, 1962 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowner sought a declaratory judgment invalidating an ordinance that rezoned certain property as a municipal high rise housing district.


OVERVIEW: In five causes of action the landowner attacked the ordinance that rezoned certain property for multi-family, high rise, dwellings, because it was "spot zoning," it was enacted pursuant to agreement with the Division of Housing and the Glen Cove Housing Authority and constituted "zoning by contract," it was confiscatory, the notice of hearing was insufficient, and the area rezoned was insufficiently described in the ordinance. The court held that the spot zoning claim was sustained. The landowner had the burden of showing that the ordinance was invalid was sufficient to entitle the to proceed to trial. The landowner's claims that the ordinance constituted zoning by contract and that it was confiscatory did not stand. An ordinance rezoning land pursuant to a housing plan had to have been enacted in conformance with the procedure established by the appropriate enabling statute. If the notice was ambiguous and misleading, adoption of the ordinance would not conform to N.Y. Gen. City Law § 83 and defendant city's ordinance. The description of the property was insufficient.


OUTCOME: The court permitted the landowner to proceed on his claims against the city in his challenge to a city zoning ordinance.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- zoning -- cause of action attacking ordinance rezoning property for high rise housing as spot zoning was sufficient -- cause of action attacking ordinance because enacted pursuant to agreement with Housing Authority was insufficient -- cause of action attacking ordinance as confiscatory was sufficient -- cause of action attacking notice of hearing with respect to ordinance was sufficient -- cause of action attacking description of property purportedly rezoned was sufficient.

1. In an action wherein plaintiffs sought a judgment declaring that an ordinance rezoning certain property as a "Municipal High Rise Housing District" was invalid, plaintiffs, in the first cause of action, alleged that the ordinance constituted spot zoning. Section 155 of the Public Housing Law provided that "All projects shall be subject to the planning, zoning * * * laws * * * applicable in the municipality in which the project is situated". The planning and zoning laws applicable in defendant city included subdivisions 24 and 25 of section 20 of the General City Law. Subdivision 25 required that zoning regulations be adopted "in accord with a well considered plan". An ordinance [***2] rezoning a particular site was invalid if it failed to conform to such plan. The first cause of action was sufficient.

2. The second cause of action, wherein plaintiffs alleged that the ordinance was enacted pursuant to agreement with the Division of Housing and the Glen Cove Housing Authority and this constituted zoning by contract was insufficient. Express permission was granted by section 99 of the Public Housing Law for a municipality to enter into an agreement with an authority or housing company to rezone any section in aid of public housing projects.

3. A third cause of action wherein plaintiffs alleged that the ordinance was confiscatory was sufficient. A zoning ordinance which deprived a property owner of all use of his land for an indefinite period of time was unconstitutional. Plaintiffs could treat this rezoning as an invasion of their property rights until such time as the city took their property in eminent domain.

4. A fourth cause of action wherein plaintiffs alleged that the notice of hearing with respect to the rezoning ordinance was insufficient was good. If the notice was ambiguous and misleading, adoption of the ordinance did not conform to section 83 of [***3] the General City Law and section 11 of the Glen Cove Ordinance.

5. A fifth cause of action wherein plaintiffs alleged that the description of the property purportedly rezoned was incorrect and incomplete was sufficient.

 

2392 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


New Rochelle Water Co. v. New Rochelle


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


34 Misc. 2d 952; 227 N.Y.S.2d 741; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3629



March 23, 1962 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff water utility company and defendant city both sought summary judgment in the company's action to recover reimbursement costs for relocation of pipes and hydrants in conjunction with road construction pursuant to its contract with the city. The city argued, inter alia, that the company was obligated to pay the costs, that the agreement was unconstitutional and that the city was had not authorized the construction.


OVERVIEW: The city entered into a contract with the company, for the company's provision of water to the city for fire extinguishment and other purposes. A subsequent amendment obligated the city to reimburse the company for the cost of relocation of the company-owned mains, pipes and hydrants where the city changed the line or grade of any street. When the parties disagreed about relocation costs associated with reconstruction of a road, they agreed that the company would accomplish the relocation without waiver of the rights of either party regarding liability. When the city refused to pay the company's claim, it instituted an action for recovery. The court granted summary judgment to the company, holding that: 1) the contract between the company and the city for provision of water, which included reimbursement of relocation costs, was valid; 2) the agreement was not unconstitutional; 3) no express statutory authority to reimburse the company was necessary because state courts had sanctioned such reimbursement when it rested on a contract; and 4) the company's contract was with the city, and it was not obligated to look elsewhere for reimbursement.


OUTCOME: The court granted summary judgment to the company in its action against the city for reimbursement of costs for relocation of pipes and hydrants pursuant to its contract with the city.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Public utilities -- relocation of pipes and hydrants -- although public utility supplying water to city for fire purposes must pay cost of relocation of mains, hydrants and appurtenances in absence of contract, city is liable for such cost if it has undertaken to pay it.

1. Since 1925 plaintiff water company has supplied water to defendant city for fire extinguishment and other fire purposes. Contracts were from time to time executed by the parties by the terms of which defendant agreed to reimburse plaintiff for expenses incident to the relocation of mains and hydrants and which provided that defendant would notify plaintiff if it planned to change the grade of any city street; that plaintiff would thereupon notify defendant of "resettings, replacements and repairs made necessary thereby" and of the estimated cost thereof, and that defendant would be responsible for the excess of this cost over $ 600 if it instructed plaintiff to proceed with the work. Defendant cannot now claim that its agreement to reimburse the company for relocation of mains, hydrants and appurtenances was without consideration and that it had no power to pay for that for which plaintiff was obligated [***2] to pay under its charter as a public utility corporation. Although a public utility must pay for such relocation in the absence of a contract, the city is liable for the cost if it has so agreed.

2. Nothing in section 1 of article VIII of the New York State Constitution or in section 42 of the Transportation Corporations Law prevents an undertaking by a municipality to bear the costs of such relocation.

3. Since plaintiffs dealt only with defendant, it was not obligated to inquire into any arrangements between defendant and other parties. Accordingly, the fact that the work which necessitated relocation was not authorized by defendant but was done by the State and county cannot bar plaintiff's claim.

Bohan v. Southampton


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, SPECIAL TERM, SUFFOLK COUNTY


227 N.Y.S.2d 712; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 4110



March 19, 1962, Dated


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owner filed an action against defendants, a town and officials, to have an amendment to a zoning ordinance declared invalid. Both parties filed motions for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The town adopted an ordinance that placed the owner's property in a residential and agricultural zone. The ordinance provided that any lawful use in existence at the time, such as the owner's business, could be continued, expanded, or changed to another use. The court disagreed with the town's claim that the owner did not have a right to expand upon his business use as planned. The ordinance as originally enacted provided that right. However, the town amended the ordinance to prohibit such expansion. The owner argued that the amended ordinance was invalid because it did not provide for the notice required by N.Y. Town Law § 264 to adjoining municipalities that had affected property owners within 500 feet of the town borders. The court agreed. The owner had a property right, which was directly and adversely affected by the amendment, and he had a right to challenge defects in the ordinance. In light of the lack of notice, the court granted summary judgment to the owner.


OUTCOME: The court granted the owner's summary judgment motion to the extent that the amendment was declared invalid. The court ruled that it could not declare that the owner was entitled to the use he sought. The town's motion for summary judgment was denied.

Seneca Falls v. State


Claim No. 39635


Court of Claims of New York


33 Misc. 2d 1023; 227 N.Y.S.2d 109; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3695



March 12, 1962 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] I hold the act constitutional and award judgment to claimant in the sum of $ 115,517.84.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: At the end of the trial, the State petitioned the court for dismissal on the grounds that the enabling act, 1961 N.Y. Laws 969, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The village, under emergency conditions, reconstructed a bridge to replace a bridge originally constructed by the state. While the State Department of Public Works approved the plans and specifications, there was no precise statute authorizing the state to pay any part of the cost. The legislature passed an enabling act to pay a portion of the cost of the bridge. The entire length of the bridge was 399.2 feet and 121 feet thereof was over state canal lands. The total cost of the bridge was $ 381,246.89. The state's share was $ 115,517.84. At the close of trial, the State sought dismissal on the grounds that the enabling act was unconstitutional. The court found the act constitutional, holding that it was within the competence of the legislature to validate retrospectively any action it might prospectively authorize. There was an emergency situation and under such circumstances no constitutional bar was violated.


OUTCOME: The court held that act constitutional and award judgment to the village for $ 115,517.84.


HEADNOTES

State -- claims against -- enabling act -- act validating retrospectively action State could have, by statute, taken prospectively was constitutional.

The Legislature had power to authorize the State to agree to pay a part of the cost of reconstructing a bridge in part over State canal lands, and it also had power to validate retrospectively such action. Accordingly, where claimant, under emergency conditions, reconstructed a bridge to replace a bridge originally constructed by the State, a statute conferring jurisdiction upon the Court of Claims to determine that portion of the costs of the bridge which the length thereof over State canal lands bore to the entire length of the bridge and authorizing claimant to make a claim therefor was constitutional.

Hempstead v. Romano


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special and Trial Term, Nassau County


33 Misc. 2d 315; 226 N.Y.S.2d 291; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3735



March 6, 1962 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Accordingly, the complaint is dismissed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff township filed an action in the court, seeking through the injunctive process to enforce an amortization provision in its building zone ordinance against the nonconforming use by defendant property owners of their land as a junk yard and automobile wrecking business.


OVERVIEW: At issue by way of defense was the constitutionality of the township's zoning ordinance, which had prohibited the use of any land in the town for a junk yard or automobile wrecking business. By his will, a decedent had devised his real property to defendants, his three sons, and had bequeathed his interest in the junkyard business to two of his sons, with the direction and request that defendants conduct the junkyard and automobile wrecking business jointly, as they had done since his death. The property was later zoned as residential and no effort had previously been made to terminate the nonconforming use, which had begun when the land was unzoned. The court determined that defendants had well-recognized legal property rights, which had to be protected by the court. The recital of the facts demonstrated the substantial loss that would have attached had the ordinance been enforced against defendants. As a matter of fact, application of the ordinance would have been tantamount to terminating the business of defendants without compensation.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the township's complaint.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- automobile junk yard -- complaint was dismissed in action to enforce zoning ordinance requiring termination within three years of automobile junk yards maintained in town as nonconforming use -- defendants had valuable property rights in said business -- business would be terminated, by enforcement, without compensation.

A town's zoning ordinance, since January 20, 1930, prohibited the use of any land for an automobile wrecking business and provided that any automobile junk yard in existence in a residence district be discontinued at the end of three years. Defendants' land had been used as an automobile junk yard since 1926. In this action by the town to enforce such ordinance by injunction, the complaint was dismissed. Defendants had a valuable property right in the land and buildings. They had equipment valued at $ 28,000 and $ 10,000 of inventory. The business could not be relocated because no land in the county was available which could be used for such business, the good will of which was $ 22,000. The cost of relocating the business into one town which permitted such use as a [***2] special exception in an industrial district was at least $ 10,000. To enforce the ordinance would be tantamount to terminating defendants' business without compensation.

Horn Constr. Co. v. Hempstead


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Nassau County


33 Misc. 2d 381; 226 N.Y.S.2d 134; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3820



February 19, 1962 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner village filed a motion to intervene in an action brought against defendant town by plaintiff landowner to obtain a judicial declaration that the town zoning was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The village sought to intervene in an action brought by a landowner against a town over zoning of land that abutted the village. The village claimed that rezoning would result in the erection of numerous dwellings on the landowner's property and that even though the dwellings would be outside the village's limits, the village would be affected by the increased use of its services and facilities. The court denied the motion to intervene. The court held that there was no factual showing that the town would omit any duty imposed upon it by law with respect to the zoning. Furthermore, the court noted that it was not clear that the village would be bound by the judgment in the action. The court held that the fact that the village, as an abutting property owner, would be affected by rezoning did not furnish a basis for intervention.


OUTCOME: The court denied the village's motion to intervene in an action brought against a town by a landowner to obtain a judicial declaration regarding rezoning of land.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- zoning -- in action by owner of tract to declare town's zoning confiscatory and void, application by village which adjoins said tract to intervene as party defendant is denied without prejudice -- village may appear as amicus curiae.

A property owner in the Town of Hempstead in Nassau County sues the town for a judgment declaring its zoning of his property to be confiscatory and void. The Village of Freeport, in the Town of Hempstead, seeks to intervene as a party defendant, on the grounds that the plaintiff's land adjoins the village's boundary line and can be reached only by way of a village street, that a rezoning of plaintiff's land would result in the building on it of about 1,000 one-family dwellings and in a greatly increased use of the village's streets and facilities, that the plaintiff and the town appear to be working out a rezoning settlement whereby only 10,000 square feet of land would be required for each house, and that the village fears that the town will not defend plaintiff's action strenuously. The village's application is denied (Civ. Prac. Act, § 193-b, subds. 1, 2), without prejudice to a renewal thereof on more adequate papers.  [***2] However, the village may appear in the action as amicus curiae and submit legal argument.

 

2398 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Jenad, Inc. v. Scarsdale


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Westchester County


35 Misc. 2d 450; 233 N.Y.S.2d 333; 1962 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3886



February 8, 1962 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The motion is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a village, its planning commission, and others, moved under N.Y. R. Civ. Prac. 107(5) to dismiss the complaint brought by plaintiff builder for the return of funds paid to the village for a park on the ground that the action was not timely instituted in accordance with the provisions of N.Y. Village Law § 341-b.


OVERVIEW: The builder applied to the planning commission for approval of its development plans. Pursuant to its code, the village required the builder to pay the village for a village park, playground and recreational facilities in lieu of such facilities being provided for in the plans of the builder. The builder made payment accordingly. However, N.Y. Town Law § 277, which was virtually identical to the village code, was found to be unconstitutional. Thereafter, the builder demanded the return of the funds, to which the village did not respond. The builder instituted an action to recover the funds, and defendants moved to dismiss the complaint on the ground that the action was time-barred pursuant to N.Y. Village Law § 341-b. The court denied the motion because that section applied only to actions in contract and in tort, where as the action here was for monies had and received, which was quasi-contractual and arose by operation of law, not out of a contract. The limitation contained in § 341-b was intended to apply to express contracts and contracts implied from the facts, but not to a quasi contract.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion to dismiss the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Limitation of actions -- actions in quasi contract -- action in quasi contract against village not governed by short Statute of Limitations contained in Village Law ( § 341-b) but by six year statute.

An action for money had and received by defendant village was an action at law based on quasi contract, which accrued on October 30, 1959 when plaintiff erroneously paid defendant $ 6,000. This action, commenced some 23 months after the payment, was not barred by the Statute of Limitations contained in section 341-b of the Village Law, which was applicable to express contracts and contracts implied by the facts. The six-year Statute of Limitations applied to an action for money had and received.

 

2399 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Streb v. Rochester


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Special Term, Monroe County


32 Misc. 2d 29; 222 N.Y.S.2d 813; 1961 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1815



December 29, 1961 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a commercial property owner and his tenants, filed a motion for an injunction pendente light in plaintiffs' pending action against defendant city officials. Plaintiffs' main action sought a declaratory judgment and a permanent injunction to preclude the officials' enforcement of three successively adopted city ordinances prohibiting the use of a specific type of elevator, one of which was in used in the owner's building..


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs challenged the validity of Section 85-2.1 of Vol. II of the Municipal Code of the City of Rochester, New York, that prohibited the use of rope-geared, hydraulic elevators for passenger transportation. Plaintiffs petitioned for an injunction pendente lite after the officials had expressed their intention to enforce the ordinance. The court granted plaintiffs' motion for the preliminary injunction based on its determination that (1) fairness and the absence of urgency dictated the issuance of the temporary restraint of enforcement until the ultimate rights of the parties could be determined at trial; (2) the ordinance denied equal protection by discriminating without just cause against the use of elevators of the identified type without satisfactory indication that passengers were in danger; (3) because the ordinance proscribed the elevator's use in contravention of plaintiffs' previously vested right to that use, the ordinance was not reasonable, absent a showing that the public need demanded the proscription; and (4) the officials did not substantiate their right to enforce the ordinance, which would result in irreparable financial hardship to plaintiffs, if enforced.


OUTCOME: The court granted plaintiffs' motion for a preliminary injunction.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Municipal corporations -- elevators -- city ordinance prohibiting use of stated type of elevator was unconstitutional as depriving plaintiffs, owner and tenants of building containing such type of elevator, of their property without due process of law.

Section 85-2.1 of volume II of the Municipal Code of the City of Rochester which prohibited the use of rope-geared hydraulic elevators for passenger service, deprived plaintiffs of due process of law and equal protection of the laws and was unconstitutional. The ordinance and its predecessors, which were not enforced for 11 years, were not related to the public safety, were unreasonably oppressive in that compliance with the ordinance would impose great costs and losses on plaintiff owner of a building, and plaintiff tenants therein of 40 and 25 years' duration, who had acquired vested and valuable rights in the elevator which elevator was essential to their businesses. The ordinances could not be taken together to provide a reasonable period of 11 years within which to terminate the prior nonconforming use of plaintiffs' elevator.

 

2400 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Good Will Club of Amsterdam, New York, Inc. v. Amsterdam


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Equity Term, Montgomery County


31 Misc. 2d 1096; 222 N.Y.S.2d 896; 1961 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 1835



December 28, 1961 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owner filed an action challenging the validity of an assessment placed against its property by defendants, a city corporation counsel, board of education, and county attorney, alleging that it was entitled to an exemption under N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 420(2) and former N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 4(6).


OVERVIEW: The property owner was a club organized for the purposes of moral and mental improvement of women and girls of a city; a portion of its activities were also allegedly devoted to educational and charitable purposes. The owner sought a property tax exemption on these grounds, but the exemption was denied. The owner then filed an action seeking declaratory judgment. The court held that in order for the owner to be entitled to the exemption, it had to show not only that it had been organized exclusively for charitable purposes, but also that its operations carried out such intent. The court held that the owner's certificate of incorporation stating the purpose of its formation was broad, and could have encompassed social and fraternal, as well as charitable, objectives. The court reasoned that proof that the owner had awarded three small scholarships, without additional proof as to how the owner was organized solely for benevolent purposes was insufficient to sustain its claim to exemption. In contrast, the court determined that the owner's primary activity was renting its property for profit, which defeated its claim to the exemption sought.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the owner's complaint, without costs.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Taxation -- exemptions -- club for women and girls was denied exemption from taxation for its real property where neither its certificate of incorporation, its by-laws nor its use of property showed that it was organized exclusively for charitable, benevolent or educational purposes.

A club organized for the moral and mental improvement of women and girls whose purposes, as stated in its certificate of incorporation, were to provide "for the happiness and development of its members and to create a center where enjoyment, friendship and opportunities of improvement can be found", and, as stated in its by-laws, to advance the well-being of its members and to provide a center where opportunities for improvement might be found, was not entitled to exemption from taxation for its clubhouse, in which were contained bedrooms rented out to tenants and other rooms used for meetings which were also rented to other organizations. Plaintiff was not organized exclusively for charitable, benevolent or educational purposes and its actual use of the property was likewise not exclusively for charitable, benevolent or educational purposes. Other than showing that three scholarships of $ 100 [***2] each were awarded in 1959, no other proof that plaintiff was organized exclusively for the moral and mental improvement of women and girls or for charitable, benevolent or educational purposes was offered.
